Reduction Methods: Conversion and Reductio
Author explains reduction techniques for modal syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply conversion (عكس) and reductio (خلف) to reduce any modal syllogism to the first figure, and explain the logical structure of the reductio argument.

Core Idea
Conversion and reductio are tools to transform any modal syllogism into a valid first-figure form.

The author describes two key reduction methods: converting the minor premise (عكس) and using reductio ad absurdum (خلف). These allow any figure to be reduced to the first figure, yielding the sought conclusion. Let's explore how they work step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of reduction to first figure
The author states that by converting the minor premise, a syllogism returns to the third figure from the first, producing the sought conclusion. This shows the role of conversion (عكس) in shifting figures.
The phrase 'يرتد إلى الاقتران الثالث من الأول' means it goes from the first figure to the third? Actually careful: 'من الأول' might mean 'from the first'? The translation says 'returns to the third figure from the first'. That implies the original was in first. But the point is conversion enables reduction.
2
Follow the reductio method
Take the contradictory of the conclusion (نقيض النتيجة) and make it a major premise for the original minor premise. This yields the contradictory of the original major premise, which is a contradiction, thus proving the conclusion.
The author says 'مهما أخذت نقيض النتيجة وجعلته كبرى للصغرى في الكل أنتج المقدمة الكبرى، وهو خلف'.
3
Note additional tools: inversion and supposition
The author mentions 'العكس' (inversion) and 'الافتراض' (supposition) as clarificatory tools. These are used for further verification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقت غیر معين وهو خلف، ولأنه بعكس الصغرى يرتد إلى الاقتران الثالث من الأول ناتجاً عين المطلوب، وعلى هذا فاعتبره في ما بقي من الاقترانات بالعكس والخلف، وسواء كانت الضرورية صغرى أو كبرى. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من الضرورية والممكنة فإن كانت الممكنة فلا خلاف في اتباع النتيجة لها كانت ممكنة بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص، إلا أن تكون الممكنة بالمعنى الخاص كليّة سالبة كما في الاقتران السادس فالخلاف فيه على نحو الخلاف في ما يرتد إلى الاقتران الثاني من الأول، كما ،عرف، ولأنك مهما أخذت نقيض النتيجة وجعلته كبرى للصغرى في الكل أنتج المقدمة الكبرى، وهو خلف وعليك باعتبار العكس والافتراض، إن رمت البيان به في ما يتبين به.
An indefinite time, and it is a reductio argument, and because by converting the minor premise it returns to the third figure from the first, producing the very sought conclusion; so consider this in the remaining figures by conversion and reductio, whether the necessary proposition is minor or major. As for mixing of the necessary and the possible: if the possible is [the one present], there is no disagreement that the conclusion follows it, whether the possible is in the general sense, the specific sense, or the most specific sense, unless the possible in the specific sense is a universal negative as in the sixth figure, then there is disagreement in the manner of the disagreement regarding what returns to the second figure from the first, as is known; and because whenever you take the contradictory of the conclusion and make it a major premise for the minor premise in all cases, it produces the major premise, which is a reductio; and you must consider inversion and supposition if you desire clarification by what is clarified.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage discusses the rules for mixed modal syllogisms, specifically when combining necessary and possible propositions. The author first mentions a reduction method using contraposition (عكس) and reductio ad absurdum (خلف) to reduce any figure to the first figure, applicable whether the necessary proposition is minor or major. Then he addresses the case of mixing necessary and possible. The general rule is that the conclusion follows the modality of the possible proposition, regardless of whether the possible is in the general sense (المعنى العام), specific sense (المعنى الخاص), or most specific sense (المعنى الأخص). An exception occurs in the sixth figure when the possible proposition is a universal negative in the specific sense; then there is disagreement analogous to a known case in the second figure. The author also explains the reductio method: taking the contradictory of the conclusion as a premise to derive the original major premise, and mentions using inversion (عكس) and supposition (افتراض) for clarification. This is a technical discussion of modal logic in the Islamic tradition.

D) Using supposition to change the figure.

Key Takeaway
Reduction to the first figure via conversion and reductio is essential for validating modal syllogisms across all figures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a modal syllogism in the second figure: 'All A are necessarily B; No C are possibly B; therefore No C are A'. Use conversion of the minor premise (if applicable) to reduce to the first figure. Show the steps and verify validity.
?

Mixing Conditioned Necessary and Possible
Rule for mixing conditioned necessary and possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the first-figure rules to any syllogism mixing conditioned necessary and possible propositions, using reductio, inversion, or supposition as needed.

Core Idea
All valid mixes of conditioned necessary and possible follow the first figure regardless of order.

In this lesson, we explore the foundational rule for mixing the two most common modal types: the conditioned necessary (e.g., 'Every animal is necessarily sentient') and the possible (e.g., 'Every human is possibly rational'). The author states that the ruling is as known in the first figure, meaning the standard syllogistic conditions apply. We will learn how to determine the conclusion's modality by using reductio (khalf), inversion (aks), or supposition (iftirad).

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
Determine which premise is conditioned necessary and which is possible. Note their quality (affirmative/negative) and which is major/minor.
The order does not affect the rule.
2
Check the figure
Ensure the syllogism is in the first figure (middle term is subject of major and predicate of minor). If not, convert or rearrange as per earlier rules.
3
Apply the standard first-figure rules
Use the methods of khalf, aks, and iftirad to verify validity. The conclusion's subject comes from the minor, predicate from the major.
The author refers to 'as known' from prior lessons.
4
Determine the conclusion's modality
Based on the methods, derive the correct modal conclusion. The ruling holds regardless of which premise is conditioned necessary or possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاختلاط بين الضرورية المشروطة والممكنة فالحكم فيها على ما عرف في الشكل الأول، وبأي اعتبار كانت المشروطة والممكنة، وأيهما كانت كبرى أو صغرى والبيان من الخلف والعكس والافتراض فكما علم. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من المشروطات بعضها مع بعض فإما أن يكون الاختلاط من جنس اتصاف الموضوع بما وضع معه، ومن المشروطة بشرط المحمول والمشروط بشرط وقت، أو من أقسام المشروطة من جنس اتصاف الموضوع بما وضع معه بعضها مع بعض، فإن كان الأول أو الثاني أو الثالث فالإنتاج واقع والحكم في اتباع النتيجة وفي البيان له بطريقة فكما سلف. وإن كان الرابع وهو اختلاط بين أقسام المشروطة بشرط الموضوع فلا إنتاج إن كان الاختلاط من أحد القسمين الأولين، وأحد القسمين الآخرين ولا تبالي أي قسم كان منها، إذ الحكم في القسمين الأولين على ما الشرط فيه دائم بدوام الموضوع، وعند ذلك فالحد الأوسط بين الطرفين يكون مختلفاً، بل إن كان الاختلاط من القسم الأول والثاني أو الثالث والرابع فالإنتاج واقع والحكم في النتيجة والبيان فكما سبق في ما إذا كانت الصغرى ضرورية. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من الممكنات بعضها مع بعض فالحكم أيضاً في الإنتاج والبيان كما تقدم. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من المطلقات والموجهات فالمطلقة إما أن تكون بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص، فإن كانت بالمعنى العام فاختلاطها إمـا بالضرورية أو الممكنة، فإن كان الأول فالمشهور أن النتيجة في الاقتران الأول والثالث تتبع الصغرى بعكس الكبرى وجعلها صغرى، وقد عرف أن البيان بهذا العكس غير حافظ للجهة، إلا في بعض المواد، ولا اعتبار به والحق أنها تتبع الكبرى لما عرف من الخلف وعكس الصغرى. وأما حكم اختلاطها بالضروريات المشروطة فحكم اختلاط الممكنات بالمشروطة، وقد عرف. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من المطلقة والممكنة فإما أن تكون الممكنة بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص، فإن كانت بالمعنى الخاص فالمشهور أيضاً أن النتيجة تكون تابعة للممكنة، والحق إنما هو اتباعها للكبرى للبيانات السابقة في اختلاط الممكنة والضرورية، وكذا إن كانت الممكنة بالمعنى العام أو الأخصي. وأما إن كانت المطلقة بالمعنى الخاصي فحكمها في اختلاطها بالموجهات هذا حكم الاختلاط العامة بها، وكذلك حكم الوقتية، وعليك إن كانت الصغرى من الشكل ممكنة سالبة بالإمكان الخاص أن تقلبها إلى الإيجاب كما سلف في الشكل الأول ليتم الإنتاج، والبيان بعكس الصغرى.
As for mixing between the conditioned necessary and the possible, the ruling therein is what is known in the first figure, and in whichever manner the conditioned and the possible are, and whichever is major or minor, the clarification by reductio and inversion and supposition is as known. As for mixing of conditioned propositions with each other: either the mixing is of the type of the subject being described by what is placed with it, and from the conditioned by condition of the predicate and the conditioned by condition of time, or from the divisions of the conditioned of the type of the subject being described by what is placed with it, some with each other. If it is the first, second, or third, then production occurs, and the ruling in following the conclusion and in the clarification for it by method is as preceding. If it is the fourth, which is mixing between divisions of the conditioned by condition of the subject, then no production if the mixing is from one of the first two divisions and one of the other two divisions; and do not care which division it is, for the ruling in the first two divisions is that the condition is permanent with the permanence of the subject, and accordingly the middle term between the two extremes becomes different. Rather, if the mixing is of the first and second or third and fourth, then production occurs and the ruling in the conclusion and the clarification is as preceded in the case where the minor is necessary. As for mixing of possible propositions with each other, the ruling also in production and clarification is as earlier. As for mixing of absolute and modal propositions: the absolute is either in the general sense, specific sense, or most specific sense. If it is in the general sense, its mixing is either with necessary or with possible. If it is the first, the well-known view is that the conclusion in the first and third figures follows the minor by converting the major and making it minor; and it is known that clarification by this conversion does not preserve the modality except in some materials, and it is not relied upon; the truth is that it follows the major, based on what is known of reductio and conversion of the minor. As for the ruling of its mixing with conditioned necessities, it is the ruling of mixing of possibles with conditioned, and it is known. As for mixing of absolute and possible: the possible is either in the general sense, specific sense, or most specific sense. If it is in the specific sense, the well-known view also is that the conclusion is dependent on the possible, but the truth is that it follows the major based on the previous clarifications in mixing possible and necessary; likewise if the possible is in the general or most specific sense. As for the absolute in the specific sense, its ruling in mixing with modals is the ruling of general mixing with them, and likewise the ruling of temporal propositions. And you must, if the minor in the figure is a negative possible by specific possibility, convert it to affirmative as preceded in the first figure so that production is completed, and the clarification is by converting the minor.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of rules for mixing various modal propositions in categorical syllogisms. It covers several cases: First, mixing conditioned necessary with possible: the rules follow the first figure pattern regardless of which is major or minor, using reductio, inversion, and supposition. Second, mixing conditioned propositions with each other: there are four types; the first three types yield a conclusion, while the fourth type (mixing two divisions of conditioned-by-subject) does not yield a conclusion unless the mixing is between specific pairs (first+second or third+fourth). The reasoning is that in the first two divisions the condition is permanent as long as the subject exists, causing the middle term to differ. Third, mixing possible propositions with each other: same rules as before. Fourth, mixing absolute with modal: here the absolute can be general, specific, or most specific. For absolute in the general sense mixed with necessary, the well-known view that the conclusion follows the minor is rejected; the truth is it follows the major, using reductio and conversion of the minor. For absolute mixed with conditioned necessary, the rule is like mixing possible with conditioned. For absolute mixed with possible, if the possible is in the specific sense, the well-known view that the conclusion follows the possible is also rejected; the truth is it follows the major, and similarly for other degrees of possible. For absolute in the specific sense mixed with modals, the rule is as general mixing. Finally, the author notes a technical point: if the minor premise in the figure is a negative possible in the specific sense, it must be converted to affirmative (by obversion?) to complete the production, using conversion of the minor. This passage is rich with modal logic distinctions and shows careful attention to the conditions under which conclusions follow.

الممكنة
الشكل الأول
الخلف
العكس
الافتراض

Key Takeaway
When mixing conditioned necessary and possible, the well-established first-figure rules apply universally, regardless of the order or specific type of conditioned.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a first-figure syllogism with a conditioned necessary major (e.g., 'Every body is necessarily composite') and a possible minor (e.g., 'Every element is possibly body'). Determine the conclusion and its modality using the first-figure rule.
?

Mixing Conditioned Propositions Among Themselves
Rules for mixing different types of conditioned propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which types of conditioned proposition mixes yield a valid conclusion and which do not, based on the condition's relation to the subject.

Core Idea
Only certain mixes of conditioned-by-subject with others are productive; otherwise, the middle term becomes mismatched.

Conditioned propositions come in different kinds depending on what the condition restricts: the predicate, the time, or the subject itself. When mixing these with each other, not all combinations yield a valid syllogism. The author distinguishes four cases and provides the conditions under which production (intaj) occurs. We will learn to classify a given mix and apply the corresponding rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of conditioned premises
Determine whether each premise is conditioned by predicate (بشرط المحمول), by time (بشرط وقت), or by subject (بشرط الموضوع). Also note the 'subject being described by what is placed with it' type.
The first three types in the passage are productive; the fourth (conditioned-by-subject mixes) needs careful handling.
2
Classify the mix into one of four categories
First: mixing of the 'subject described' type with conditioned-by-predicate or conditioned-by-time. Second: mixing among the 'subject described' types. Third: mixing among the conditioned-by-predicate/time types. Fourth: mixing two divisions of conditioned-by-subject.
The passage explicitly says first, second, third are productive.
3
Apply the rule for the fourth case
If the mix is of two conditioned-by-subject divisions, then production fails unless you mix the first division with the second, or the third with the fourth. Any other combination fails because the condition is permanent and the middle term becomes different.
The author says 'لا إنتاج إن كان الاختلاط من أحد القسمين الأولين، وأحد القسمين الآخرين'.
4
Verify the conclusion's modality
For productive mixes, use the standard methods (reductio, inversion, supposition) as in the case with necessary minor.

Source Text

وأما إن كان الاختلاط بين الضرورية المشروطة والممكنة فالحكم فيها على ما عرف في الشكل الأول، وبأي اعتبار كانت المشروطة والممكنة، وأيهما كانت كبرى أو صغرى والبيان من الخلف والعكس والافتراض فكما علم. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من المشروطات بعضها مع بعض فإما أن يكون الاختلاط من جنس اتصاف الموضوع بما وضع معه، ومن المشروطة بشرط المحمول والمشروط بشرط وقت، أو من أقسام المشروطة من جنس اتصاف الموضوع بما وضع معه بعضها مع بعض، فإن كان الأول أو الثاني أو الثالث فالإنتاج واقع والحكم في اتباع النتيجة وفي البيان له بطريقة فكما سلف. وإن كان الرابع وهو اختلاط بين أقسام المشروطة بشرط الموضوع فلا إنتاج إن كان الاختلاط من أحد القسمين الأولين، وأحد القسمين الآخرين ولا تبالي أي قسم كان منها، إذ الحكم في القسمين الأولين على ما الشرط فيه دائم بدوام الموضوع، وعند ذلك فالحد الأوسط بين الطرفين يكون مختلفاً، بل إن كان الاختلاط من القسم الأول والثاني أو الثالث والرابع فالإنتاج واقع والحكم في النتيجة والبيان فكما سبق في ما إذا كانت الصغرى ضرورية. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من الممكنات بعضها مع بعض فالحكم أيضاً في الإنتاج والبيان كما تقدم. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من المطلقات والموجهات فالمطلقة إما أن تكون بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص، فإن كانت بالمعنى العام فاختلاطها إمـا بالضرورية أو الممكنة، فإن كان الأول فالمشهور أن النتيجة في الاقتران الأول والثالث تتبع الصغرى بعكس الكبرى وجعلها صغرى، وقد عرف أن البيان بهذا العكس غير حافظ للجهة، إلا في بعض المواد، ولا اعتبار به والحق أنها تتبع الكبرى لما عرف من الخلف وعكس الصغرى. وأما حكم اختلاطها بالضروريات المشروطة فحكم اختلاط الممكنات بالمشروطة، وقد عرف. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من المطلقة والممكنة فإما أن تكون الممكنة بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص، فإن كانت بالمعنى الخاص فالمشهور أيضاً أن النتيجة تكون تابعة للممكنة، والحق إنما هو اتباعها للكبرى للبيانات السابقة في اختلاط الممكنة والضرورية، وكذا إن كانت الممكنة بالمعنى العام أو الأخصي. وأما إن كانت المطلقة بالمعنى الخاصي فحكمها في اختلاطها بالموجهات هذا حكم الاختلاط العامة بها، وكذلك حكم الوقتية، وعليك إن كانت الصغرى من الشكل ممكنة سالبة بالإمكان الخاص أن تقلبها إلى الإيجاب كما سلف في الشكل الأول ليتم الإنتاج، والبيان بعكس الصغرى.
As for mixing between the conditioned necessary and the possible, the ruling therein is what is known in the first figure, and in whichever manner the conditioned and the possible are, and whichever is major or minor, the clarification by reductio and inversion and supposition is as known. As for mixing of conditioned propositions with each other: either the mixing is of the type of the subject being described by what is placed with it, and from the conditioned by condition of the predicate and the conditioned by condition of time, or from the divisions of the conditioned of the type of the subject being described by what is placed with it, some with each other. If it is the first, second, or third, then production occurs, and the ruling in following the conclusion and in the clarification for it by method is as preceding. If it is the fourth, which is mixing between divisions of the conditioned by condition of the subject, then no production if the mixing is from one of the first two divisions and one of the other two divisions; and do not care which division it is, for the ruling in the first two divisions is that the condition is permanent with the permanence of the subject, and accordingly the middle term between the two extremes becomes different. Rather, if the mixing is of the first and second or third and fourth, then production occurs and the ruling in the conclusion and the clarification is as preceded in the case where the minor is necessary. As for mixing of possible propositions with each other, the ruling also in production and clarification is as earlier. As for mixing of absolute and modal propositions: the absolute is either in the general sense, specific sense, or most specific sense. If it is in the general sense, its mixing is either with necessary or with possible. If it is the first, the well-known view is that the conclusion in the first and third figures follows the minor by converting the major and making it minor; and it is known that clarification by this conversion does not preserve the modality except in some materials, and it is not relied upon; the truth is that it follows the major, based on what is known of reductio and conversion of the minor. As for the ruling of its mixing with conditioned necessities, it is the ruling of mixing of possibles with conditioned, and it is known. As for mixing of absolute and possible: the possible is either in the general sense, specific sense, or most specific sense. If it is in the specific sense, the well-known view also is that the conclusion is dependent on the possible, but the truth is that it follows the major based on the previous clarifications in mixing possible and necessary; likewise if the possible is in the general or most specific sense. As for the absolute in the specific sense, its ruling in mixing with modals is the ruling of general mixing with them, and likewise the ruling of temporal propositions. And you must, if the minor in the figure is a negative possible by specific possibility, convert it to affirmative as preceded in the first figure so that production is completed, and the clarification is by converting the minor.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of rules for mixing various modal propositions in categorical syllogisms. It covers several cases: First, mixing conditioned necessary with possible: the rules follow the first figure pattern regardless of which is major or minor, using reductio, inversion, and supposition. Second, mixing conditioned propositions with each other: there are four types; the first three types yield a conclusion, while the fourth type (mixing two divisions of conditioned-by-subject) does not yield a conclusion unless the mixing is between specific pairs (first+second or third+fourth). The reasoning is that in the first two divisions the condition is permanent as long as the subject exists, causing the middle term to differ. Third, mixing possible propositions with each other: same rules as before. Fourth, mixing absolute with modal: here the absolute can be general, specific, or most specific. For absolute in the general sense mixed with necessary, the well-known view that the conclusion follows the minor is rejected; the truth is it follows the major, using reductio and conversion of the minor. For absolute mixed with conditioned necessary, the rule is like mixing possible with conditioned. For absolute mixed with possible, if the possible is in the specific sense, the well-known view that the conclusion follows the possible is also rejected; the truth is it follows the major, and similarly for other degrees of possible. For absolute in the specific sense mixed with modals, the rule is as general mixing. Finally, the author notes a technical point: if the minor premise in the figure is a negative possible in the specific sense, it must be converted to affirmative (by obversion?) to complete the production, using conversion of the minor. This passage is rich with modal logic distinctions and shows careful attention to the conditions under which conclusions follow.

D) When mixing possible with necessary.

Key Takeaway
When mixing conditioned propositions, not all cases are productive; careful attention to the type of condition determines validity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Suppose we have two conditioned-by-subject propositions: first division 'Every human is rational as long as human' and second division 'Every writer is rational as long as writing'. Does this mix produce a conclusion? Explain.

Mixing Absolute (General Sense) with Modals
Correct rule for mixing general absolute with modals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine that the conclusion follows the major premise when mixing absolute (general sense) with necessary or possible, contrary to the well-known view.

Core Idea
When absolute (general sense) is mixed with modal, the conclusion's modality follows the major, not the minor.

The absolute proposition in the 'general sense' is a fundamental modal type. When mixed with necessary or possible propositions, there is a well-known but incorrect view that the conclusion follows the minor by converting the major. The author refutes this, arguing that the truth is that the conclusion follows the major. We will learn the correct method using reductio and conversion of the minor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sense of the absolute proposition
Check if the absolute premise is in the general sense (المعنى العام). The passage discusses that case first.
2
Identify the modal premise
Determine if the other premise is necessary or possible. The rule differs for each.
3
Apply the correct rule for necessary
If mixing general absolute with necessary, the well-known view is that the conclusion follows the minor by converting the major. However, the author says the truth is that it follows the major. Use reductio (khalf) and conversion of the minor (aks al-sughra) to verify.
Conversion of the major does not preserve modality except in some cases.
4
Apply the rule for conditioned necessary
For general absolute mixed with conditioned necessary, treat it like mixing possible with conditioned (as known from previous rules).
This is a special case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كان الاختلاط بين الضرورية المشروطة والممكنة فالحكم فيها على ما عرف في الشكل الأول، وبأي اعتبار كانت المشروطة والممكنة، وأيهما كانت كبرى أو صغرى والبيان من الخلف والعكس والافتراض فكما علم. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من المشروطات بعضها مع بعض فإما أن يكون الاختلاط من جنس اتصاف الموضوع بما وضع معه، ومن المشروطة بشرط المحمول والمشروط بشرط وقت، أو من أقسام المشروطة من جنس اتصاف الموضوع بما وضع معه بعضها مع بعض، فإن كان الأول أو الثاني أو الثالث فالإنتاج واقع والحكم في اتباع النتيجة وفي البيان له بطريقة فكما سلف. وإن كان الرابع وهو اختلاط بين أقسام المشروطة بشرط الموضوع فلا إنتاج إن كان الاختلاط من أحد القسمين الأولين، وأحد القسمين الآخرين ولا تبالي أي قسم كان منها، إذ الحكم في القسمين الأولين على ما الشرط فيه دائم بدوام الموضوع، وعند ذلك فالحد الأوسط بين الطرفين يكون مختلفاً، بل إن كان الاختلاط من القسم الأول والثاني أو الثالث والرابع فالإنتاج واقع والحكم في النتيجة والبيان فكما سبق في ما إذا كانت الصغرى ضرورية. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من الممكنات بعضها مع بعض فالحكم أيضاً في الإنتاج والبيان كما تقدم. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من المطلقات والموجهات فالمطلقة إما أن تكون بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص، فإن كانت بالمعنى العام فاختلاطها إمـا بالضرورية أو الممكنة، فإن كان الأول فالمشهور أن النتيجة في الاقتران الأول والثالث تتبع الصغرى بعكس الكبرى وجعلها صغرى، وقد عرف أن البيان بهذا العكس غير حافظ للجهة، إلا في بعض المواد، ولا اعتبار به والحق أنها تتبع الكبرى لما عرف من الخلف وعكس الصغرى. وأما حكم اختلاطها بالضروريات المشروطة فحكم اختلاط الممكنات بالمشروطة، وقد عرف. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من المطلقة والممكنة فإما أن تكون الممكنة بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص، فإن كانت بالمعنى الخاص فالمشهور أيضاً أن النتيجة تكون تابعة للممكنة، والحق إنما هو اتباعها للكبرى للبيانات السابقة في اختلاط الممكنة والضرورية، وكذا إن كانت الممكنة بالمعنى العام أو الأخصي. وأما إن كانت المطلقة بالمعنى الخاصي فحكمها في اختلاطها بالموجهات هذا حكم الاختلاط العامة بها، وكذلك حكم الوقتية، وعليك إن كانت الصغرى من الشكل ممكنة سالبة بالإمكان الخاص أن تقلبها إلى الإيجاب كما سلف في الشكل الأول ليتم الإنتاج، والبيان بعكس الصغرى.
As for mixing between the conditioned necessary and the possible, the ruling therein is what is known in the first figure, and in whichever manner the conditioned and the possible are, and whichever is major or minor, the clarification by reductio and inversion and supposition is as known. As for mixing of conditioned propositions with each other: either the mixing is of the type of the subject being described by what is placed with it, and from the conditioned by condition of the predicate and the conditioned by condition of time, or from the divisions of the conditioned of the type of the subject being described by what is placed with it, some with each other. If it is the first, second, or third, then production occurs, and the ruling in following the conclusion and in the clarification for it by method is as preceding. If it is the fourth, which is mixing between divisions of the conditioned by condition of the subject, then no production if the mixing is from one of the first two divisions and one of the other two divisions; and do not care which division it is, for the ruling in the first two divisions is that the condition is permanent with the permanence of the subject, and accordingly the middle term between the two extremes becomes different. Rather, if the mixing is of the first and second or third and fourth, then production occurs and the ruling in the conclusion and the clarification is as preceded in the case where the minor is necessary. As for mixing of possible propositions with each other, the ruling also in production and clarification is as earlier. As for mixing of absolute and modal propositions: the absolute is either in the general sense, specific sense, or most specific sense. If it is in the general sense, its mixing is either with necessary or with possible. If it is the first, the well-known view is that the conclusion in the first and third figures follows the minor by converting the major and making it minor; and it is known that clarification by this conversion does not preserve the modality except in some materials, and it is not relied upon; the truth is that it follows the major, based on what is known of reductio and conversion of the minor. As for the ruling of its mixing with conditioned necessities, it is the ruling of mixing of possibles with conditioned, and it is known. As for mixing of absolute and possible: the possible is either in the general sense, specific sense, or most specific sense. If it is in the specific sense, the well-known view also is that the conclusion is dependent on the possible, but the truth is that it follows the major based on the previous clarifications in mixing possible and necessary; likewise if the possible is in the general or most specific sense. As for the absolute in the specific sense, its ruling in mixing with modals is the ruling of general mixing with them, and likewise the ruling of temporal propositions. And you must, if the minor in the figure is a negative possible by specific possibility, convert it to affirmative as preceded in the first figure so that production is completed, and the clarification is by converting the minor.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of rules for mixing various modal propositions in categorical syllogisms. It covers several cases: First, mixing conditioned necessary with possible: the rules follow the first figure pattern regardless of which is major or minor, using reductio, inversion, and supposition. Second, mixing conditioned propositions with each other: there are four types; the first three types yield a conclusion, while the fourth type (mixing two divisions of conditioned-by-subject) does not yield a conclusion unless the mixing is between specific pairs (first+second or third+fourth). The reasoning is that in the first two divisions the condition is permanent as long as the subject exists, causing the middle term to differ. Third, mixing possible propositions with each other: same rules as before. Fourth, mixing absolute with modal: here the absolute can be general, specific, or most specific. For absolute in the general sense mixed with necessary, the well-known view that the conclusion follows the minor is rejected; the truth is it follows the major, using reductio and conversion of the minor. For absolute mixed with conditioned necessary, the rule is like mixing possible with conditioned. For absolute mixed with possible, if the possible is in the specific sense, the well-known view that the conclusion follows the possible is also rejected; the truth is it follows the major, and similarly for other degrees of possible. For absolute in the specific sense mixed with modals, the rule is as general mixing. Finally, the author notes a technical point: if the minor premise in the figure is a negative possible in the specific sense, it must be converted to affirmative (by obversion?) to complete the production, using conversion of the minor. This passage is rich with modal logic distinctions and shows careful attention to the conditions under which conclusions follow.

الضرورية
الممكنة
الخلف
عكس الصغرى

Mixing Absolute (Specific Sense) and Converting Negative Possible Minor
Conversion of negative possible minor when mixing absolute (specific) with modals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to handle mixing absolute propositions in the specific sense with modals, and convert a negative possible minor to affirmative to enable production.

Core Idea
A negative possible minor must be converted to affirmative to complete the syllogism when mixing absolute (specific) with modals.

The absolute proposition can also be in the 'specific sense' (المعنى الخاص). Its mixing with modals follows the same general rules as the general sense. However, a special nuance arises: if the minor premise in the figure is a negative possible by specific possibility, it must be converted to affirmative using the method from earlier lessons. This conversion is essential for the syllogism to produce a conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sense of the absolute
Check if the absolute is in the specific sense (المعنى الخاص). The ruling is the same as for general mixing.
2
Check the modality of the other premise
Determine if the other premise is modal (necessary, possible, etc.) and note its quality.
3
Examine the minor premise
If the minor premise is a negative possible in the specific sense (سالبة بالإمكان الخاص), it needs conversion.
The author says 'عليك إن كانت الصغرى من الشكل ممكنة سالبة بالإمكان الخاص أن تقلبها إلى الإيجاب'.
4
Convert the minor to affirmative
Use the method of conversion (عكس الصغرى) as taught earlier for the first figure. This will change the negative possible to an affirmative possible (or necessary?) without changing the modality? The conversion preserves the possibility.
After conversion, proceed with the standard first-figure rule to derive the conclusion.

Mixing Absolute and Modal Propositions: Which Premise Determines the Conclusion's Modality?
Critique of the famous view on conclusion modality in mixed modal syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct ruling for the conclusion's modality when mixing absolute and modal premises, and distinguish it from the famous but incorrect view.

Core Idea
When mixing absolute and modal premises, the conclusion's modality follows the major, not the minor.

In this passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq, al-Samarqandi addresses a key controversy in modal syllogistics: when an absolute proposition (mutlaqa) is mixed with a modal one (muwajjaha), which premise determines the modality of the conclusion? The famous view (mashhur) says it follows the minor after inverting the major. But al-Samarqandi decisively rejects this, arguing that the conclusion must follow the major. Understanding this correction is essential for constructing valid modal syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing views
Find the famous view (mashhur) and the author's truth (haqq) regarding which premise determines the conclusion's modality when mixing absolute and modal propositions.
2
Understand the famous view's mechanism
The famous view says: invert the major premise (make it minor) and then the conclusion follows the minor. Note that the author says this inversion does not preserve modality (jah) except in some materials.
3
Grasp the author's correction
The author's truth is that the conclusion follows the major premise, as known from reductio (khalaf) and inversion of the minor. This means the major premise's modality governs.
4
Apply to the mixing of absolute (general sense) with necessary
The passage explicitly says: when absolute (general) is mixed with necessary, the conclusion follows the major. This rule applies for first and third figures.
5
Extend the rule to other mixes
The same principle holds for absolute with possible (regardless of specific/general/most specific) and for absolute in the specific sense mixing with modals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دقائق الحقائق: الجزء الأول / المقالة الرابعة في تعريف صور الحجج وأما إن كان الاختلاط من المطلقات والموجهات فالمطلقة إما أن تكون بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص، فإن كانت بالمعنى العام فاختلاطها إما بالضرورية أو الممكنة، فإن كان الأول فالمشهور أن النتيجة في الاقتران الأول والثالث تتبع الصغرى بعكس الكبرى وجعلها صغرى، وقد عرف أن البيان بهذا العكس غير حافظ للجهة، إلا في بعض المواد، ولا اعتبار به والحق أنها تتبع الكبرى لما عرف من الخلف وعكس الصغرى. وأما حكم اختلاطها بالضروريات المشروطة فحكم اختلاط الممكنات بالمشروطة، وقد عرف. وأما إن كان الاختلاط من المطلقة والممكنة فإما أن تكون الممكنة بالمعنى العام أو الخاص أو الأخص، فإن كانت بالمعنى الخاص فالمشهور أيضاً أن النتيجة تكون تابعة للممكنة، والحق إنما هو اتباعها للكبرى للبيانات السابقة في اختلاط الممكنة والضرورية، وكذا إن كانت الممكنة بالمعنى العام أو الأخصي. وأما إن كانت المطلقة بالمعنى الخاصي فحكمها في اختلاطها بالموجهات هذا حكم الاختلاط العامة بها، وكذلك حكم الوقتية، وعليك إن كانت الصغرى من الشكل ممكنة سالبة بالإمكان الخاص أن تقلبها إلى الإيجاب كما سلف في الشكل الأول ليتم الإنتاج، والبيان بعكس الصغرى.
Daqaiq al-Haqaiq: Part One / Article Four on Defining the Forms of Arguments. As for the mixing of absolute and modal propositions: the absolute is either in the general sense, the specific sense, or the most specific. If it is in the general sense, then its mixing is either with necessary or possible. If the former, the famous view is that the conclusion in the first and third conjunction follows the minor premise by inverting the major and making it minor. It is known that the demonstration by this inversion does not preserve the modality except in some matters, and it is not considered. The truth is that it follows the major premise, as known from the reductio and the inversion of the minor. As for the ruling of mixing it with conditional necessities, it is the ruling of mixing possibles with conditionals, and it is known. As for the mixing of absolute and possible: the possible is either in the general sense, specific, or most specific. If it is in the specific sense, the famous view also is that the conclusion follows the possible, but the truth is that it follows the major, due to the previous explanations in mixing possible and necessary. Likewise if the possible is in the general or most specific sense. As for if the absolute is in the specific sense, its ruling in mixing with modals is this ruling of the general mixing with them, and likewise the ruling of the temporal. And it is upon you, if the minor premise in the first figure is possible negative with specific possibility, to convert it to affirmative as earlier in the first figure for production, and the demonstration is by inverting the minor.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage critically examines the ruling on the conclusion's modality when mixing absolute (mutlaqa) and modal (muwajjaha) premises. It distinguishes between absolute in the general, specific, and most specific senses. The author first addresses the case where absolute in the general sense is mixed with necessary or possible. The famous view (mashhur) holds that the conclusion follows the minor premise after inverting the major and making it minor. However, the author rejects this, stating that this inversion does not preserve the modality (jah) except in some materials, so it is not reliable. The truth (haqq) is that the conclusion follows the major premise, as known from the reductio (khalaf) and the inversion of the minor. Next, the ruling for mixing with conditional necessities is said to be the same as mixing possibles with conditionals, which is already known. Then the passage discusses mixing absolute with possible. Again, the famous view says the conclusion follows the possible, but the truth is that it follows the major, consistent with earlier discussions on mixing possible and necessary. This applies whether the possible is in the general, specific, or most specific sense. Finally, if the absolute is in the specific sense, its ruling in mixing with modals is the same as the general mixing. Additionally, for the first figure, if the minor is a possible negative with specific possibility (imkan khas), one must convert it to affirmative as previously explained to ensure production. The demonstration is by inverting the minor premise. Throughout, the author upholds that the major premise determines the conclusion's modality, opposing the famous view.

Key Terms
المطلقة
الموجهات
الضرورية
الممكنة

The conclusion's modality in mixed modal syllogisms is determined by the major premise, not the minor.

Fourth Figure: Properties and First Three Conditions
Defines the fourth figure and enumerates its special properties and conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth figure of a categorical syllogism by the position of its middle term and verify whether its first three conditions (affirmative premise, universal premise, universal negative) are satisfied.

Core Idea
The fourth figure yields all conclusions except the universal affirmative, but strict conditions govern its validity.

The fourth figure is the least intuitive of the categorical syllogism figures, yet it completes the logical system. In this lesson, we will examine its defining structure—where the middle term is subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major—and learn the first three conditions that a syllogism must meet to be valid in this figure. Understanding these conditions will sharpen your ability to evaluate arguments in Islamic logic and beyond.

Guided Path
1
Identify the position of the middle term
In the fourth figure, the middle term (الحد الأوسط) appears as the subject of the minor premise and the predicate of the major premise. Verify this ordering in any given syllogism to confirm the figure.
2
List the conclusions the fourth figure can produce
According to the passage, the fourth figure can produce three of the four categorical propositions: universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), and particular negative (O). It cannot produce the universal affirmative (A).
Remember: 'all S are P' (A) is impossible in this figure.
3
Apply the first condition: one premise must be affirmative
The passage states that two negative premises yield no necessary conclusion. Examine the premises: if both are negative, the syllogism is invalid unless one is equivalent to an affirmative (e.g., 'No S is P' can sometimes be rephrased). The examples with 'horse' and 'human' illustrate this.
Check if at least one premise is in the form 'All... is...' or 'Some... is...'.
4
Apply the second condition: one premise must be universal
Two particular premises (e.g., 'Some S is P', 'Some P is M') cannot yield a valid conclusion because the middle term may not be distributed. Ensure at least one premise is a universal proposition (A or E).
5
Apply the third condition: if negative, it must be universal
If a premise is negative (E or O), that negative premise must be universal (E). A particular negative (O) invalidates the syllogism. The passage explains why: if the minor is O, the middle term may not be common; if the major is O, the conclusion does not follow necessarily.
Count the negatives: if you find an O proposition, the syllogism fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو الفصل السادس في تحقيق الشكل الرابع وقرائنه ما الحد الأوسط فيه موضوع في الصغرى ومحمول في الكبرى، وله في إنتاجه خواص وشروط. فأما خواصه فإنتاج المطالب الثلاثة ما عدا الكلي الموجب وإنتاج الجزئي، وإن كانت المقدمات كليّة. وأما شروطه فخمسة: الشرط الأول: إيجاب إحدى مقدمتيه، فلا إنتاج فيه عن سالبتين، إلا أن تكون إحداهما في قوة موجبة، إذ ليس يلزم من سلب الأصغر عن الأوسط وسلب الأوسط عن الأكبر أن تكون النسبة بين الأكبر والأصغر بالإيجاب، وإلا لزم من قولنا: (لا شيء من الفرس (إنسان) و (لا شيء من الحمار فرس) أن يكون كل فرس أو بعض الفرس (إنسان ولا بالسلب وإلا لزم من قولنا: (لا شيء من الفرس (كاتب و (لا شيء من الإنسان (فرس أن يكون بعض الكاتب أو كل كاتب ليس بإنسان بل إن لزم من سلب أو إيجاب فليس إلا باعتبار خصوص المادة، ولا اعتبار به. الشرط الثاني: كليّة إحدى مقدمتيه، فلا قياس عن جزئيتين؛ لجواز اختلاف الحد الأوسط كما سبق. الشرط الثالث كليّة السالبة من مقدمتيه، فلا قياس فيه عما السالبة فيه جزئية؛ لأنها إما صغرى وإما كبرى، فإن كانت صغرى فلجواز أن يكون البعض المحكوم عليه في الصغرى غير البعض المحكوم به في الكبرى، وإذ ذاك فلا يكون الحد الأوسط بينهما مشتركاً، وإن كانت كبرى فالحد الأوسط وإن كان مشتركاً فليس يلزم أن يكون الأكبر ثابتاً للأصغر ولا مسلوباً عنه، أما الأول فلجواز أن تكون النسبة بين كل الأكبر وكل الأصغر بالسلب، كما إذا قلنا كل إنسان حيوان وبعض الحجر
This is the sixth chapter on investigating the fourth figure and its conditions. What is the middle term? It is subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major premise. It has special properties and conditions for its production. As for its properties: it produces three of the four demands except the universal affirmative, and it produces the particular even if the premises are universal. As for its conditions, they are five: The first condition: affirmativeness of one of its two premises, so no production from two negatives unless one of them is in force of affirmative, for it does not follow from 'No horse is a human' and 'No donkey is a horse' that the relation between the greater and the smaller is affirmative; otherwise it would follow from our saying 'No horse is a writer' and 'No human is a horse' that some or every writer is not a human. Rather, if something follows from negation or affirmation, it is only due to the particularity of the matter, and it is not considered. The second condition: universality of one of its two premises, so no syllogism from two particulars because of the possibility of difference of the middle term as previously. The third condition: universality of the negative of its two premises, so no syllogism in which the negative is particular, because it is either minor or major. If it is minor, it is possible that the part judged in the minor is not the part judged in the major, and then the middle term is not common between them. If it is major, even if the middle term is common, it does not necessitate that the greater is established for the smaller nor negated from it. As for the first, it is possible that the relation between all the greater and all the smaller is negative, as when we say 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some stone...'
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and enumerative exposition of the fourth figure (shakl rabi') of the categorical syllogism. It begins by specifying the position of the middle term in this figure: it is the subject in the minor premise and the predicate in the major premise. Then it lists the special properties (khawass) of this figure: it can produce three of the four standard categorical conclusions (A, E, I, O) — specifically, it can produce the universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), and particular negative (O), but not the universal affirmative (A). Moreover, even if both premises are universal, it can still produce a particular conclusion (particulari). Next, the passage enumerates the conditions (shurut) for a valid syllogism in the fourth figure. There are five conditions, but only the first three are given here. The first condition: one premise must be affirmative. Two negative premises yield no necessary conclusion unless one negative is equivalent to an affirmative (e.g., 'No S is P' is equivalent to 'All S is not-P'? The text explains that from two negatives like 'No horse is human' and 'No donkey is horse' we cannot derive an affirmative relation between donkey and human. Likewise, from 'No horse is writer' and 'No human is horse', we cannot infer that some writer is not human; any such inference would depend on the specific matter, not the form, so it is invalid. The second condition: one premise must be universal. Two particular premises fail because the middle term may not be distributed, leading to ambiguity. The third condition: if one premise is negative, it must be universal. A particular negative premise invalidates the syllogism. If it is the minor premise, the part of the subject being negated may not be the same as the part involved in the major premise, so the middle term is not common. If it is the major premise, even if the middle term is common, we cannot deduce that the predicate applies or does not apply to the subject; for example, from 'Every human is animal' and 'Some stone...' (the example is cut off, but it likely illustrates that we need a universal negative to get a valid conclusion). The passage requires careful parsing to understand the conditions.

Key Terms
الشكل الرابع (al-shakl al-rabi'): The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism, where the middle term is subject in the minor and predicate in the major. [Note: In earlier logic, only three figures were recognized; the fourth was added by later logicians like Galen.]
الحد الأوسط (al-hadd al-awsat): The middle term, which appears in both premises but not in the conclusion. [Note: No significant shift.]
المطلوب (al-matlab): The desired conclusion; here refers to the four categorical proposition types: A, E, I, O. [Note: Standard term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) From two universal negatives, a universal negative conclusion.
B) From a particular affirmative and a particular negative, a particular conclusion.
C) From one affirmative premise and one universal premise, any conclusion is possible.
D) From a universal negative and universal affirmative, a particular negative conclusion is possible.

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure produces only three conclusion types (E, I, O) and requires at least one affirmative premise, one universal premise, and that any negative premise be universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Given the syllogism: 'Every ignorant person is blameworthy' (مقدمه صغرى: كل جاهل مذموم) and 'No wise person is ignorant' (مقدمه كبرى: لا شيء من العاقل بجاهل). Identify the figure, check the first three conditions, and state the valid conclusion if any.

The Fourth Figure: Definition and Characteristics
Author defines the fourth figure and its two characteristics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the position of the middle term in the fourth figure and state its two special characteristics.

Core Idea
The fourth figure uniquely excludes universal affirmative conclusions and yields particular conclusions even from universal premises.

The fourth figure of the categorical syllogism is the final figure discussed by logicians. In this lesson, we will define its structure and highlight its two unique properties: it cannot produce a universal affirmative conclusion, and it can produce a particular conclusion even when both premises are universal. Understanding these characteristics is essential for evaluating the validity of arguments in Islamic sciences, such as kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the middle term position
In the fourth figure, the middle term (hadd al-awsat) is the subject of the minor premise and the predicate of the major premise. Compare this to the first figure where the middle term is subject in the major and predicate in the minor, etc.
Memorize: fourth figure = middle term subject in minor, predicate in major.
2
List the possible conclusions
The fourth figure can produce three types of conclusions: universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), and particular negative (O). It cannot produce a universal affirmative (A). This is a key difference from other figures.
3
Understand the second characteristic
Even if both premises are universal, the conclusion can be particular. This is unique to the fourth figure; in other figures, universal premises always yield a universal conclusion if the form is valid.
Example: 'Every A is B; Every B is C' in the fourth figure does not yield 'Every A is C' (that would require a different figure).
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden (major premise); Every khamr is an intoxicant (minor premise).' In the fourth figure, the middle term 'intoxicant' is subject in the minor and predicate in the major. The conclusion cannot be 'Every khamr is forbidden' (universal affirmative); it would rather be 'Some forbidden things are khamr' (particular affirmative) or a negative particular. Understand that the fourth figure does not directly give a universal affirmative conclusion.

This is the sixth chapter on the investigation of the fourth figure and its moods. The middle term in it is subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major premise. It has in its production characteristics and conditions. As for its characteristics: it produces the three desired conclusions except the universal affirmative, and it produces the particular even if the premises are universal. As for its conditions, they are five: The first condition: affirmation of one of its two premises, so there is no production from two negative premises, unless one of them is in the power of an affirmative, because it is not necessary from the negation of the minor from the middle and the negation of the middle from the major that the relation between the major and the minor be affirmative; otherwise it would follow from our statement: 'No horse is a human' and 'No donkey is a horse' that every horse or some horse is a human; and not by negation, otherwise it would follow from our statement: 'No horse is a writer' and 'No human is a horse' that some writer or every writer is not a human. Rather, if a conclusion follows from negation or affirmation, it is only by considering the particular matter, and it is not considered. The second condition: universality of one of its two premises, so no syllogism from two particulars, because of the possibility of difference of the middle term as previously. The third condition: universality of the negative among its two premises, so no syllogism in which the negative is particular, because it is either the minor or the major. If it is the minor, then it is possible that the part judged in the minor is not the part judged in the major, and then the middle term is not shared between them. If it is the major, then the middle term, even if shared, does not necessitate that the major be affirmed of the minor or denied of it. As for the first [i.e., no affirmation], it is possible that the relation between all the major and all the minor is [one of] negation, as when we say: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some stone is not a human.' As for the second [i.e., no negation], it is possible that some part of the major, which is 'A', is not the part from which 'B' is denied, and it is affirmed of all the minor; and then there is no negation, as when we say: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some body is not a human.' The fourth condition: its minor should be universal affirmative if its major is particular affirmative. As for the condition of its universality, it is because there is no production from two particulars. As for the condition of its affirmation, it is because there is no syllogism if it is negative, and it is not necessary that the major be affirmed of the minor or denied of it. As for there being no affirmation, it is because one of the two premises is negative, and negation does not produce affirmation. As for there being no negation, it is because it is possible that the other part of the major, which is 'A' that is not judged by 'B', is affirmed of all the minor, as when we say: 'No human is a stone' and 'Some body is a human.' The fifth condition: its major should be universal negative if its minor is particular affirmative. As for the condition of its negation, it is because there is no syllogism if it is affirmative, because it is possible that some part of the limit, which is the major, is not the part judged by 'J' in the middle that is judged of the minor; and when the middle term differs, there is no production. As for its moods composed of quantified propositions, they are sixteen as previously, except that the condition of affirmation of one of its two premises drops from them four: the two universal negatives, the two particulars, and that in which the minor is particular or the major [is particular]. And the condition of universality of one of its two premises drops from them three: those composed of two particulars affirmative, and of two particulars with the major negative and the minor affirmative, and of two particulars with the minor negative and the major affirmative. And the condition of universality of the negative among its two premises drops from them two: those composed of two universals, minor affirmative and major particular negative, and the one composed of minor particular negative and major universal affirmative. And the condition that the minor be universal affirmative when the major is particular affirmative drops from it one conjunction: that composed of minor universal negative and major particular affirmative.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed exposition of the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism (al-shakl al-rabi' min al-qiyas al-hamli). The author begins by defining the position of the middle term in this figure: it is subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major premise. Then he states that the fourth figure has two special characteristics: first, it produces three of the four standard conclusions (the universal affirmative is excluded), and second, it can produce a particular conclusion even when both premises are universal. After this, the five necessary conditions for a valid syllogism in the fourth figure are listed and explained. The first condition is that at least one premise must be affirmative; two negatives yield no necessary conclusion unless one of them is equivalent to an affirmative due to the matter. The author illustrates with examples: from 'No horse is a human' and 'No donkey is a horse' we cannot conclude that any horse is a human, nor can we conclude a negative universally. He warns that any conclusion here would depend on the specific matter, not the form. The second condition is that one premise must be universal; two particulars are invalid because the middle term may not be common. The third condition is that if a premise is negative, it must be universal. The author explains the problems when the particular negative is the minor (the middle term may not be shared) or the major (the conclusion may not follow necessarily). Examples are given: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some stone is not a human' fails to give a conclusion, while 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some body is not a human' also fails. The fourth condition: when the major is particular affirmative, the minor must be universal affirmative. The reason for universality is to avoid two particulars, and for affirmation is that a negative minor would prevent both affirmative and negative conclusions (as shown by the example: 'No human is a stone' and 'Some body is a human' yields no conclusion). The fifth condition: when the minor is particular affirmative, the major must be universal negative. If the major were affirmative, the middle term might not be common, leading to no production. After explaining these conditions, the author enumerates how many of the sixteen possible moods (composed of quantified premises: A, E, I, O) are eliminated by each condition. First condition drops four: the two universal negatives (EE), the two particulars (II, OO?), and the moods where the minor or major is particular. Then the second condition drops three more (the ones with two particulars). The third condition drops two (one with universal affirmative minor and particular negative major, and one with particular negative minor and universal affirmative major). Finally, the fourth condition drops one more (universal negative minor with particular affirmative major). The resulting valid moods after applying all conditions are not explicitly listed here but can be derived. The passage is a typical logical analysis of the fourth figure, showing the syntactical constraints for valid inference.

Key Terms

Checkpoint
According to the author, which type of conclusion is NOT produced by the fourth figure?
A) Universal negative (E)
B) Particular affirmative (I)
C) Universal affirmative (A)
D) Particular negative (O)

Key Takeaway
The fourth figure is defined by the middle term as subject in the minor and predicate in the major, and it uniquely excludes universal affirmative conclusions while allowing particular conclusions from universal premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the following conclusions can be produced in the fourth figure: 'Every human is mortal', 'Some scholars are pious', 'No animal is stone', 'Some water is not pure'. Explain why or why not.
?
Take the premises 'Every fajr prayer is obligatory' (major) and 'Every obligatory act is rewarded' (minor). Determine which figure this is and list the possible conclusions.

The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
The Five Conditions for Valid Fourth-Figure Syllogisms
Author details the five conditions for validity in the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the five conditions to determine whether a fourth-figure syllogism is valid.

Core Idea
Five conditions filter out invalid moods: they ensure the middle term is shared and the conclusion follows necessarily.

In the previous lesson, we learned the basic structure of the fourth figure. Now we delve into the five conditions that must be satisfied for a fourth-figure syllogism to be valid. These conditions eliminate many of the sixteen possible moods (combinations of premise types) and guarantee that the middle term is properly connected. Each condition is justified with logical reasoning, often illustrated with examples that show what can go wrong when the condition is violated.

Guided Path
1
Understand Condition 1: One premise must be affirmative
Two negative premises cannot produce a necessary conclusion. The author illustrates with the example of 'No horse is a human' and 'No donkey is a horse' – no conclusion about horse and human follows formally. Replace with an Islamic example: 'No prayer is invalid' (major) and 'No invalid act is required' (minor) yields no conclusion.
Exception: if one negative is equivalent to an affirmative due to the matter, but that is not formal.
2
Understand Condition 2: One premise must be universal
Two particular premises fail because the middle term may not be common between the two parts. For example, 'Some scholars are wealthy' and 'Some wealthy are generous' does not guarantee a conclusion about scholars and generosity.
This is a general rule for all figures.
3
Understand Condition 3: Any negative premise must be universal
If the negative is particular, the middle term may not be shared (if minor is particular negative) or the conclusion does not follow (if major is particular negative). The author gives examples using variables: 'Every A is B; Some C is not A' fails to produce a conclusion about B and C.
4
Understand Conditions 4 and 5: Specific restrictions on minors and majors
Condition 4: If the major is particular affirmative (I), the minor must be universal affirmative (A). Condition 5: If the minor is particular affirmative (I), the major must be universal negative (E). These prevent situations where the middle term is not distributed properly.
Use Islamic examples: e.g., for condition 4: major 'Some permissible things are food' (I), minor must be 'Every date is permissible' (A) to avoid two particulars.
5
Apply the conditions to a candidate mood
Take the mood where minor is universal affirmative (A) and major is universal negative (E). Both conditions are satisfied: one affirmative, one universal, and the negative is universal. This mood is valid for the fourth figure (producing a particular negative conclusion). Check any other mood against the five conditions.

الكبرى، وإذ ذاك فلا يكون الحد الأوسط بينهما مشتركاً، وإن كانت كبرى فالحد الأوسط وإن كان مشتركاً فليس يلزم أن يكون الأكبر ثابتاً للأصغر ولا مسلوباً عنه، أما الأول فلجواز أن تكون النسبة بين كل الأكبر قلنا (كل إنسان حيوان) و(بعض الجسم ليس إنساناً). الشرط الرابع أن تكون صغراه كليّة موجبة إن كانت كبراه جزئية موجبة، أما اشتراط كليتها فلعدم الإنتاج عن جزئيتين، وأما اشتراط إيجابها فلأنه لا قياس إن كانت سالبة، ولا يلزم أن يكون الأكبر ثابتاً للأصغر ولا مسلوباً عنه، أما أنه لا إيجاب فلأن إحدى المقدمتين سالبة، والسلب لا ينتج الإيجاب، وأما أنه لا سلب فلجواز أن يكون البعض الآخر من الحد الأكبر وهو "أ" الذي إن كانت موجبة لجواز أن يكون البعض من الحد وهو الحد الأكبر غير البعض المحكوم عليه بـ "ج" في الأوسط المحكوم به على الصغرى، وعند اختلاف الحد الأوسط فلا إنتاج. وأما قرائنه المؤلفة من المحصورات فست عشرة على ما سبق، إلا أن اشتراط إيجاب إحدى مقدمتيه أسقط منها أربعاً، وهي السالبتان الكليتان، والجزئيتان، وما الصغرى فيه جزئية أو الكبرى. واشتراط كليّة إحدى مقدمتيه أسقط منها ثلاثاً، ثلاثاً، هي الكائنة الكائنة من جزئيتين موجبتين ومن جزئيتين و الكبرى سالبة والصغرى موجبة، ومن جزئيتين والصغرى سالبة والكبرى موجبة. واشتراط كليّة السالبة من مقدمتيه أسقط منها اثنتين وهما الكائنة من كليتين موجبة صغرى وجزئية سالبة كبرى والكائنة من جزئية سالبة صغرى وكلية موجبة كبرى. واشتراط كون الصغرى كليّة موجبة عند كون الكبرى جزئية موجبة أسقط منها اقتراناً واحداً، وهو الكائن من كلية سالبة صغرى وجزئية موجبة كبرى.
This is the sixth chapter on the investigation of the fourth figure and its moods. The middle term in it is subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major premise. It has in its production characteristics and conditions. As for its characteristics: it produces the three desired conclusions except the universal affirmative, and it produces the particular even if the premises are universal. As for its conditions, they are five: The first condition: affirmation of one of its two premises, so there is no production from two negative premises, unless one of them is in the power of an affirmative, because it is not necessary from the negation of the minor from the middle and the negation of the middle from the major that the relation between the major and the minor be affirmative; otherwise it would follow from our statement: 'No horse is a human' and 'No donkey is a horse' that every horse or some horse is a human; and not by negation, otherwise it would follow from our statement: 'No horse is a writer' and 'No human is a horse' that some writer or every writer is not a human. Rather, if a conclusion follows from negation or affirmation, it is only by considering the particular matter, and it is not considered. The second condition: universality of one of its two premises, so no syllogism from two particulars, because of the possibility of difference of the middle term as previously. The third condition: universality of the negative among its two premises, so no syllogism in which the negative is particular, because it is either the minor or the major. If it is the minor, then it is possible that the part judged in the minor is not the part judged in the major, and then the middle term is not shared between them. If it is the major, then the middle term, even if shared, does not necessitate that the major be affirmed of the minor or denied of it. As for the first [i.e., no affirmation], it is possible that the relation between all the major and all the minor is [one of] negation, as when we say: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some stone is not a human.' As for the second [i.e., no negation], it is possible that some part of the major, which is 'A', is not the part from which 'B' is denied, and it is affirmed of all the minor; and then there is no negation, as when we say: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some body is not a human.' The fourth condition: its minor should be universal affirmative if its major is particular affirmative. As for the condition of its universality, it is because there is no production from two particulars. As for the condition of its affirmation, it is because there is no syllogism if it is negative, and it is not necessary that the major be affirmed of the minor or denied of it. As for there being no affirmation, it is because one of the two premises is negative, and negation does not produce affirmation. As for there being no negation, it is because it is possible that the other part of the major, which is 'A' that is not judged by 'B', is affirmed of all the minor, as when we say: 'No human is a stone' and 'Some body is a human.' The fifth condition: its major should be universal negative if its minor is particular affirmative. As for the condition of its negation, it is because there is no syllogism if it is affirmative, because it is possible that some part of the limit, which is the major, is not the part judged by 'J' in the middle that is judged of the minor; and when the middle term differs, there is no production. As for its moods composed of quantified propositions, they are sixteen as previously, except that the condition of affirmation of one of its two premises drops from them four: the two universal negatives, the two particulars, and that in which the minor is particular or the major [is particular]. And the condition of universality of one of its two premises drops from them three: those composed of two particulars affirmative, and of two particulars with the major negative and the minor affirmative, and of two particulars with the minor negative and the major affirmative. And the condition of universality of the negative among its two premises drops from them two: those composed of two universals, minor affirmative and major particular negative, and the one composed of minor particular negative and major universal affirmative. And the condition that the minor be universal affirmative when the major is particular affirmative drops from it one conjunction: that composed of minor universal negative and major particular affirmative.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed exposition of the fourth figure of the categorical syllogism (al-shakl al-rabi' min al-qiyas al-hamli). The author begins by defining the position of the middle term in this figure: it is subject in the minor premise and predicate in the major premise. Then he states that the fourth figure has two special characteristics: first, it produces three of the four standard conclusions (the universal affirmative is excluded), and second, it can produce a particular conclusion even when both premises are universal. After this, the five necessary conditions for a valid syllogism in the fourth figure are listed and explained. The first condition is that at least one premise must be affirmative; two negatives yield no necessary conclusion unless one of them is equivalent to an affirmative due to the matter. The author illustrates with examples: from 'No horse is a human' and 'No donkey is a horse' we cannot conclude that any horse is a human, nor can we conclude a negative universally. He warns that any conclusion here would depend on the specific matter, not the form. The second condition is that one premise must be universal; two particulars are invalid because the middle term may not be common. The third condition is that if a premise is negative, it must be universal. The author explains the problems when the particular negative is the minor (the middle term may not be shared) or the major (the conclusion may not follow necessarily). Examples are given: 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some stone is not a human' fails to give a conclusion, while 'Every human is an animal' and 'Some body is not a human' also fails. The fourth condition: when the major is particular affirmative, the minor must be universal affirmative. The reason for universality is to avoid two particulars, and for affirmation is that a negative minor would prevent both affirmative and negative conclusions (as shown by the example: 'No human is a stone' and 'Some body is a human' yields no conclusion). The fifth condition: when the minor is particular affirmative, the major must be universal negative. If the major were affirmative, the middle term might not be common, leading to no production. After explaining these conditions, the author enumerates how many of the sixteen possible moods (composed of quantified premises: A, E, I, O) are eliminated by each condition. First condition drops four: the two universal negatives (EE), the two particulars (II, OO?), and the moods where the minor or major is particular. Then the second condition drops three more (the ones with two particulars). The third condition drops two (one with universal affirmative minor and particular negative major, and one with particular negative minor and universal affirmative major). Finally, the fourth condition drops one more (universal negative minor with particular affirmative major). The resulting valid moods after applying all conditions are not explicitly listed here but can be derived. The passage is a typical logical analysis of the fourth figure, showing the syntactical constraints for valid inference.

Key Takeaway
The five conditions ensure that the middle term is properly distributed and the conclusion follows necessarily; violating any of them renders a fourth-figure syllogism invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Check if the following fourth-figure syllogism is valid using the five conditions: Minor premise: 'Some prayer is obligatory' (I). Major premise: 'Every obligatory act is rewarded' (A). State whether it satisfies each condition and why.
?
Construct a valid fourth-figure syllogism using Islamic terms that satisfies all five conditions. For example, minor: 'Every fajr prayer is obligatory' (A), major: 'No obligatory act is sinful' (E). Determine the conclusion.

The Syllogism
