Reducing 16 Conjunctions to 5 Productive Moods
Author enumerates conditions that eliminate 11 of 16 possible conjunctions, leaving 5 productive moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which conjunctions are eliminated by each condition and verify that only 5 productive moods remain in the first figure.

Core Idea
Only 5 of 16 first-figure conjunctions survive after applying four key conditions.

In the first figure of categorical syllogism, there are 16 possible conjunctions based on quantity and quality. However, not all of them yield a valid conclusion. The author systematically applies four conditions derived from syllogistic rules to eliminate the invalid ones, leaving exactly 5 productive moods. This lesson guides you through each condition and its effect on the set of conjunctions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the starting set of 16 conjunctions
Recall that the first figure has 16 possible combinations of premises in terms of quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative). This is the baseline before applying any conditions.
The 16 come from 2 prem times 2 quantities times 2 qualities for each prem? Actually 4 types per premise × 4 = 16.
2
Trace the requirement of at least one affirmative premise
The first condition states that one premise must be affirmative. This eliminates 4 conjunctions: those with both premises negative, and those where both are particular? Actually the text says it drops the two universal negatives, the two particulars, and those with minor particular or major particular? Let's parse: the condition drops four specific types: 1) both universal negatives, 2) both particular negatives? The Arabic says 'السالبتان الكليتان، والجزئيتان، وما الصغرى فيه جزئية أو الكبرى' meaning the two universal negatives, the two partculars (presumably both particular negatives?), and those in which the minor is particular or the major is particular. This is a bit ambiguous, but the key is that 4 conjunctions are removed.
The condition eliminates cases where both premises are negative (AA? Actually it's about affirmation, so at least one must be affirmative; thus all-negative conjunctions are out.
3
Trace the requirement of at least one universal premise
The second condition requires at least one universal premise. This drops 3 conjunctions: those composed of two particular affirmatives; two particulars with major negative and minor affirmative; and two particulars with minor negative and major affirmative. Note that these are all cases where both premises are particular.
The condition ensures that we avoid the invalid case of two particulars producing nothing.
4
Trace the requirement that the negative premise be universal
The third condition: if a premise is negative, it must be universal. This eliminates 2 conjunctions: one with universal affirmative minor and particular negative major, and one with particular negative minor and universal affirmative major.
This prevents cases where the negative is particular, which is insufficient for a valid syllogism.
5
Apply the fourth and fifth specific conditions
The fourth condition: when the major premise is particular affirmative, the minor must be universal affirmative. This drops the conjunction with universal negative minor and particular affirmative major. The fifth condition: when the minor premise is particular affirmative, the major must be universal negative. This drops the conjunction with two affirmatives where the minor is particular and the major is universal. Each drops one more conjunction, totaling 11 dropped and 5 remaining.
These are the final two conditions that fine-tune the set.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرط الرابع أن تكون صغراه كليّة موجبة إن كانت كبراه جزئية موجبة، أما اشتراط كليتها فلعدم الإنتاج عن جزئيتين، وأما اشتراط إيجابها فلأنه لا قياس إن كانت سالبة، ولا يلزم أن يكون الأكبر ثابتاً للأصغر ولا مسلوباً عنه، أما أنه لا إيجاب فلأن إحدى المقدمتين سالبة، والسلب لا ينتج الإيجاب، وأما أنه لا سلب فلجواز أن يكون البعض الآخر من الحد الأكبر وهو "أ" الذي لم يحكم عليه بـ "ب" ثابتاً لجميع الحد الأصغر، كما إذا قلنا لا شيء من الإنسان (حجر) و(بعض الجسم إنسان). الشرط الخامس : أن تكون كبراه كليّة سالبة إن كانت صغراه جزئية موجبة، أما اشتراط سلبها فلأنه لا قياس إن كانت موجبة لجواز أن يكون البعض من الحد وهو الحد الأكبر غير البعض المحكوم عليه بـ "ج" في الأوسط المحكوم به على الصغرى، وعند اختلاف الحد الأوسط فلا إنتاج. وأما قرائنه المؤلفة من المحصورات فست عشرة على ما سبق، إلا أن اشتراط إيجاب إحدى مقدمتيه أسقط منها أربعاً، وهي السالبتان الكليتان، والجزئيتان، وما الصغرى فيه جزئية أو الكبرى. واشتراط كليّة إحدى مقدمتيه أسقط منها ثلاثاً، ثلاثاً، هي الكائنة الكائنة من جزئيتين موجبتين ومن جزئيتين و الكبرى سالبة والصغرى موجبة، ومن جزئيتين والصغرى سالبة والكبرى موجبة. واشتراط كليّة السالبة من مقدمتيه أسقط منها اثنتين وهما الكائنة من كليتين موجبة صغرى وجزئية سالبة كبرى والكائنة من جزئية سالبة صغرى وكلية موجبة كبرى. واشتراط كون الصغرى كليّة موجبة عند كون الكبرى جزئية موجبة أسقط منها اقتراناً واحداً، وهو الكائن من كلية سالبة صغرى وجزئية موجبة كبرى. واشتراط كون كبراه سالبة كلية عند كون صغراه موجبة جزئية أسقط منها اقتراناً واحداً، وهو الكائن من موجبتين والصغرى جزئية والكبرى كليّة، فكانت جملة السواقط إحدى عشرة قرينة، فعادت قرائنه المنتجة إلى خمس.
The fourth condition: that its minor premise be universal affirmative if its major premise is particular affirmative. As for the requirement of its universality, it is because no production results from two particulars. As for the requirement of its affirmation, it is because there is no syllogism if it is negative. And it is not necessary that the major term be affirmed of the minor nor denied of it. As for that there is no affirmation, it is because one of the two premises is negative, and negation does not produce affirmation. As for that there is no negation, it is because it is possible that the other part of the major term, which is 'A' that was not judged by 'B', is affirmed of all the minor term, as when we say: 'No human is (stone)' and '(Some body is human)'. The fifth condition: that its major premise be universal negative if its minor premise is particular affirmative. As for the requirement of its negation, it is because there is no syllogism if it is affirmative, due to the possibility that some part of the term, namely the major term, is not the part judged by 'C' in the middle term judged of the minor, and when the middle term differs, there is no production. As for its conjunctions composed of quantified propositions, they are sixteen as previously, except that the requirement of affirmation of one of its two premises drops four of them: namely the two universal negatives, the two particulars, and those in which the minor is particular or the major. And the requirement of universality of one of its two premises drops three of them: namely those composed of two particular affirmatives, and of two particulars with the major negative and the minor affirmative, and of two particulars with the minor negative and the major affirmative. And the requirement of universality of the negative of its two premises drops two of them: namely those composed of two universals: affirmative minor and particular negative major, and those composed of particular negative minor and universal affirmative major. And the requirement that the minor be universal affirmative when the major is particular affirmative drops one conjunction: namely that composed of universal negative minor and particular affirmative major. And the requirement that its major be universal negative when its minor is particular affirmative drops one conjunction: namely that composed of two affirmatives with the minor particular and the major universal. So the total dropped is eleven conjunctions, so its productive conjunctions return to five.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the conditions that restrict the valid moods (conjunctions) in the first figure of categorical syllogism. The author specifies the fourth and fifth conditions, then quantifies how each condition reduces the initial sixteen possible conjunctions. The fourth condition requires that when the major is particular affirmative, the minor must be universal affirmative. The reasoning is that two particulars produce nothing, and a negative minor cannot produce an affirmative conclusion, nor can it guarantee a negative conclusion because the major term might not be entirely denied of the minor. The example given is 'No human is a stone' (universal negative) and 'Some body is human' (particular affirmative) — here the minor is 'human', the major is 'body', and the middle is 'stone'? Actually the example is unclear but illustrates the possibility. The fifth condition requires the major to be universal negative when the minor is particular affirmative, because an affirmative major could allow the middle term to differ across the premises. Then the author tabulates the effect of each condition: requiring at least one affirmative premise eliminates 4; requiring at least one universal eliminates 3 (but the count is given as 'three, three' perhaps meaning three from two separate lists? The text seems to repeat 'three' and lists three cases); requiring the negative to be universal eliminates 2; and the last two conditions each eliminate one. The total dropped is 11, leaving 5 productive conjunctions. This enumeration is typical of Aristotelian logic texts that systematically derive valid moods.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
From the list of 16 possible first-figure conjunctions, apply the four conditions discussed in this lesson to determine which 5 remain. Write them out in standard form (e.g., universal affirmative minor, universal affirmative major, etc.).
?
Construct a valid first-figure categorical syllogism on a fiqh topic (e.g., concerning the prohibition of riba). Verify that its mood is among the 5 productive ones.

The Syllogism

Classification of Modal Syllogisms
Classification of modal syllogisms into necessary vs possible, absolute vs conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any modal syllogism into its proper category (modal vs absolute, necessary vs possible, absolute vs conditional) based on the mode of its premises.

Core Idea
Master the classification tree to apply the correct syllogistic rules.

Before diving into the proofs of modal syllogisms, the author first establishes a clear classification of possible premise types. This taxonomy is essential because each category has its own rules for validity. In this lesson, we will follow the author's logical divisions to understand where the first two conjunctions fit.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overall classification
Read the opening sentence: 'وإما أن تكون من ذوات الجهة، أو الإطلاق، أو مختلطة'. This divides conjunctions into three types: modal (dhāt al-jiha), absolute (muṭlaqa), and mixed (mukhtalita). Note the author's plan to treat each in turn.
Modal conjunctions have premises with modal qualifiers (necessity, possibility).
2
Distinguish necessary from possible
Within modal, the author separates 'ضروريات' (necessary) from 'ممكنات' (possible). Necessary propositions assert that a predicate holds necessarily for the subject; possible ones assert non-impossibility.
The first two conjunctions are from the necessary category.
3
Subclassify necessary propositions
Necessary propositions are further divided into 'مطلقة' (absolute) and 'مشروطة' (conditional). The author limits the discussion to absolute necessary, which are unconditional necessary statements.
Examples: 'Necessarily, no B is C' is absolute necessary.
4
Locate the first two conjunctions
Having fixed the category (absolute necessary modal), the author introduces the first two conjunctions: one with a negative and an affirmative universal premise (producing universal negative), and one with two affirmative universal premises (producing particular affirmative with possibility). This is the focus of the next lessons.
Understanding the hierarchy helps you quickly identify which rules apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإما أن تكون من ذوات الجهة، أو الإطلاق، أو مختلطة، فإن كانت من ذوات الجهة فإما من الضروريات أو الممكنات، فإن كانت من الضروريات فإما من المطلقة أو المشروطة، ولنفرض الكلام أولاً في الضروريات المطلقة منها: الاقتران الأول من كليتين صغراهما سالبة وكبراهما موجبة ونتيجته كلية سالبة، كقولنا: بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") و (بالضرورة كل "أ" "ب") فـ (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ج" "أ") لأن "أ" داخل تحت "ب" و"ب" مباين لكل "ج" فـ "أ" مباين لكل "ج" بالضرورة، ويجب أن يكون "ج" مبايناً لكل "أ" بالضرورة، وإن جعلت الصغرى كبرى عاد إلى الاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول ناتجاً بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "") وعكس بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ج") (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ج" "أ") وهو عين المطلوب، ولأنه لو لم يصدق بالضرورة لا شيء من "ج" "أ") لصدق (بالإمكان العام بعض "ج" "أ" ) وكان (بالضرورة كل "أ" "ب") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ج" "ب") وهو خلف، إذ هو نقيض عكس (لا شيء من "ب" "ج"). الاقتران الثاني من كليتين موجبتين ونتيجته جزئية موجبة بالإمكان العام كقولنا : (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج" ) و (بالضرورة كل "أ" "ب") فـ (بالإمكان العام بعض "ج" "أ") لأن "ج" ضروري لكل "ب" و"ب" ضروري لكل "أ" فـ "ج" ضروري لكل "أ" و عكس بالضرورة كل "أ" "ج") (بالإمكان العام بعض "ج" "أ") لأنك إن جعلت الصغرى كبرى أنتج من الاقتران الأول من الشكل الأول (كل "أ" "ج" بالضرورة) وعكسه (بعض "أ" "ج" بالإمكان العام ولأنه إن لم يصدق (بالإمكان العام بعض "ج" "أ") فـ (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ج" "أ") وكان (كل "ب" "ج" بالضرورة) ف بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "أ") وعكسه (بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ب") وكان بالضرورة كل "أ" "ب") وهو خلف، وهذا الاقتران وإن شارك الأول في الكمية ولم يفارقه في غير الكيفية، فليس يجب أن تكون نتيجته كليّة كالأول، ولا نتيجته الأول
And they may be from the modal propositions, or absolute, or mixed. If they are from the modal, then either necessary or possible. If from the necessary, then either absolute or conditional. Let us first assume speech about the absolute necessary from them: The first conjunction: from two universals, their minor negative and their major affirmative, and its conclusion is universal negative, as we say: 'Necessarily, no B is C' and 'Necessarily, every A is B' so 'Necessarily, no C is A' because A is contained under B and B is separate from every C, so A is separate from every C necessarily, and it must be that C is separate from every A necessarily. And if you make the minor major, it returns to the second conjunction of the first figure producing necessarily no A is [C?] and the converse 'Necessarily, no A is C' [gives] 'Necessarily, no C is A' which is exactly what is required. And because if 'Necessarily, no C is A' were not true, then 'In general possibility, some C is A' would be true, and (necessarily every A is B) so (necessarily some C is B) which is a contradiction, since it is the contradictory of the converse of 'no B is C'. The second conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and its conclusion is particular affirmative in general possibility, as we say: 'Necessarily, every B is C' and 'Necessarily, every A is B' so 'In general possibility, some C is A' because C is necessary for every B and B is necessary for every A, so C is necessary for every A, and the converse of 'Necessarily, every A is C' [gives] 'In general possibility, some C is A'. Because if you make the minor major, it produces from the first conjunction of the first figure 'Every A is C necessarily' and its converse 'Some A is C in general possibility'. And because if 'In general possibility, some C is A' were not true, then 'Necessarily, no C is A' would be true, and (every B is C necessarily) so (necessarily no B is A) and its converse (necessarily no A is B) while (necessarily every A is B) which is a contradiction. And this conjunction, although it shares with the first in quantity and does not differ from it except in quality, its conclusion does not have to be universal like the first, nor its conclusion the first.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents the first two productive conjunctions under the category of absolute necessary modal propositions. The author first classifies conjunctions into modal, absolute, and mixed; within modal, into necessary and possible; within necessary, into absolute and conditional. He then focuses on absolute necessary. The first conjunction consists of two universal premises: the minor is negative, the major affirmative, producing a universal negative conclusion. The example uses terms A, B, C: necessarily no B is C, necessarily every A is B, therefore necessarily no C is A. The proof proceeds by subsumption: A is under B, B is separate from all C, so A is separate from all C necessarily. The author also provides a conversion proof: swapping premises yields the second conjunction of the first figure (which would be universal negative), and then converse of the conclusion gives the same result. A reductio argument is given: if the conclusion were false, then some C is possibly A, but then since every A is necessarily B, some C would be necessarily B, contradicting the first premise's converse (no B is C). The second conjunction is from two universal affirmatives, producing a particular affirmative conclusion in general possibility. Example: necessarily every B is C, necessarily every A is B, so in general possibility some C is A. The proof: since C is necessary for every B and B for every A, C is necessary for every A, and conversion from universal affirmative to particular (aks) yields the conclusion. The reductio: if it were false that some C is possibly A, then necessarily no C is A, and with every B necessarily C, we get necessarily no B is A, converted to necessarily no A is B, contradicting the second premise. The author notes that despite resembling the first in quantity (both universal), the difference in quality (affirmative vs negative) forces the conclusion to be particular affirmative rather than universal, explaining that the conclusion of the second is not universal like the first.

إمكان
مطلقة
مشروطة
اقتران

A) Absolute and conditional

C) Affirmative and negative
D) Necessary and possible

Key Takeaway
Modal syllogisms are first classified by modality (necessary/possible) and then by type (absolute/conditional).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the premise 'Possibly, the world is eternal', classify it within the modal taxonomy (modal, necessary or possible?).

The Syllogism

The First Conjunction: Universal Negative (Celarent)
First productive modal conjunction with universal negative conclusion and its proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and prove the first modal conjunction (universal negative conclusion from two universal premises, one negative one affirmative) using subsumption, conversion, and reductio ad absurdum.

Core Idea
A negative premise and an affirmative premise yield a universal negative conclusion in modal logic.

The first conjunction is the modal analogue of Celarent in Aristotelian logic. The author presents it with two universal premises: one necessary negative, one necessary affirmative. The conclusion is a universal negative. He gives three proofs: direct subsumption, conversion to a standard form, and reductio. Let's walk through each proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The example uses terms A, B, C: 'Necessarily, no B is C' (saliba kulliya) and 'Necessarily, every A is B' (mujiba kulliya). Note that the first premise is negative, the second affirmative; both are universal and necessary.
The order is minor negative, major affirmative according to the author.
2
Apply subsumption directly
The author says because A is contained under B (كل أ ب) and B is separate from every C (لا شيء من ب ج), therefore A is separate from every C. This is a direct inference: if all A are B and no B is C, then no A is C.
This is valid in first figure: middle term B.
3
Perform the conversion proof
Swap the premises: make the original minor the major and vice versa. This yields the second conjunction of the first figure (which also produces a universal negative). Then convert the conclusion 'Necessarily no A is C' to 'Necessarily no C is A', which is the desired conclusion.
This shows the symmetry of the first figure.
4
Confirm via reductio
Suppose the conclusion 'Necessarily no C is A' is false. Then its contradictory 'Possibly some C is A' (bi-l-imkān al-‘āmm) is true. But with 'Necessarily every A is B', we get 'Necessarily some C is B'. This contradicts the first premise's converse 'Necessarily no B is C'. Therefore the conclusion must be true.
Reductio relies on the impossibility of the contradictory.

The Second Conjunction: Particular Affirmative (Darii Analogue)
Second productive modal conjunction with particular affirmative conclusion in general possibility and its proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and prove the second modal conjunction (particular affirmative conclusion in general possibility from two universal affirmative premises) using subsumption, conversion, and reductio, and explain why the conclusion is particular rather than universal.

Core Idea
Two universal affirmatives yield a particular affirmative conclusion in general possibility, not a universal, due to the nature of modal conversion.

The second conjunction involves two universal affirmative premises. Intuitively, we might expect a universal affirmative conclusion (Barbara), but the author shows that the conclusion is actually a particular affirmative with the modal qualifier 'in general possibility' (imkān ‘āmm). The reason lies in the conversion step required to reach the desired conclusion. Let's examine the proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises
The example: 'Necessarily, every B is C' and 'Necessarily, every A is B'. Both are universal affirmative and necessary. The conclusion is 'In general possibility, some C is A'.
The major premise is 'every B is C', the minor is 'every A is B'.
2
Apply subsumption to get a universal conclusion
Since C is necessary for every B, and B is necessary for every A, then C is necessary for every A. So we have 'Necessarily, every A is C'. This is a direct Barbara inference.
This step yields a universal affirmative, not the final conclusion.
3
Convert to particular by 'aks
The universal affirmative 'Necessarily, every A is C' is converted per accidens to 'In general possibility, some C is A'. Because conversion of a necessary universal affirmative does not preserve universality, only particularity with possibility.
This is the key: 'aks of a necessary U.A. gives a particular possible, not a universal necessary.
4
Confirm via reductio
Suppose the conclusion 'In general possibility, some C is A' is false. Then its contradictory 'Necessarily, no C is A' is true. With 'Necessarily, every B is C', we get 'Necessarily, no B is A' by subsumption? Actually the author's reducio uses the second premise to derive a contradiction with the first. Let's reconstruct: if no C is A necessarily, and every B is C necessarily, then no B is A necessarily. Then by conversion, no A is B necessarily, but the second premise says every A is B necessarily. Contradiction.
The reductio validates the particular possible conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second conjunction: from two universal affirmatives, and its conclusion is particular affirmative in general possibility, as we say: 'Necessarily, every B is C' and 'Necessarily, every A is B' so 'In general possibility, some C is A' because C is necessary for every B and B is necessary for every A, so C is necessary for every A, and the converse of 'Necessarily, every A is C' [gives] 'In general possibility, some C is A'. Because if you make the minor major, it produces from the first conjunction of the first figure 'Every A is C necessarily' and its converse 'Some A is C in general possibility'. And because if 'In general possibility, some C is A' were not true, then 'Necessarily, no C is A' would be true, and (every B is C necessarily) so (necessarily no B is A) and its converse (necessarily no A is B) while (necessarily every A is B) which is a contradiction. And this conjunction, although it shares with the first in quantity and does not differ from it except in quality, its conclusion does not have to be universal like the first, nor its conclusion the first.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents the first two productive conjunctions under the category of absolute necessary modal propositions. The author first classifies conjunctions into modal, absolute, and mixed; within modal, into necessary and possible; within necessary, into absolute and conditional. He then focuses on absolute necessary. The first conjunction consists of two universal premises: the minor is negative, the major affirmative, producing a universal negative conclusion. The example uses terms A, B, C: necessarily no B is C, necessarily every A is B, therefore necessarily no C is A. The proof proceeds by subsumption: A is under B, B is separate from all C, so A is separate from all C necessarily. The author also provides a conversion proof: swapping premises yields the second conjunction of the first figure (which would be universal negative), and then converse of the conclusion gives the same result. A reductio argument is given: if the conclusion were false, then some C is possibly A, but then since every A is necessarily B, some C would be necessarily B, contradicting the first premise's converse (no B is C). The second conjunction is from two universal affirmatives, producing a particular affirmative conclusion in general possibility. Example: necessarily every B is C, necessarily every A is B, so in general possibility some C is A. The proof: since C is necessary for every B and B for every A, C is necessary for every A, and conversion from universal affirmative to particular (aks) yields the conclusion. The reductio: if it were false that some C is possibly A, then necessarily no C is A, and with every B necessarily C, we get necessarily no B is A, converted to necessarily no A is B, contradicting the second premise. The author notes that despite resembling the first in quantity (both universal), the difference in quality (affirmative vs negative) forces the conclusion to be particular affirmative rather than universal, explaining that the conclusion of the second is not universal like the first.

إمكان عام

Third Mood: Two Affirmatives with a Particular Major
Author defines and proves the third mood of two affirmative premises with a particular major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third mood of two affirmative premises with a particular major, state its conclusion's modality, and reproduce its proof by supposition using categorical syllogisms.

Core Idea
A particular major weakens the conclusion from necessary to possible; the proof by supposition reveals its validity.

In previous lessons, we encountered moods with universal major premises. Now, the third mood introduces a particular major premise, which changes the modality of the conclusion from necessary to possible. The author provides an example and a clever proof using the method of supposition (al-iftirāḍ), which converts the particular premise into a universal through a hypothetical individual. Let's unpack this mood step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Look at the example: (necessarily every B is C) and (necessarily some A is B). The conclusion is (by general possibility some C is A). Note that the major premise is 'some A is B' (particular affirmative) and the minor is 'every B is C' (universal affirmative).
The major contains the predicate of the conclusion (A), and the minor contains the subject (C).
2
Understand the modality of the conclusion
The conclusion is qualified by 'general possibility' (bi-al-imkān al-‘āmm). Why not necessary? Because the major premise is particular. Compare with the previous mood where both premises were universal and the conclusion was necessary.
3
Trace the proof by supposition
The author offers an alternative proof: specify a part of A that is B and call it D. Then deduce: every D is A (since D is part of A) and every D is B (by definition). Both are necessary because the original premises are necessary.
This method is called al-iftirāḍ (supposition) and is similar to ecthesis in Aristotelian logic.
4
Use the original minor to produce an intermediate conclusion
Take 'every D is B' as minor and the original minor 'every B is C' as major. From the first figure, first mood (Barbara), deduce 'every D is C' (necessary).
The original minor is 'every B is C', which is the major in this sub-syllogism.
5
Combine the implications to get the desired conclusion
Now we have 'every D is A' and 'every D is C'. These two universal affirmatives in the third figure produce 'some C is A'. Although the premises are necessary, the conclusion is qualified by 'general possibility' because the original major was particular; the proof shows the mood is valid.
The final step uses the first mood of the third figure (Darapti) to yield the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الثالث من موجبتين كبراهما جزئية ونتيجته كنتيجة الاقتران الذي قبله كقولنا: (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") و(بالضرورة بعض "أ" "ب") فـ (بالإمكان العام بعض "ج " "أ") والبيان لذلك كبيان ما قبله وإن شئتَ بيّنت بالافتراض وعينتَ البعض من "أ" الذي هو "ب" شيئاً معيناً وليكن مثلاً "د" فيلزمه كل "د" "أ" بالضرورة) و(كل "د" "ب" بالضرورة) فإذا جعل كل "د" "ب") صغرى للصغرى أنتج من الاقتران الأول من الشكل الأول (كل "د" "ج" بالضرورة) فإذا أخذ هذا اللازم وجعل كبرى اللازم الآخر أنتج من الاقتران الأول من الشكل الثالث عين المطلوب.
The third conjunction of two affirmative [premises whose] major is particular, and its result is like the result of the conjunction before it, as in our saying: (necessarily every B is C) and (necessarily some A is B) so (by general possibility some C is A). And the demonstration of that is like the demonstration of what preceded it; and if you wish you may demonstrate by supposition, and specify the part of A which is B as a specific thing, let it be for example D. So it follows that (every D is A necessarily) and (every D is B necessarily). Then if you make (every D is B) a minor premise for the [original] minor, it produces from the first conjunction of the first figure (every D is C necessarily). Then if you take this implication and make it a major premise for the other implication, it produces from the first conjunction of the third figure the same as the desired.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage defines and proves the third syllogistic mood, which consists of two affirmative premises where the major premise is particular. The conclusion is a particular affirmative with general possibility, matching the conclusion of the previous mood. The author provides an example: necessarily every B is C, necessarily some A is B, therefore by general possibility some C is A. The proof is similar to the preceding mood, but an alternative proof by supposition is given: take a specific individual D that is a part of A that is B. Then necessarily every D is A, and necessarily every D is B. Using (every D is B) as a minor premise with the original major (every B is C) in first figure, we get necessarily every D is C. Then combining (every D is A) and (every D is C) in the first mood of the third figure yields the required conclusion: some C is A (in possibility). This demonstrates that the mood is valid.

Fourth Mood of Syllogism: Two Universals with Negative Major
Author defines the fourth mood with two universal premises and a negative major, proving a particular negative conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth mood, construct its proof using conversion, and derive its conclusion via reductio.

Core Idea
A negative major with two universal premises forces a particular negative conclusion.

In this lesson, we examine a specific syllogistic mood where both premises are universal but the major is negative. Unlike the first mood (Barbara) which yields a universal affirmative, this mood yields a particular negative necessary. The author demonstrates its validity through direct reasoning, conversion, and reductio, showing its relationship to other figures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Locate the minor premise: 'necessarily every B is C' (universal affirmative, A), and the major premise: 'necessarily nothing of A is B' (universal negative, E). The conclusion is 'necessarily some C is not A' (particular negative, O).
The terms: B is the middle term, C is the subject of the conclusion, A is the predicate.
2
Understand the direct proof
The author argues: since C applies to every B (minor), and B is negated from every A (major), then any B is incompatible with A. The lowest degree of C is that it includes some B, so that part of C is necessarily not A.
This uses the principle that if all B is C, then some C is B (by conversion of the minor).
3
Follow the conversion proof 1
Convert the minor (every B is C) to 'some C is B' (particular affirmative). Combine with the major (no A is B) to form a syllogism in the second figure (Camestres or Cesare?) yielding the same conclusion.
The text says it returns to 'the third conjunction of the second figure' – which is likely a mood with universal negative major and particular affirmative minor.
4
Follow the conversion proof 2
Convert the major (no A is B) to 'no B is A' (universal negative, simple conversion). Combine with the minor (every B is C) to form a syllogism in the third figure (Felapton) yielding the conclusion some C is not A.
The text says it returns to 'the second conjunction of the third figure' – Felapton is EIO in third figure.
5
Analyze the reductio proof
Assume the conclusion is false: 'necessarily some C is not A' is false, so 'possibly every C is A' is true (in the general sense). With the major (no A is B), we derive 'no C is B' by transitivity, which converts to 'no B is C', contradicting the minor 'every B is C'.
This reductio shows the necessity of the particular negative conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الرابع من كليتين والكبرى سالبة ونتيجته جزئية سالبة بالضرورة، كقولنا: (بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") و(بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ب") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ج" ليس "أ") لأن "ج" ضروري لكل "ب" و"ب" مسلوب بالضرورة عن كل "أ" وأدنى درجات "ج" أن يكون بعض "ب" فبعض "ج" مسلوب عن كل "أ" بالضرورة، فإن ذلك البعض المفروض منه "ب" و"ب" مباينة لكل "أ" بالضرورة، وإن عكست الصغرى عاد إلى الاقتران الثالث من الثاني ناتجاً عين المطلوب، وإن عكست الكبرى منه عاد إلى الاقتران الثاني من الثالث ناتجاً عين المطلوب، ولأنه لو لم يصدق بالضرورة بعض "ج" ليس هو "أ") وإلا صدق ممكن بالمعنى العام (كل) "ج" "أ") وكان (بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ب") فـ (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ج" "ب") وعكسه (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ب" "ج") وكان بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") خلف، وهذا الاقتران وإن وافق الأول في كمية المقدمات وكيفيتها وخالفه في كمية النتيجة، فليس إلا بسبب العكس، إذ هو في الأول برده إلى ما ينتج الكلي، وهو في هذا إنما يردّه إلى ما ينتج الجزئي كما علم.
The fourth conjunction is of two universals and the major is negative, and its result is a particular negative necessarily, as in our saying: (necessarily every B is C) and (necessarily nothing of A is B) so (necessarily some C is not A). Because C is necessary for every B, and B is negated necessarily from every A, and the lowest degree of C is that it is some B, so some C is negated from every A necessarily. For that assumed part is from B, and B is incompatible with every A necessarily. And if you convert the minor, it returns to the third conjunction of the second [figure] producing the same required; and if you convert the major from it, it returns to the second conjunction of the third [figure] producing the same required. And because if it were not true that necessarily some C is not A, then it would be true as possible in the general sense that every C is A, and (necessarily nothing of A is B) so (necessarily nothing of C is B), and its converse (necessarily nothing of B is C), and it would be necessarily every B is C [but that contradicts], a reductio. And this conjunction, although it agrees with the first in the quantity and quality of the premises and differs in the quantity of the result, it is only because of conversion; for in the first it is reduced to that which produces the universal, and in this it is reduced to that which produces the particular, as known.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth mood: two universal premises with a negative major, yielding a particular negative necessary conclusion. The example is: necessarily every B is C, necessarily no A is B, therefore necessarily some C is not A. The proof proceeds by noting that C applies to every B, and B is negated from every A; the lowest degree of C is that it applies to some B (since the major is universal affirmative). That part of C is B, and B is incompatible with all A, so some C is not A necessarily. Alternative proofs via conversion: converting the minor premise yields the third mood of the second figure, converting the major yields the second mood of the third figure. A reductio proof is also given: if the conclusion were false, then possibly all C is A, but with the major (no A is B) we get no C is B, which converts to no B is C, contradicting the minor (all B is C). The mood differs from the first (which produces a universal conclusion) only because of conversion, which reduces to a particular conclusion.

Fifth Mood: Particular Minor Affirmative, Universal Major Negative
Author defines the fifth mood and proves its necessary conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the fifth mood of the categorical syllogism (particular affirmative minor, universal negative major) and validate it using at least one proof method.

Core Idea
A particular affirmative minor with a universal negative major yields a particular negative conclusion necessarily.

In this passage, the author presents the fifth mood of the categorical syllogism and demonstrates its validity through multiple rigorous proofs. This mood is essential in Islamic theology and jurisprudence when reasoning from a partial affirmation combined with a universal negation. You will learn to identify this mood's structure and verify its necessary conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises and conclusion
Locate the minor premise (particular affirmative: some B is C), the major premise (universal negative: no A is B), and the conclusion (particular negative: some C is not A). Note the order and quantities.
The minor premise is about 'some B' and the major is about 'all A' in relation to B.
2
Determine the figure and mood
The middle term 'B' is the predicate in both premises, so this is a second-figure syllogism. The mood is Festino (EIO) if we use standard Latin names, but the author calls it the fifth mood.
In the second figure, the middle term is predicate in both premises.
3
Check validity using the categorical rules
Apply the rules: the middle term is distributed in the major premise (universal negative), the minor premise is particular, and no term is used more extensively in the conclusion. The mood is valid.
The conclusion follows necessarily because some C is B and no B is A, so that part of C is not A.
4
Examine the conversion proof
The author shows that converting the minor (some C is B to some B is C) yields a syllogism of the second figure, third mood, and converting the major (no A is B to no B is A) yields a syllogism of the third figure. Confirm these transformations.
Conversion proofs demonstrate the interrelation of moods.
5
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
Assume the conclusion is false (some C is not A is false, so all C is A). From the premises, derive a contradiction: all B is C and no C is B, which is impossible. This confirms the conclusion's necessity.
Reductio is a powerful tool for validating necessary conclusions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الخامس من جزئية صغرى موجبة وكليّة كبرى سالبة ونتيجته جزئية سالبة بالضرورة، كقولنا : بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") و(بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ب") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ج" ليس ("أ") لأنه لما كان "ج" محكوماً به على بعض "ب" فبعض ما هو "ج" هو "ب" وكل "ب" مباين لكل "أ" بالضرورة فبعض "ج" مباين لكل "أ" بالضرورة ، ولأنك إن عكست الصغرى منه عاد إلى الاقتران الثالث من الثاني ناتجاً عين المطلوب، وإن عكست الكبرى منه عاد إلى الاقتران السادس الثالث ناتجاً عين المطلوب، ولأنه لو لم يصدق بالضرورة بعض "ج" ليس بالإمكان العام لصدق بالضرورة بعض "ج" ليس "أ" بالإمكان العام لصدق كل "ج" "أ") وكان (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ج" "ب") وعكسه (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ج" ) فكان بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") وهو خلف، ولأنا إذا فرضنا البعض من ب" الذي هو "ج" شيئاً معيناً وليكن مثلاً "د" فيلزمه كل "د" "ب" بالضرورة) و(كل "د" "ج" فإذا جعلنا (كـل "د" "ب" صغرى للكبرى أنتج من الاقتران الأول من الشكل الثاني بالضرورة لا شيء من "د" "أ") فإذا جعلنا هذا لازم كبرى للازم الآخر أنتج من الاقتران الرابع من الشكل الثالث عين المطلوب، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى حكم ما بقي من القرائن الصرفة..
The fifth conjunction is of a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, and its result is a particular negative necessarily, as in our saying: (necessarily some B is C) and (necessarily nothing of A is B) so (necessarily some C is not A). Because since C is predicated of some B, then some of what is C is B, and every B is incompatible with every A necessarily, so some C is incompatible with every A necessarily. And because if you convert the minor from it, it returns to the third conjunction of the second [figure] producing the same required; and if you convert the major from it, it returns to the sixth? third conjunction of the third [figure]? producing the same required. And because if it were not true that necessarily some C is not A, then by general possibility it would be true that some C is not A? Actually: if it were not true that necessarily some C is not A, then by general possibility it would be true that every C is A, and (necessarily nothing of C is B) and its converse (necessarily nothing of C is ?) so it would be necessarily every B is C, which is a contradiction. And because if we suppose the part of B which is C as a specific thing, let it be for example D, then it follows that (every D is B necessarily) and (every D is C); then if we make (every D is B) a minor for the major, it produces from the first conjunction of the second figure: necessarily nothing of D is A. Then if we make this implication a major for the other implication, it produces from the fourth conjunction of the third figure the same required. And on this, the ruling for what remains of the pure moods is not hidden.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth mood: a particular affirmative minor premise and a universal negative major premise yield a particular negative necessary conclusion. Example: necessarily some B is C, necessarily no A is B, therefore necessarily some C is not A. The proof is multiple: 1) Since C is predicated of some B, that part of C is B; and every B is incompatible with A, so that part of C is incompatible with A. 2) Conversion proofs: converting the minor gives third mood of second figure; converting the major gives (perhaps) sixth mood of third figure (there is an OCR uncertainty). 3) Reductio: if the conclusion were false, then either it is possible that all C is A, leading to a contradiction. 4) Proof by supposition: take a specific D that is the part of B which is C; then it follows that no D is A (via second figure), and combining with D is C in third figure yields the conclusion. The author notes that this method covers all remaining pure moods.

Rules for Mixed Moods in Categorical Syllogisms
Author explains rules for mixed moods in categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules for determining the conclusion in categorical syllogisms with mixed premises (modal and non-modal).

Core Idea
In mixed moods, the conclusion's quantity depends on which premise is negative and its convertibility.

This lesson explains the rules for syllogisms where premises may mix necessity and possibility. Based on principles of immediate inference, the author shows how to derive the correct conclusion for both affirmative and negative mixed cases. Understanding these rules is essential for evaluating arguments in kalam and fiqh that involve modal claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify premises and their modality
Determine each premise's quantity, quality, and modality (necessary, possible, or non-modal). The rules apply when premises mix modalities.
Modality may be explicit (e.g., 'necessarily', 'possibly') or implicit.
2
Check if both premises are affirmative
If both are affirmative, the conclusion is a particular affirmative proposition in general possibility (al-imkan al-'amm), regardless of the specific mood.
This rule is a departure from non-modal categorical logic, where two universals yield a universal.
3
If one premise is universal negative, assess convertibility
Universal negatives (E) are convertible (al-sawālib al-mun'akisa); particular negatives (O) are not. If the negative is of the non-convertible sort, no conclusion is produced.
Conversion: 'No S is P' becomes 'No P is S'.
4
Determine conclusion based on position of negative
If the convertible negative is the minor premise, the conclusion is universal negative; if it is the major premise, the conclusion is particular negative.
This mirrors the rules from the 'necessary moods' (al-ḍarūriyyāt).
5
Verify with a concrete example
Test the rules by constructing a mixed mood syllogism from kalam or fiqh and deriving the conclusion step by step.
Ensure the example respects the conditions of modality and conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت القرائن ،مختلطة، فإن كان الاقتران من موجبتين فالنتيجة جزئية موجبة بالإمكان العام كيف ما كان الاقتران على ما سبق في الضروريات، وإن كانت إحدى المقدمتين كليّة سالبة فإن كانت من السوالب المنعكسة فالنتيجة تابعة لها، وإن كانت السالبة صغرى فالنتيجة كليّة، وإن كانت كبرى فالنتيجة جزئية، كما سبق في الضروريات، وإن كانت السالبة مما لا تنعكس فلا إنتاج، واعتبره.
And if the premises are mixed, then if the conjunction is of two affirmatives, the result is a particular affirmative by general possibility, however the conjunction is, as preceded in the necessary [moods]. And if one of the two premises is a universal negative, then if it is of the convertible negatives, the result follows it; and if the negative is the minor, the result is universal; and if it is the major, the result is particular, as preceded in the necessary [moods]. And if the negative is of the non-convertible sort, then there is no production. And consider it.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage discusses mixed moods, where one premise may be necessary and the other possible, or other combinations. The author summarizes: if both premises are affirmative, the conclusion is a particular affirmative in general possibility, regardless of the specific mood, as established in the necessary moods. If one premise is a universal negative, the type of conversion matters. If the negative is convertible (i.e., a universal negative converts to a universal negative, and a particular negative does not convert), then the conclusion follows the negativity: if the negative is the minor premise, the conclusion is universal (negative); if it is the major, the conclusion is particular (negative). If the negative is not convertible (like a particular negative), no conclusion is produced. The author instructs the reader to reflect on this rule.

Key Terms
القرائن المختلطة
السوالب المنعكسة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In mixed moods, if one premise is a universal negative and it is the minor, what is the conclusion?
A) Particular affirmative
B) Universal negative
C) Particular negative
D) No conclusion

Key Takeaway
In mixed moods, the conclusion's quantity is determined by the position and convertibility of any universal negative premise.

?
Construct a mixed mood categorical syllogism with one necessary and one possible premise, both affirmative. Determine the conclusion using the rule from this passage.

Identifying the Fifth Mixed Syllogism
Defining and proving a modal syllogism mood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the premises and conclusion of the fifth mixed modal syllogism with necessary premises.

Core Idea
A particular affirmative necessary minor and a universal negative necessary major yield a particular negative necessary conclusion.

This passage defines a specific mood of modal categorical syllogism in the first figure. You will learn to recognize this mood and understand its direct proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the minor premise
Find the particular affirmative necessary proposition: 'Necessarily, some B is C'. It is called the minor because it contains the minor extreme (C) and the middle term (B).
In the source example: 'Necessarily, some bodies are originated'.
2
Identify the major premise
Find the universal negative necessary proposition: 'Necessarily, no A is B'. It contains the major extreme (A) and the middle term (B).
Example: 'Necessarily, no eternal being is a body'.
3
State the conclusion
The conclusion is 'Necessarily, some C is not A'. It is a particular negative necessary proposition.
Thus: 'Necessarily, some originated things are not eternal'.
4
Understand the direct proof
Since some B is C, there exists something that is both B and C. Every B is necessarily distinct from A (from the major). Therefore, that something is C and not A, so some C is not A necessarily.
This uses the first-figure valid mood Ferio with modal necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الخامس من جزئية صغرى موجبة وكليّة كبرى سالبة ونتيجته جزئية سالبة بالضرورة، كقولنا : بالضرورة بعض "ب" "ج") و(بالضرورة لا شيء من "أ" "ب") فـ (بالضرورة بعض "ج" ليس ("أ") لأنه لما كان "ج" محكوماً به على بعض "ب" فبعض ما هو "ج" هو "ب" وكل "ب" مباين لكل "أ" بالضرورة فبعض "ج" مباين لكل "أ" بالضرورة ، ولأنك إن عكست الصغرى منه عاد إلى الاقتران الثالث من الثاني ناتجاً عين المطلوب، وإن عكست الكبرى منه عاد إلى الاقتران السادس الثالث ناتجاً عين المطلوب، ولأنه لو لم يصدق بالضرورة بعض "ج" ليس بالإمكان العام لصدق بالضرورة بعض "ج" ليس "أ" بالإمكان العام لصدق كل "ج" "أ") وكان (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ج" "ب") وعكسه (بالضرورة لا شيء من "ج") فكان بالضرورة كل "ب" "ج") وهو خلف، ولأنا إذا فرضنا البعض من ب" الذي هو "ج" شيئاً معيناً وليكن مثلاً "د" فيلزمه كل "د" "ب" بالضرورة) و(كل "د" "ج" فإذا جعلنا (كـل "د" "ب" صغرى للكبرى أنتج من الاقتران الأول من الشكل الثاني بالضرورة لا شيء من "د" "أ") فإذا جعلنا هذا لازم كبرى للازم الآخر أنتج من الاقتران الرابع من الشكل الثالث عين المطلوب، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى حكم ما بقي من القرائن الصرفة.
The fifth syllogism: from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, its conclusion is a particular negative necessarily, like our saying: 'Necessarily, some B is C' and 'Necessarily, no A is B' so 'Necessarily, some C is not A'. Because since C is predicated of some B, then some of what is C is B, and every B is distinct from every A necessarily, so some C is distinct from every A necessarily. And because if you convert the minor from it, it returns to the third syllogism of the second [figure] producing the same sought; and if you convert the major from it, it returns to the sixth syllogism of the third [figure] producing the same sought. And because if it were not true that necessarily some C is not, by general possibility [i.e., if it were possible that all C is A], then [it would be true that] necessarily some C is not A by general possibility [i.e., possible that some C is not A]? [Actually: if it were not true that necessarily some C is not A, then] for truth of all C is A, [and] it would be [that] necessarily no C is B, and its converse [would be] necessarily no [B] is [C?] so [it would be] necessarily every B is C, which is a contradiction. And because if we suppose that some of B which is C is a definite thing, for example D, then it follows that necessarily every D is B, and every D is C. Then if we make 'every D is B' a minor for the major [i.e., 'no A is B'], it produces from the first syllogism of the second figure 'necessarily, no D is A'. Then if we make this a necessary major for the other necessary [minor?], it produces from the fourth syllogism of the third figure exactly the sought. And upon this, the ruling of what remains of the pure modes is not hidden.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage defines and proves a specific mood of modal categorical syllogism, here called the 'fifth syllogism' (al-iqtiran al-khamis). The author states the mood: a necessary particular affirmative minor (some B is necessarily C) and a necessary universal negative major (no A is necessarily B) yield a necessary particular negative conclusion (some C is not necessarily A). He then provides multiple proofs. The first proof is direct: since C is predicated of some B, there is some C that is B. Every B is necessarily distinct (mubayin) from every A, so that some C is necessarily distinct from every A, i.e., not A. The second proof uses conversion: converting the minor (some B is C becomes some C is B) yields a mood in the second figure that gives the desired conclusion; converting the major (no A is B becomes no B is A) yields a mood in the third figure that also gives the same conclusion. The third proof is a reduction ad absurdum: assume the conclusion is false, i.e., it is not necessarily true that some C is not A, but rather it is possible that all C is A. Then together with the major premise (no A is B necessarily) we would get that no C is B necessarily (by a mood?), and its converse would give every B is C necessarily, contradicting the minor (some B is C, but not all). The fourth proof uses an arbitrary constant D to represent the part of B that is C, then using the second figure a universal negative is derived about D, and then the third figure yields the desired conclusion. The author concludes that the same reasoning applies to the remaining pure modes (al-qara'in al-sirfa). This is a typical detailed exposition of modal syllogistic in the style of Ibn Sina's influence.

Key Terms
قرائن صرفة (qara'in sirfa): Pure modes: syllogisms where both premises are of the same modal strength (e.g., both necessary). [Note: In earlier logic, 'qara'in' refers broadly to premises; here it specifically denotes modal premises in the first figure.]
اقتران (iqtiran): A categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani) in the first figure; here specifically a mood with two premises. [Note: In later texts, iqtiran often refers to the first figure alone, but here it is used generically for a syllogism.]
جزئية (juz'iyya): Particular proposition (some S is P). [Note: Stable term.]
كليّة (kulliyya): Universal proposition (every S is P / no S is P). [Note: Stable term.]
بالضرورة (bi-d-darura): Modal qualifier indicating necessity (the proposition is necessarily true). [Note: In earlier Aristotelian logic, 'necessary' often means eternal; in Islamic modal logic it is a temporal modality.]
بالإمكان العام (bi-l-imkan al-'amm): General possibility: the proposition is possibly true in a general sense (contingent). [Note: Distinct from 'imkan al-khaṣṣ' (particular possibility) which is a later distinction.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the fifth syllogism described, if the minor premise is 'necessarily some B is C' and the major is 'necessarily no A is B', what is the conclusion?
A) Necessarily some C is not A
B) Necessarily all C is A
C) Possibly some C is not A
D) Necessarily no C is B

Key Takeaway
The fifth mixed syllogism in the first figure with necessary premises yields a particular negative necessary conclusion, proven directly by the dictum de omni et nullo.

Alternative Validations of the Fifth Syllogism
Author provides alternative proofs for the same modal mood

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to validate the fifth modal syllogism using conversion, reductio ad absurdum, and the constant method.

Core Idea
Three indirect proofs confirm the same conclusion, showing the logical robustness of modal syllogisms.

Beyond the direct proof, the author offers three more ways to derive the conclusion: by converting premises to other figures, by contradiction, and by selecting a representative individual. Each method illuminates a different aspect of the syllogism's validity.

Guided Path
1
Proof by converting the minor premise
Convert the minor 'some B is C' to 'some C is B'. With the unchanged major 'no A is B', this yields a syllogism in the second figure (Festino) which produces the same conclusion 'some C is not A'.
This shows the conclusion can be derived via a different figure.
2
Proof by converting the major premise
Convert the major 'no A is B' to 'no B is A'. With the unchanged minor 'some B is C', this yields a syllogism in the third figure (Ferison) which also produces the same conclusion.
Again, the reasoning is valid.
3
Proof by reductio ad absurdum
Assume the conclusion is false, i.e., it is necessarily false that some C is not A, meaning it is necessarily true that all C is A. From this and the major 'no A is B necessarily', derive that no C is B necessarily. Convert this to get 'every B is C necessarily', which contradicts the minor (which says only some B is C). Hence, the assumption is false, so the conclusion must be true.
This is a classic proof by contradiction.
4
Proof by the constant method (instantiation)
Pick an arbitrary individual D that is both B and C (exists from the minor). From the major, we get 'no D is A necessarily' via a second-figure syllogism (Camestres). Then, since every D is C, combine with 'no D is A' to get 'some C is not A' via a third-figure syllogism (Felapton or Ferison).
This method uses a representative sample to generalize.

Rules for Mixed Modal Syllogisms
Author classifies mixed modal syllogisms based on premise forms and convertibility of negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a conclusion follows in a mixed modal syllogism and classify the conclusion's quantity, quality, and modality based on the premises.

Core Idea
Mixed modal syllogisms: two affirmatives yield a possible particular; a negative works only if convertible.

In the study of modal logic within the Islamic tradition, syllogisms often mix premises of different modalities. This passage from Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq provides clear rules for such 'mixed modes' (qara'in mukhtalita). You will learn how to handle cases where premises are affirmative or negative, and crucially, how the convertibility of a negative premise determines whether a conclusion is produced. This knowledge is essential for evaluating theological proofs that blend necessary and contingent claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify if the syllogism is mixed modal
Check that the two premises have different modal strengths (e.g., one necessary, one possible). If both are the same modality, these specific rules do not apply.
Mixed modalities are indicated by terms like 'necessarily' and 'possibly' in the premises.
2
Check if both premises are affirmative
If both are affirmative, the conclusion is always a particular affirmative in the general possible mode (bi-l-imkan al-'amm), regardless of whether the premises are universal or particular.
This rule mirrors the non-modal case for two affirmatives, but the modality shifts to possibility.
3
If one premise is universal negative, test convertibility
Determine whether the negative premise is 'among the convertible negatives' (as-sawalib al-mun'akisa). If convertible, the conclusion follows that premise: if the negative is the minor, the conclusion is universal; if it is the major, the conclusion is particular. If not convertible, no conclusion follows.
Convertible negatives here are universal negatives that remain true after obversion in modal contexts.
4
Apply the rule to a concrete example
Work through an example from kalam: 'Every accident is necessarily temporally originated; Some substance is possibly an accident; therefore, some substance is possibly temporally originated.' This is two affirmatives, so the conclusion is particular affirmative possible.
Try constructing your own mixed syllogism and determine the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت القرائن مختلطة، فإن كان الاقتران من موجبتين فالنتيجة جزئية موجبة بالإمكان العام كيف ما كان الاقتران على ما سبق في الضروريات، وإن كانت إحدى المقدمتين كليّة سالبة فإن كانت من السوالب المنعكسة فالنتيجة تابعة لها، وإن كانت السالبة صغرى فالنتيجة كليّة، وإن كانت كبرى فالنتيجة جزئية، كما سبق في الضروريات، وإن كانت السالبة مما لا تنعكس فلا إنتاج، واعتبره.
And as for if the modes are mixed: if the syllogism is from two affirmatives, then the conclusion is particular affirmative by general possibility, regardless of how the syllogism is, as preceded in the necessary [modes]. And if one of the two premises is universal negative: if it is among the convertible negatives, then the conclusion follows it; if the negative is minor, then the conclusion is universal; if it is major, then the conclusion is particular, as preceded in the necessary [modes]. And if the negative is among those that do not convert, then no production. And consider it.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage provides rules for mixed modal syllogisms (qara'in mukhtalita), where one premise is necessary and the other is possible (lāzim? Actually 'mixed' here likely means one necessary and one possible, as opposed to pure modes where both are same modality). The author states four cases: (1) If both premises are affirmative, the conclusion is always a particular affirmative in the general possible mode (bi-l-imkan al-'amm), regardless of the quantity (i.e., even if one or both are universal, the conclusion is particular). This mirrors the rule for pure necessary syllogisms where two affirmatives gave a particular affirmative. (2) If one premise is universal negative, then the conclusion's quality and quantity depend on whether the negative is convertible (mun'akisa). Convertible negatives are those that allow obversion (e.g., 'no A is B' converts to 'no B is A'). If the negative is convertible, the conclusion follows the negative premise: if the negative is the minor, the conclusion is universal negative; if the negative is the major, the conclusion is particular negative. (3) If the negative is not convertible (e.g., indefinite or privative negatives that don't convert), then no conclusion is produced. The author instructs to examine this carefully. This is a typical medieval Islamic logical analysis of modal syllogisms, extending Aristotle's non-modal theory.

Key Terms
قرائن مختلطة
السوالب المنعكسة
لا تنعكس
بالإمكان العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a mixed modal syllogism where one premise is a universal negative that is not convertible, what is the result?
A) A conclusion follows in the particular negative
B) A conclusion follows in the universal negative
C) No conclusion is produced
D) A conclusion follows in the particular affirmative

Key Takeaway
In mixed modal syllogisms, two affirmatives always yield a particular affirmative possible; a negative premise only yields a conclusion if it is convertible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a mixed modal syllogism from kalam where one premise is universal negative (convertible) and determine the conclusion's quantity and quality. For example: 'No eternal thing is temporally originated (necessary). Every accident is possibly eternal. Therefore, no accident is temporally originated?' Check if this is valid.
?
Identify whether the following negative premise is convertible in a modal context: 'Some human is not necessarily rational' (particular negative). Explain why no conclusion follows.

Agreements and Differences Among the Four Figures
Author enumerates agreements and differences among the four figures of the syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the general rules valid for all four figures of the categorical syllogism and the specific agreements and differences between any two figures.

Core Idea
The four figures share foundational constraints but each has unique logical features determining which conclusions they produce.

This passage is a systematic enumeration of the points of agreement and difference among the four figures of the categorical syllogism (al-shakl al-awwal, al-thani, al-thalith, al-rabi'). The author first lays down the general rules that apply to all figures, then proceeds pairwise through the agreements and finally marks the distinguishing features of each. Mastering this taxonomy is essential for deciding which syllogistic forms are valid in each figure and for recognizing when a conclusion must be reduced to the first figure for certainty.

Guided Path
1
Extract the general rules for all figures
Read the opening lines of the passage. Note the four prohibitions: no two particulars, no two negatives (unless one acts as affirmative), no negative minor with a particular major, and the conclusion always takes the 'inferior' quality and quantity of the premises. These constraints hold across every figure.
These rules limit which premise combinations are allowed in any valid syllogism.
2
Pairwise agreements: first with second and first with third
Identify the specific points where the first figure agrees with the second (universal major, production of universal negative, four moods) and with the third (affirmative minor, production of particular affirmative, conclusion following the major premise except for an exception).
The exception may refer to special moods where the conclusion's modality differs.
3
Pairwise agreements: first with fourth, second with third
Note the agreements: first with fourth (produces three standard propositions, middle term subject in one premise and predicate in the other); second with third (both reduce to the first by conversion, and their conclusions are not certain without proof).
Reduction to the first figure is a common method to verify validity of second and third figure syllogisms.
4
Pairwise agreements: second with fourth, third with fourth
Continue: second with fourth (allow negative minor, produce universal negative, invalid with two absolute/possible premises or mixing, conclusion does not follow the premises directly); third with fourth (produce particular from universal, require at least one universal premise).
The text mentions 'no production from two absolute or two possible premises' – this refers to modal restrictions.
5
Distinguish each figure's unique features
Compile the distinguishing marks: first figure is self-evident, others reduce to it, and it alone yields the universal affirmative; second figure differs in producing only negative conclusions (the text may be ambiguous); third figure requires a universal major, produces universal conclusions, has four moods; fourth figure requires both an affirmative minor and universal major, conclusion follows the major, also four moods.
The second figure's distinction is that it only produces negative conclusions (universal or particular negative).

In the name of God, the Merciful, the Compassionate. Chapter Seven: On verifying what the figures agree upon and what they differ. That by which they agree is that there is no production in them from two particulars, nor from two negatives, unless one of them is in the ruling of the affirmative as previously mentioned, nor from a negative minor premise whose major premise is particular, and that the conclusion in them follows the inferior of the two premises in quantity and quality. And that by which each one of them agrees with the other: the first figure agrees with the second in the condition of the universality of its major premise, and the production of the universal negative, and in its moods being four. It agrees with the third in the condition of the affirmation of its minor premise, and the production of the particular affirmative, and in the conclusion following in it the major premise, except for what is excluded. It agrees with the fourth in the production of the three required propositions, and that the middle term is subject of one of the extremes and predicate of the other. And that by which the second figure agrees with the third: in the return of each of them to the first by conversion, and that the conclusion in them is not certain except by proof. And that by which it agrees with the fourth: in the permissibility of its minor premise being negative and the production of the universal negative, and that there is no production in them from two absolute [premises], nor from two possible [premises], nor from mixing some with some when one of the premises from the fourth is negative, and in the lack of following of the conclusion, so one of the two premises becomes specific. And that by which the third agrees with the fourth: in the production of the particular from the universal and the condition of universality of one of its two premises. As for that by which they differ: that by which the first figure differs from all: with it being evident in production, and the return of the remaining figures to it, and the production of the universal affirmative. That by which the second differs: in the production of the affirmative, and the condition of the affirmation of its minor premise, and the following of the conclusion in it to the modality of the major premise. That by which the third differs: in the condition of the universality of its major premise, and the production of the universal, and that its moods are four. That by which the fourth differs: in the condition of the affirmation of the minor and the universality of its major, and the following of the conclusion to its major, and its moods being four. And that by which the second differs [from the third?]

Commentary
This passage is a systematic enumeration of the points of agreement and difference among the four figures of the categorical syllogism (al-shakl al-awwal, al-thani, al-thalith, al-rabi'). The author first states the general rules that apply to all figures: (1) No syllogism can be composed of two particular premises, (2) nor of two negative premises unless one is equivalent to affirmative, (3) nor of a negative minor with a particular major, and (4) the conclusion always takes the 'inferior' quality and quantity of the premises. Then, the author goes through pairwise agreements: the first figure agrees with the second in requiring a universal major and yielding the universal negative, and both have four valid moods. It agrees with the third in requiring an affirmative minor and yielding the particular affirmative, but with a caveat that the conclusion follows the major's modality except in some exception (referring to second-figure moods? The text is unclear). It agrees with the fourth in yielding three of the standard propositions (universal affirmative? Actually, the fourth figure yields all four but the text says 'three', likely universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative) and in the middle term being subject in one premise and predicate in the other. The second and third figures agree in that they both reduce to the first by conversion, and their conclusions are not certain without proof (i.e., they require reduction). The second figure agrees with the fourth in allowing a negative minor and yielding a universal negative, and in being invalid with two absolute or two possible premises, or mixing absolute and possible if one premise in the fourth is negative—also the conclusion does not follow the premises directly so one premise becomes the specific conclusion? The third agrees with the fourth in producing a particular from a universal and requiring one universal premise. Then, distinctions: The first figure is distinguished by being self-evident in production, all others reduce to it, and it alone yields the universal affirmative. The second figure is distinguished by only producing affirmative conclusions? Actually, 'fi al-ijab' likely means in the production of affirmative? But the second figure yields only negative conclusions. This seems contradictory—perhaps a copyist error or the text means something else. The third figure is distinguished by requiring a universal major, producing universal conclusions, and having exactly four moods. The fourth figure requires both minor affirmative and major universal, conclusion follows major, and also four moods. Finally, the author mentions 'what the second figure differs by' but the text cuts off.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Common Rules and Pairwise Agreements of the Four Figures
Common rules and pairwise agreements of the four figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the rules common to all four figures of categorical syllogism and identify which agreements hold between any two figures.

Core Idea
The four figures share foundational rules but differ in their natural conclusions.

In this passage, the author systematically compares the four figures of categorical syllogism. Before examining their differences, he lays out what they all have in common: no conclusion from two particulars, two negatives, or a negative minor with a particular major. Then he shows how each figure pairs with another in certain conditions. Understanding these agreements helps you see the underlying unity of syllogistic form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three universal prohibitions
Find the rules that apply to all figures: no conclusion from two particulars, two negatives (unless one is effectively affirmative), and a negative minor with a particular major.
These are foundational constraints that every syllogism must respect.
2
Note the conclusion's dependence on the weaker premise
The conclusion follows the 'inferior' premise in quantity and quality.
This means if one premise is particular and the other universal, the conclusion is particular; if one is affirmative and the other negative, the conclusion is negative.
3
Map the pairwise agreements
For each pair (1-2, 1-3, 1-4, 2-3, 2-4, 3-4), extract the specific condition they share (e.g., requiring universal major, producing particular affirmative, etc.).
For example, first and second agree on requiring the major to be universal and producing universal negative; first and third agree on requiring the minor to be affirmative.
4
Count the moods (qarā'in)
Notice how many valid moods each figure has: first 4, second 4, third 6, fourth 4.
The number of moods reflects the valid combinations of premises that yield a conclusion in that figure.
