أعني الكلي السالب والجزئي الموجب والجزئي السالب - إنما يتم تحصيلها بواسطة الحد الأوسط، وهو وإن اتفق إن كانت هيئته من الشكل الأول، فليس يلزم أن لا يكون من غيره، وعند ذلك فحصول المطالب إنما يتم بتحقيق الشكل الذي منه هيئة الحد الأوسط، وليس كل ما كان من الحدود الوسطية على هيئة أحد الأشكال الثلاثة - أعني ما بعد الشكل الأول - مما يمكن القول برده إلى الشكل الأول، حتى إن ما نرى مطلب إلا وحده الأوسط قد يرد إلى الشكل الأول كما في الاقتران الرابع من الثاني والثاني والثالث والخامس من الثالث، بل وهو عائد إليها من اقترانات الشكل الرابع كما سبق، وفي غير ذلك من الاقترانات في بعض المواد التي لا تعكس كما لو كانت المقدمة التي بعكسها يرتد الاقتران من الشكل الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع إلى الأول ممكنة أو مطلقة. ثم وإن أمكن رد جميع الأشكال إلى الأول، فليست مما يخلو عن فائدة لا تحصل من الأول، فإنه ربما كان السابق إلى الفهم منها ما الموضوع فيها موضوع بالطبع، والمحمول محمول بالطبع، ولو عكس الحال بأن جعل المحمول موضوعاً، والموضوع محمولاً، لم يكن ذلك النمط الطبيعي السابق إلى الفهم، وذلك مما قد لا يتم إلا على هيئة الشكل الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع، أما ما لا يتم من غير الشكل الثاني، فكما لو كان مطلوبنا (لا) شيء من النار فلك بواسطة الحرارة، إذ الحرارة على ما هو السابق إلى الفهم واجب أن تكون محمولة والفلك والنار لها موضوعان، وهو أن نقول كل نار حارة ولا شيء من الفلك حار فإنه وهو خارج عن الوضع الطبيعي السابق إلى الفهم. وأما ما لا يتم حصوله على الوضع الطبيعي من غير الشكل الثالث، فكما لو كان
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. The seventh chapter on the investigation of what the figures agree upon and differ in. That which they agree upon is that there is no production from two particulars, nor from two negatives, unless one of them is in the ruling of an affirmative as previously mentioned, nor from a negative minor whose major is particular, and that the conclusion follows the inferior of the two premises in quantity and quality. And that by which each one agrees with another: the first figure agrees with the second in requiring the universality of its major, and producing the universal negative, and in its moods being four; and it agrees with the third in requiring the affirmation of its minor, and producing the particular affirmative, and in the conclusion following the major except in what is excluded; and it agrees with the fourth in producing the three demands, and that the middle term is subject of one extreme and predicate of the other. And that by which the second figure agrees with the third: they both reduce to the first by conversion, and their conclusion is not certain except by explanation; and what agrees with the fourth is the permissibility of its minor being negative and producing the universal negative, and that there is no production in them from two absolute premises, nor from two possible premises, nor mixing some with others when one of the premises from the fourth is negative, and in the lack of following of the conclusion thus one of the two premises is specified. And what the third agrees with the fourth is in producing the particular from the universal and requiring the universality of one of its two premises. As for what they differ in: what distinguishes the first figure from all is that it is obvious in production, and the other figures reduce to it, and it produces the universal affirmative. What distinguishes the second is producing the affirmative, requiring the affirmation of its minor, and the conclusion follows the modality of the major. What distinguishes the third is requiring the universality of its major, producing the universal, and its moods are four. What distinguishes the fourth is requiring the affirmation of the minor and universality of the major, the conclusion follows the major, and its moods are four. What distinguishes the second from the fourth is the condition that its two premises differ in quality, requiring the universality of its major, and that the conclusion is only negative, and its moods are four. What distinguishes the third from the fourth is requiring the affirmation of its minor, and that the conclusion is only particular, and the conclusion follows the modality of its major, and its moods are six. Know that the first figure, although it produces the four demands, is not isolated from the subsequent figures, for the universal demands—namely the universal negative, the particular affirmative, and the particular negative—are only attained by means of the middle term, and even if its form happens to be from the first figure, it does not necessarily mean it is not from another. And in that case, attaining the demands is only completed by realizing the figure from which the form of the middle term comes. Not every middle term that is in the form of one of the three figures—i.e., those after the first figure—can be reduced to the first figure, such that we see no demand except its middle term may reduce to the first figure as in the fourth mood of the second, the second and third and fifth of the third, rather it returns to them from the moods of the fourth as previously, and in other moods in some materials that do not convert, as when the premise whose conversion would reduce the mood from the second, third, or fourth figure to the first is possible or absolute. Then even if it were possible to reduce all figures to the first, they are not devoid of a benefit not obtained from the first, for perhaps what is foremost to the understanding is that the subject is naturally subject and the predicate naturally predicate. And if the situation were reversed by making the predicate subject and the subject predicate, that natural pattern foremost to the understanding would not occur, and that may only be achieved in the form of the second, third, or fourth figure. As for what is not achieved except from the second figure, it is like when our demand is 'Nothing of the fire is the celestial sphere' by means of heat, since heat, according to what is foremost to the understanding, must be predicated, and the celestial sphere and fire are subjects for it, which is to say 'Every fire is hot' and 'Nothing of the celestial sphere is hot' — that is outside the natural position foremost to the understanding. As for what is not attained in the natural position except from the third figure, it is like when...
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the agreement (ittifaq) and disagreement (iftiraq) of the four figures of the categorical syllogism (ashkal al-qiyas al-iqtirani). The author first states the rules common to all figures: no conclusion from two particular premises, no conclusion from two negative premises (unless one is effectively affirmative), no conclusion from a minor negative with a particular major, and that the conclusion follows the weaker premise in quantity and quality. Then he systematically compares each pair of figures. He lists what the first figure shares with the second, third, and fourth respectively. For example, the first shares with the second the requirement that the major be universal and the production of universal negative conclusions, and both have four moods. Then he lists agreements of the second with the third, the second with the fourth, and the third with the fourth. After that, he turns to differences (ma bihi al-iftiraq). He enumerates how the first figure differs from all others: it is evident in production (bayan fi al-intaj), other figures reduce to it, and it produces universal affirmative conclusions. Then he specifies how the second differs from the fourth, and the third from the fourth. Finally, he addresses a key point: the first figure is not always the only natural one. While the first figure can produce all four kinds of conclusions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative), sometimes the natural order of terms (subject-predicate) fits better with another figure. He gives concrete examples: to prove 'Nothing of the celestial sphere is fire' using heat as a middle term, the natural arrangement is to say 'Every fire is hot' and 'Nothing of the celestial sphere is hot', which is a second figure syllogism. In this arrangement, 'heat' is naturally the predicate, and 'fire' and 'celestial sphere' are subjects. If one forced it into the first figure, the natural order would be reversed, making it less intuitive. Thus, the other figures have utility beyond mere reducibility to the first.

Distinguishing Features and the Necessity of All Four Figures
Distinguishing features and necessity of all four figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the four figures based on their unique conditions and argue why a figure other than the first may be necessary for natural reasoning.

Core Idea
Each figure has unique features; the first is not always the most natural, as other figures preserve the intuitive subject-predicate order.

After establishing the common ground, the author turns to what sets each figure apart. He explains how the first figure is unique in being obvious and producing universal affirmatives, while the second, third, and fourth have their own distinctive conditions. More importantly, he argues that the other figures are not mere reductions of the first; they serve a vital purpose when the natural subject-predicate order would be violated by forcing an argument into the first figure.

Guided Path
1
Identify how the first figure differs from all others
The first is 'obvious in production' (bayan fi al-intaj), other figures reduce to it, and it produces universal affirmative conclusions.
This makes the first figure the standard for assessing validity, but not always the most intuitive.
2
List the unique conditions of the second, third, and fourth figures
The second: produces affirmative conclusions, requires affirmative minor, conclusion follows modality of major, has 4 moods. The third: requires universal major, produces universal conclusions, has 4 moods. The fourth: requires affirmative minor and universal major, conclusion follows major, has 4 moods.
The third figure actually has 6 moods according to the author, but the passage lists 4 for others.
3
Analyze the example of the second figure
The author gives: to prove 'Nothing of the celestial sphere is fire' using heat as middle term, the natural order is 'Every fire is hot; nothing of the celestial sphere is hot' – a second figure syllogism. Here heat is naturally predicated of both subjects.
If forced into the first figure, the subject-predicate order would be reversed, making it less intuitive.
4
Generalize the principle
Other figures preserve the natural subject-predicate order when the middle term's natural position is as predicate or subject in a way that doesn't fit the first figure's arrangement.
This shows that figures beyond the first are not merely redundant; they serve a cognitive and pedagogical purpose.

بسم الله الرحمن الرحيم الفصل السابع في تحقيق ما تتفق به الأشكال وتفترق والذي تتفق به أنه لا إنتاج فيها عن جزئيتين، ولا عن سالبتين، إلا أن تكون إحداهما في حكم الموجبة كما سبق، ولا عن سالبة صغرى كبراها جزئية، وأن النتيجة فيها تابعة لأخسّ المقدمتين في الكم والكيف، وما به موافقة كل واحد واحد منها مع الآخر، فالشكل الأول يوافق الثاني في اشتراط كلية كبراه، وإنتاج الكلي السالب وفي قرائنه أربعة، ويوافق الثالث في اشتراط إيجاب صغراه، وإنتاج الجزئي الموجب وفي اتباع النتيجة فيه للكبرى، إلا في ما استثني، ويوافق الرابع في إنتاج المطالب الثلاثة، وأن الحد الأوسط موضوع لأحد الطرفين، ومحمول على الآخر، وما به يوافق الشكل الثاني للثالث، ففي عود كل واحد منهما إلى الأول بالعكس، وأن النتيجة فيهما غير يقينية إلا ببيان وما به يوافق الرابع ففي جواز كون صغراه سالبة وإنتاج الكلي السالب وفي أنه لا إنتاج فيهما عن مطلقتين، ولا عن ممكنتين ولا خلط بعضها مع بعض إذا كانت إحدى المقدمتين من الرابع سالبة وفي عدم اتباع النتيجة فيتعين إحدى المقدمتين وما به يوافق الثالث الرابع ففي إنتاج الجزئي عن الكلي واشتراط كليّة إحدى مقدمتيه، وأما ما به الافتراق فما به يفارق الشكل الأول للجميع مع كونه بينا في الإنتاج، وفي عود باقي الأشكال إليه، وإنتاج الكلي الموجب، وما به يفارق الثاني ففي إنتاج الموجب، واشتراط إيجاب صغراه، واتباع النتيجة فيه لجهة الكبرى، وما به يفارق الثالث ففي اشتراط كليّة كبراه، وإنتاج الكلي وفي أن قرائنه أربعة، وما به يفارق الرابع ففي اشتراط إيجاب الصغرى وكليّة كبراه، واتباع النتيجة لكبراه، وفي قرائنه أربعة، وما به مفارقة الثاني الرابع ففي شرط اختلاف مقدمتيه في الكيف، واشتراط كلية كبراه، وأن النتيجة لا تكون إلا سالبة، وأن قرائنه أربعة، وما به مفارقة الثالث الرابع ففي اشتراط إيجاب صغراه، وأن النتيجة لا تكون إلا جزئية، وفي اتباع النتيجة فيه لجهة كبراه، وأن قرائنه ستة. واعلم أن الشكل الأول، وإن أنتج المطالب الأربعة، فليس بمعزل عما بعده من الأشكال، إذ المطالب الكلية - أعني الكلي السالب والجزئي الموجب والجزئي السالب - إنما يتم تحصيلها بواسطة الحد الأوسط، وهو وإن اتفق إن كانت هيئته من الشكل الأول، فليس يلزم أن لا يكون من غيره، وعند ذلك فحصول المطالب إنما يتم بتحقيق الشكل الذي منه هيئة الحد الأوسط، وليس كل ما كان من الحدود الوسطية على هيئة أحد الأشكال الثلاثة - أعني ما بعد الشكل الأول - مما يمكن القول برده إلى الشكل الأول، حتى إن ما نرى مطلب إلا وحده الأوسط قد يرد إلى الشكل الأول كما في الاقتران الرابع من الثاني والثاني والثالث والخامس من الثالث، بل وهو عائد إليها من اقترانات الشكل الرابع كما سبق، وفي غير ذلك من الاقترانات في بعض المواد التي لا تعكس كما لو كانت المقدمة التي بعكسها يرتد الاقتران من الشكل الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع إلى الأول ممكنة أو مطلقة. ثم وإن أمكن رد جميع الأشكال إلى الأول، فليست مما يخلو عن فائدة لا تحصل من الأول، فإنه ربما كان السابق إلى الفهم منها ما الموضوع فيها موضوع بالطبع، والمحمول محمول بالطبع، ولو عكس الحال بأن جعل المحمول موضوعاً، والموضوع محمولاً، لم يكن ذلك النمط الطبيعي السابق إلى الفهم، وذلك مما قد لا يتم إلا على هيئة الشكل الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع، أما ما لا يتم من غير الشكل الثاني، فكما لو كان مطلوبنا (لا) شيء من النار فلك بواسطة الحرارة، إذ الحرارة على ما هو السابق إلى الفهم واجب أن تكون محمولة والفلك والنار لها موضوعان، وهو أن نقول كل نار حارة ولا شيء من الفلك حار فإنه وهو خارج عن الوضع الطبيعي السابق إلى الفهم. وأما ما لا يتم حصوله على الوضع الطبيعي من غير الشكل الثالث، فكما لو كان
In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. The seventh chapter on the investigation of what the figures agree upon and differ in. That which they agree upon is that there is no production from two particulars, nor from two negatives, unless one of them is in the ruling of an affirmative as previously mentioned, nor from a negative minor whose major is particular, and that the conclusion follows the inferior of the two premises in quantity and quality. And that by which each one agrees with another: the first figure agrees with the second in requiring the universality of its major, and producing the universal negative, and in its moods being four; and it agrees with the third in requiring the affirmation of its minor, and producing the particular affirmative, and in the conclusion following the major except in what is excluded; and it agrees with the fourth in producing the three demands, and that the middle term is subject of one extreme and predicate of the other. And that by which the second figure agrees with the third: they both reduce to the first by conversion, and their conclusion is not certain except by explanation; and what agrees with the fourth is the permissibility of its minor being negative and producing the universal negative, and that there is no production in them from two absolute premises, nor from two possible premises, nor mixing some with others when one of the premises from the fourth is negative, and in the lack of following of the conclusion thus one of the two premises is specified. And what the third agrees with the fourth is in producing the particular from the universal and requiring the universality of one of its two premises. As for what they differ in: what distinguishes the first figure from all is that it is obvious in production, and the other figures reduce to it, and it produces the universal affirmative. What distinguishes the second is producing the affirmative, requiring the affirmation of its minor, and the conclusion follows the modality of the major. What distinguishes the third is requiring the universality of its major, producing the universal, and its moods are four. What distinguishes the fourth is requiring the affirmation of the minor and universality of the major, the conclusion follows the major, and its moods are four. What distinguishes the second from the fourth is the condition that its two premises differ in quality, requiring the universality of its major, and that the conclusion is only negative, and its moods are four. What distinguishes the third from the fourth is requiring the affirmation of its minor, and that the conclusion is only particular, and the conclusion follows the modality of its major, and its moods are six. Know that the first figure, although it produces the four demands, is not isolated from the subsequent figures, for the universal demands—namely the universal negative, the particular affirmative, and the particular negative—are only attained by means of the middle term, and even if its form happens to be from the first figure, it does not necessarily mean it is not from another. And in that case, attaining the demands is only completed by realizing the figure from which the form of the middle term comes. Not every middle term that is in the form of one of the three figures—i.e., those after the first figure—can be reduced to the first figure, such that we see no demand except its middle term may reduce to the first figure as in the fourth mood of the second, the second and third and fifth of the third, rather it returns to them from the moods of the fourth as previously, and in other moods in some materials that do not convert, as when the premise whose conversion would reduce the mood from the second, third, or fourth figure to the first is possible or absolute. Then even if it were possible to reduce all figures to the first, they are not devoid of a benefit not obtained from the first, for perhaps what is foremost to the understanding is that the subject is naturally subject and the predicate naturally predicate. And if the situation were reversed by making the predicate subject and the subject predicate, that natural pattern foremost to the understanding would not occur, and that may only be achieved in the form of the second, third, or fourth figure. As for what is not achieved except from the second figure, it is like when our demand is 'Nothing of the fire is the celestial sphere' by means of heat, since heat, according to what is foremost to the understanding, must be predicated, and the celestial sphere and fire are subjects for it, which is to say 'Every fire is hot' and 'Nothing of the celestial sphere is hot' — that is outside the natural position foremost to the understanding. As for what is not attained in the natural position except from the third figure, it is like when...
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on the agreement (ittifaq) and disagreement (iftiraq) of the four figures of the categorical syllogism (ashkal al-qiyas al-iqtirani). The author first states the rules common to all figures: no conclusion from two particular premises, no conclusion from two negative premises (unless one is effectively affirmative), no conclusion from a minor negative with a particular major, and that the conclusion follows the weaker premise in quantity and quality. Then he systematically compares each pair of figures. He lists what the first figure shares with the second, third, and fourth respectively. For example, the first shares with the second the requirement that the major be universal and the production of universal negative conclusions, and both have four moods. Then he lists agreements of the second with the third, the second with the fourth, and the third with the fourth. After that, he turns to differences (ma bihi al-iftiraq). He enumerates how the first figure differs from all others: it is evident in production (bayan fi al-intaj), other figures reduce to it, and it produces universal affirmative conclusions. Then he specifies how the second differs from the fourth, and the third from the fourth. Finally, he addresses a key point: the first figure is not always the only natural one. While the first figure can produce all four kinds of conclusions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative), sometimes the natural order of terms (subject-predicate) fits better with another figure. He gives concrete examples: to prove 'Nothing of the celestial sphere is fire' using heat as a middle term, the natural arrangement is to say 'Every fire is hot' and 'Nothing of the celestial sphere is hot', which is a second figure syllogism. In this arrangement, 'heat' is naturally the predicate, and 'fire' and 'celestial sphere' are subjects. If one forced it into the first figure, the natural order would be reversed, making it less intuitive. Thus, the other figures have utility beyond mere reducibility to the first.

Key Takeaway
Although the first figure is the most obvious and all others can be reduced to it, the other figures are not redundant because they often preserve the natural subject-predicate order that the first figure would disrupt.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Why the Second Figure Is Necessary
Author argues for the indispensability of the second figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a categorical syllogism that requires the second figure based on the natural order of the middle term as predicate in both premises.

Core Idea
When the middle term is naturally a predicate in both premises, the second figure is necessary.

The author demonstrates that for some conclusions, the natural order demands that the middle term be the predicate in both premises, which is the second figure. We will examine the example of proving that nothing from fire is a celestial sphere using 'heat'. Pay attention to how the terms align.

Guided Path
1
Find the conclusion
The conclusion is 'Nothing from fire is a celestial sphere'. Identify the subject (fire) and predicate (celestial sphere).
The conclusion is universal negative.
2
Identify the middle term
The middle term is 'heat', which connects fire and celestial sphere. In the natural order, heat is understood as a predicate for both fire and celestial sphere.
Heat is an attribute, so it naturally takes predicate position.
3
Construct the premises naturally
Write the premises so that the middle term is predicate in both: 'Every fire is hot' and 'Nothing from the celestial sphere is hot'. This is the second figure (predicate in both premises).
The first premise is universal affirmative, the second universal negative.
4
Check if the first figure would be unnatural
To use the first figure, you would need to invert the order, e.g., make heat the subject. That would violate the natural understanding, confirming the second figure is required.
The first figure requires the middle term to be subject in the major and predicate in the minor.

واعلم أن الشكل الأول، وإن أنتج المطالب الأربعة، فليس بمعزل عما بعده من الأشكال، إذ المطالب الكلية - أعني الكلي السالب والجزئي الموجب والجزئي السالب - إنما يتم تحصيلها بواسطة الحد الأوسط، وهو وإن اتفق إن كانت هيئته من الشكل الأول، فليس يلزم أن لا يكون من غيره، وعند ذلك فحصول المطالب إنما يتم بتحقيق الشكل الذي منه هيئة الحد الأوسط، وليس كل ما كان من الحدود الوسطية على هيئة أحد الأشكال الثلاثة - أعني ما بعد الشكل الأول - مما يمكن القول برده إلى الشكل الأول، حتى إن ما نرى مطلب إلا وحده الأوسط قد يرد إلى الشكل الأول كما في الاقتران الرابع من الثاني والثاني والثالث والخامس من الثالث، بل وهو عائد إليها من اقترانات الشكل الرابع كما سبق، وفي غير ذلك من الاقترانات في بعض المواد التي لا تعكس كما لو كانت المقدمة التي بعكسها يرتد الاقتران من الشكل الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع إلى الأول ممكنة أو مطلقة. ثم وإن أمكن رد جميع الأشكال إلى الأول، فليست مما يخلو عن فائدة لا تحصل من الأول، فإنه ربما كان السابق إلى الفهم منها ما الموضوع فيها موضوع بالطبع، والمحمول محمول بالطبع، ولو عكس الحال بأن جعل المحمول موضوعاً، والموضوع محمولاً، لم يكن ذلك النمط الطبيعي السابق إلى الفهم، وذلك مما قد لا يتم إلا على هيئة الشكل الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع، أما ما لا يتم من غير الشكل الثاني، فكما لو كان مطلوبنا (لا) شيء من النار فلك بواسطة الحرارة، إذ الحرارة على ما هو السابق إلى الفهم واجب أن تكون محمولة والفلك والنار لها موضوعان، وهو أن نقول كل نار حارة ولا شيء من الفلك حار فإنه وهو خارج عن الوضع الطبيعي السابق إلى الفهم. وأما ما لا يتم حصوله على الوضع الطبيعي من غير الشكل الثالث، فكما لو كان مطلوبنا بعض المتحرك (حساس بواسطة الحيوان واللائق بالطبيعي السابق إلى الفهم أن يكون الحيوان موضوعاً للمتحرك والحساس، ولا يتهيأ ذلك على غير نمط الشكل الثالث على الوجه الطبيعي، وهو أن نقول كل حيوان متحرك) و(كل حيوان حساس) فـ بعض المتحرك (حساس) كما عرف ولو أردت استنتاجه من الشكل الأول لما أمكن، إلا أن نقول بعض المتحرك (حيوان) وكل حيوان حساس وهو خارج عن الوضع الطبيعي. وأما ما لا يتم حصوله على النمط الطبيعي من غير الشكل الرابع، فكما لو كان مطلوبنا بعض المتغير فلك بواسطة المتحرك، والمتحرك على ما هو اللائق بالوضع الطبيعي السابق إلى الفهم، يجب أن يكون موضوعاً للمتغير ومحمولاً على الفلك، وذلك إنما يتم على هيئة الشكل الرابع دون الأول والثاني والثالث، وهذا الشكل - أعني الرابع - وإن أهمله المعلم الأول؛ لبعده عن الطباع، وجعل فائدة الترتيب الطبيعي فيه مهملة بالنظر إلى عسر إنتاجه، وتباعده عن الطبع دون الشكلين قبله فالموجب لذكره هاهنا - وإن كان محذور إهماله لبعده عن الطبع - يزيد على محذور تعلمه وتعلق غرض بعض المطالب به والتنبيه على ضعف المستلحق له بعد المعلم الأول عن الوقوف على غوره، ولا الكشف عنه، وعما يتعلق به من بيان الإنتاج، وشروطه، وأحكام الاختلاط، وغير ذلك مما أوضحناه.
Know that the first figure, even though it produces the four conclusions, is not independent of the figures after it. For the universal conclusions—I mean the universal negative, the particular affirmative, and the particular negative—are only obtained through the middle term, and even if it happens that its configuration is from the first figure, it does not necessarily follow that it is not from another. And then the obtaining of the conclusion is only achieved by realizing the figure from which the configuration of the middle term is. And not every case where the middle term is in the configuration of one of the three figures—I mean those after the first figure—can be reduced to the first figure. Indeed, we do not see a conclusion whose middle term cannot be reduced to the first figure, as in the fourth mood of the second, the second and third of the third, and the fifth of the third; rather, it returns to them from the moods of the fourth figure as mentioned. And other moods in some matters that do not convert, as if the premise whose conversion would revert the mood from the second, third, or fourth figure to the first is possible or absolute. Then, even if it were possible to reduce all figures to the first, they are not devoid of a benefit not obtained from the first. For sometimes what is foremost to the understanding is that the subject is naturally subject, and the predicate naturally predicate. If the situation were reversed by making the predicate a subject and the subject a predicate, that natural mode would not be foremost to the understanding. And that may only be accomplished in the configuration of the second, third, or fourth figure. As for what cannot be accomplished except by the second figure: for example, if our demand is 'Nothing from fire is celestial sphere' by means of heat, since heat, as is foremost to the understanding, must be a predicate, and the celestial sphere and fire are its subjects. That is to say 'Every fire is hot' and 'Nothing from the celestial sphere is hot'—then it is outside the natural order foremost to the understanding. As for what cannot be obtained in the natural order except by the third figure: for example, if our demand is 'Some moving things are sentient' by means of 'animal'. And appropriate to the natural order foremost to the understanding is that 'animal' be a subject for 'moving' and 'sentient'. That cannot be prepared except in the pattern of the third figure in the natural way, which is to say 'Every animal is moving' and 'Every animal is sentient', so 'Some moving things are sentient' as known. If you wanted to deduce it from the first figure, it would not be possible unless you said 'Some moving things are animal' and 'Every animal is sentient', which is outside the natural order. As for what cannot be obtained in the natural mode except by the fourth figure: for example, if our demand is 'Some changing things are celestial sphere' by means of 'moving'. And 'moving', as is appropriate to the natural order foremost to the understanding, must be a subject for 'changing' and a predicate for 'celestial sphere'. That is only accomplished in the configuration of the fourth figure, not the first, second, or third. This figure—I mean the fourth—although the First Teacher (Aristotle) neglected it due to its distance from natural disposition, and considered the benefit of its natural arrangement neglected in view of the difficulty of its production, and its distance from nature is greater than the two figures before it. Nevertheless, the reason for mentioning it here—even though the harm of its neglect due to its distance from nature outweighs the harm of learning it—is that some conclusions depend on it, and to alert the weakness of the one who added it after the First Teacher in not grasping its depth, nor uncovering it, nor what pertains to it of the explanation of production, its conditions, the rules of mixing, and other things we have clarified.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage explains why the three figures beyond the first are indispensable, despite the first figure's ability to produce all four types of conclusions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). The author begins by stating that while the first figure can produce all conclusions, it is not sufficient because the middle term may naturally take a configuration that belongs to another figure. Even if all moods could theoretically be reduced to the first figure, that would not eliminate the benefit of the other figures: the natural order of subject and predicate as they come to the mind. The author then gives three examples. For the second figure, the natural order requires the middle term to be a predicate in both premises. The example: to prove that nothing from fire is a celestial sphere using 'heat', the natural arrangement is 'Every fire is hot' and 'Nothing from the celestial sphere is hot'—here heat is the predicate in both, which is the second figure. For the third figure, the middle term is the subject in both premises. The example: to prove that some moving things are sentient using 'animal', the natural arrangement is 'Every animal is moving' and 'Every animal is sentient'—animal is the subject in both, which is the third figure. For the fourth figure, the middle term is the predicate of the minor and subject of the major. The example: to prove that some changing things are celestial spheres using 'moving', the natural arrangement is 'Every moving thing is changing' and 'Every celestial sphere is moving'—here moving is the predicate in the first and subject in the second, which is the fourth figure. The author then justifies including the fourth figure, even though Aristotle neglected it due to its unnaturalness and difficulty, because some conclusions can only be obtained through it, and to alert those who added it without fully understanding its depth.

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism

Why the Third Figure Is Necessary
Author argues for the indispensability of the third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a categorical syllogism that requires the third figure based on the natural order of the middle term as subject in both premises.

Core Idea
When the middle term is naturally a subject in both premises, the third figure is necessary.

The author gives an example where the conclusion 'Some moving things are sentient' naturally requires the third figure. The middle term 'animal' is most naturally a subject for both 'moving' and 'sentient'. Let's walk through this example to see why the third figure is indispensable.

Guided Path
1
Find the conclusion
The conclusion is 'Some moving things are sentient'. Subject: moving things; predicate: sentient.
It is a particular affirmative.
2
Identify the middle term
The middle term is 'animal'. In the natural order, 'animal' is a subject for both 'moving' and 'sentient' because it is a substance and these are attributes.
Substances typically take subject position.
3
Construct the premises naturally
Write the premises with 'animal' as the subject in both: 'Every animal is moving' and 'Every animal is sentient'. This is the third figure (subject in both premises).
Both premises are universal affirmative.
4
Verify that the first figure would be unnatural
To use the first figure, you would need to say 'Some moving things are animal' and 'Every animal is sentient'. Here 'animal' is subject in one and predicate in the other, which disrupts the natural order where 'animal' is primarily a subject.
The first figure requires the middle term to be subject in the major and predicate in the minor.

واعلم أن الشكل الأول، وإن أنتج المطالب الأربعة، فليس بمعزل عما بعده من الأشكال، إذ المطالب الكلية - أعني الكلي السالب والجزئي الموجب والجزئي السالب - إنما يتم تحصيلها بواسطة الحد الأوسط، وهو وإن اتفق إن كانت هيئته من الشكل الأول، فليس يلزم أن لا يكون من غيره، وعند ذلك فحصول المطالب إنما يتم بتحقيق الشكل الذي منه هيئة الحد الأوسط، وليس كل ما كان من الحدود الوسطية على هيئة أحد الأشكال الثلاثة - أعني ما بعد الشكل الأول - مما يمكن القول برده إلى الشكل الأول، حتى إن ما نرى مطلب إلا وحده الأوسط قد يرد إلى الشكل الأول كما في الاقتران الرابع من الثاني والثاني والثالث والخامس من الثالث، بل وهو عائد إليها من اقترانات الشكل الرابع كما سبق، وفي غير ذلك من الاقترانات في بعض المواد التي لا تعكس كما لو كانت المقدمة التي بعكسها يرتد الاقتران من الشكل الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع إلى الأول ممكنة أو مطلقة. ثم وإن أمكن رد جميع الأشكال إلى الأول، فليست مما يخلو عن فائدة لا تحصل من الأول، فإنه ربما كان السابق إلى الفهم منها ما الموضوع فيها موضوع بالطبع، والمحمول محمول بالطبع، ولو عكس الحال بأن جعل المحمول موضوعاً، والموضوع محمولاً، لم يكن ذلك النمط الطبيعي السابق إلى الفهم، وذلك مما قد لا يتم إلا على هيئة الشكل الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع، أما ما لا يتم من غير الشكل الثاني، فكما لو كان مطلوبنا (لا) شيء من النار فلك بواسطة الحرارة، إذ الحرارة على ما هو السابق إلى الفهم واجب أن تكون محمولة والفلك والنار لها موضوعان، وهو أن نقول كل نار حارة ولا شيء من الفلك حار فإنه وهو خارج عن الوضع الطبيعي السابق إلى الفهم. وأما ما لا يتم حصوله على الوضع الطبيعي من غير الشكل الثالث، فكما لو كان مطلوبنا بعض المتحرك (حساس بواسطة الحيوان واللائق بالطبيعي السابق إلى الفهم أن يكون الحيوان موضوعاً للمتحرك والحساس، ولا يتهيأ ذلك على غير نمط الشكل الثالث على الوجه الطبيعي، وهو أن نقول كل حيوان متحرك) و(كل حيوان حساس) فـ بعض المتحرك (حساس) كما عرف ولو أردت استنتاجه من الشكل الأول لما أمكن، إلا أن نقول بعض المتحرك (حيوان) وكل حيوان حساس وهو خارج عن الوضع الطبيعي. وأما ما لا يتم حصوله على النمط الطبيعي من غير الشكل الرابع، فكما لو كان مطلوبنا بعض المتغير فلك بواسطة المتحرك، والمتحرك على ما هو اللائق بالوضع الطبيعي السابق إلى الفهم، يجب أن يكون موضوعاً للمتغير ومحمولاً على الفلك، وذلك إنما يتم على هيئة الشكل الرابع دون الأول والثاني والثالث، وهذا الشكل - أعني الرابع - وإن أهمله المعلم الأول؛ لبعده عن الطباع، وجعل فائدة الترتيب الطبيعي فيه مهملة بالنظر إلى عسر إنتاجه، وتباعده عن الطبع دون الشكلين قبله فالموجب لذكره هاهنا - وإن كان محذور إهماله لبعده عن الطبع - يزيد على محذور تعلمه وتعلق غرض بعض المطالب به والتنبيه على ضعف المستلحق له بعد المعلم الأول عن الوقوف على غوره، ولا الكشف عنه، وعما يتعلق به من بيان الإنتاج، وشروطه، وأحكام الاختلاط، وغير ذلك مما أوضحناه.
Know that the first figure, even though it produces the four conclusions, is not independent of the figures after it. For the universal conclusions—I mean the universal negative, the particular affirmative, and the particular negative—are only obtained through the middle term, and even if it happens that its configuration is from the first figure, it does not necessarily follow that it is not from another. And then the obtaining of the conclusion is only achieved by realizing the figure from which the configuration of the middle term is. And not every case where the middle term is in the configuration of one of the three figures—I mean those after the first figure—can be reduced to the first figure. Indeed, we do not see a conclusion whose middle term cannot be reduced to the first figure, as in the fourth mood of the second, the second and third of the third, and the fifth of the third; rather, it returns to them from the moods of the fourth figure as mentioned. And other moods in some matters that do not convert, as if the premise whose conversion would revert the mood from the second, third, or fourth figure to the first is possible or absolute. Then, even if it were possible to reduce all figures to the first, they are not devoid of a benefit not obtained from the first. For sometimes what is foremost to the understanding is that the subject is naturally subject, and the predicate naturally predicate. If the situation were reversed by making the predicate a subject and the subject a predicate, that natural mode would not be foremost to the understanding. And that may only be accomplished in the configuration of the second, third, or fourth figure. As for what cannot be accomplished except by the second figure: for example, if our demand is 'Nothing from fire is celestial sphere' by means of heat, since heat, as is foremost to the understanding, must be a predicate, and the celestial sphere and fire are its subjects. That is to say 'Every fire is hot' and 'Nothing from the celestial sphere is hot'—then it is outside the natural order foremost to the understanding. As for what cannot be obtained in the natural order except by the third figure: for example, if our demand is 'Some moving things are sentient' by means of 'animal'. And appropriate to the natural order foremost to the understanding is that 'animal' be a subject for 'moving' and 'sentient'. That cannot be prepared except in the pattern of the third figure in the natural way, which is to say 'Every animal is moving' and 'Every animal is sentient', so 'Some moving things are sentient' as known. If you wanted to deduce it from the first figure, it would not be possible unless you said 'Some moving things are animal' and 'Every animal is sentient', which is outside the natural order. As for what cannot be obtained in the natural mode except by the fourth figure: for example, if our demand is 'Some changing things are celestial sphere' by means of 'moving'. And 'moving', as is appropriate to the natural order foremost to the understanding, must be a subject for 'changing' and a predicate for 'celestial sphere'. That is only accomplished in the configuration of the fourth figure, not the first, second, or third. This figure—I mean the fourth—although the First Teacher (Aristotle) neglected it due to its distance from natural disposition, and considered the benefit of its natural arrangement neglected in view of the difficulty of its production, and its distance from nature is greater than the two figures before it. Nevertheless, the reason for mentioning it here—even though the harm of its neglect due to its distance from nature outweighs the harm of learning it—is that some conclusions depend on it, and to alert the weakness of the one who added it after the First Teacher in not grasping its depth, nor uncovering it, nor what pertains to it of the explanation of production, its conditions, the rules of mixing, and other things we have clarified.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage explains why the three figures beyond the first are indispensable, despite the first figure's ability to produce all four types of conclusions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative). The author begins by stating that while the first figure can produce all conclusions, it is not sufficient because the middle term may naturally take a configuration that belongs to another figure. Even if all moods could theoretically be reduced to the first figure, that would not eliminate the benefit of the other figures: the natural order of subject and predicate as they come to the mind. The author then gives three examples. For the second figure, the natural order requires the middle term to be a predicate in both premises. The example: to prove that nothing from fire is a celestial sphere using 'heat', the natural arrangement is 'Every fire is hot' and 'Nothing from the celestial sphere is hot'—here heat is the predicate in both, which is the second figure. For the third figure, the middle term is the subject in both premises. The example: to prove that some moving things are sentient using 'animal', the natural arrangement is 'Every animal is moving' and 'Every animal is sentient'—animal is the subject in both, which is the third figure. For the fourth figure, the middle term is the predicate of the minor and subject of the major. The example: to prove that some changing things are celestial spheres using 'moving', the natural arrangement is 'Every moving thing is changing' and 'Every celestial sphere is moving'—here moving is the predicate in the first and subject in the second, which is the fourth figure. The author then justifies including the fourth figure, even though Aristotle neglected it due to its unnaturalness and difficulty, because some conclusions can only be obtained through it, and to alert those who added it without fully understanding its depth.

Key Terms
الشكل الأول
الحد الأوسط
الوضع الطبيعي
الاقتران

A) Because it is a universal term.
B) Because it is a substance, so it naturally takes subject position.
C) Because the conclusion is a universal affirmative.
D) Because the first figure cannot handle particular affirmatives.

Key Takeaway
The third figure is necessary when the middle term is naturally a subject in both premises, preserving the intuitive order of substance and attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a third-figure syllogism using an Islamic example: 'Every prayer (salat) is a form of worship; every prayer is a mandated action; so some forms of worship are mandated actions.' Identify the figure and explain the natural order of the middle term.
?
Analyse: 'Some animals are not dogs; all animals are mortal; so some mortals are not dogs.' Is this a valid third-figure syllogism? If not, why?

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Why the Fourth Figure Is Necessary
Author argues for the indispensability of the fourth figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a categorical syllogism that requires the fourth figure based on the natural order of the middle term as predicate in the minor and subject in the major.

Core Idea
When the middle term is naturally predicate in the minor premise and subject in the major, the fourth figure is necessary.

The author gives an example where the conclusion 'Some changing things are celestial sphere' naturally requires the fourth figure. The middle term 'moving' is most naturally a predicate for 'celestial sphere' and a subject for 'changing'. This dual role fits the fourth figure uniquely. Let's explore why the first three figures fail to capture this natural order.

Guided Path
1
Find the conclusion
The conclusion is 'Some changing things are celestial sphere'. Subject: changing things; predicate: celestial sphere.
It is a particular affirmative.
2
Identify the middle term
The middle term is 'moving'. In the natural order, 'moving' is a predicate for 'celestial sphere' (celestial sphere is moving) and a subject for 'changing' (moving changes).
Middle term has a dual nature: it can be subject or predicate depending on its relation.
3
Construct the premises naturally
Write the premises so that 'moving' is subject in the major and predicate in the minor: 'Every moving thing is changing' (major) and 'Every celestial sphere is moving' (minor). This is the fourth figure (subject-subject? Actually: major middle term is subject, minor middle term is predicate).
The fourth figure is defined as: major premise has middle as subject, minor premise has middle as predicate.
4
Verify that the first three figures are unnatural
In the first figure, the middle term would need to be subject in the major and predicate in the minor, but here the natural order requires the opposite. The second and third figures also fail because they require uniform positions.
Only the fourth figure accommodates this mixed natural order.

The Syllogism

The Third Figure of Categorical Syllogisms
Author explains the third figure with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a third-figure categorical syllogism and explain why it is the natural pattern for certain conclusions.

Core Idea
The third figure makes the middle term subject in both premises, producing a particular conclusion.

The author introduces the third figure through the example of deriving 'some moving thing is sensitive' using 'animal' as the middle term. This shows how the natural mental preference for the first figure must sometimes yield to the third figure. We will analyze this example step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired conclusion and middle term
Locate the conclusion the author wants: 'some moving thing is sensitive' (بعض المتحرك حساس). The middle term that connects the two premises is 'animal' (حيوان).
2
Recognize the natural order preference
The natural order that comes first to the mind (الوضع الطبيعي) would have the middle term as subject of the major premise and predicate of the minor (first figure). Here, that would require 'animal' to be subject for 'moving' and predicate for 'sensitive', which is impossible.
3
See why the third figure is the only natural pattern
The only way to place 'animal' as subject for both 'moving' and 'sensitive' is to make it the subject in both premises. This is the defining feature of the third figure: middle term subject in both.
4
Compare with a first-figure attempt
To derive the same conclusion from the first figure, you would need to say 'some moving thing is animal' and 'every animal is sensitive'. But that reverses the natural order, making 'animal' the predicate of the minor premise.
5
Verify the syllogism's validity
Both premises are universal affirmative ('Every animal is moving', 'Every animal is sensitive'). In the third figure, two universal affirmatives yield a particular affirmative conclusion ('Some moving is sensitive'). This mood (Darapti) is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة الفن الأول في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية
مطلوبنا بعض المتحرك حساس بواسطة الحيوان واللائق بالطبيعي السابق إلى الفهم أن يكون الحيوان موضوعاً للمتحرك والحساس، ولا يتهيأ ذلك على غير نمط الشكل الثالث على الوجه الطبيعي، وهو أن نقول كل حيوان متحرك وكل حيوان حساس فبعض المتحرك حساس كما عرف ولو أردت استنتاجه من الشكل الأول لما أمكن، إلا أن نقول بعض المتحرك حيوان وكل حيوان حساس وهو خارج عن الوضع الطبيعي.
وأما ما لا يتم حصوله على النمط الطبيعي من غير الشكل الرابع، فكما لو كان مطلوبنا بعض المتغير فلك بواسطة المتحرك، والمتحرك على ما هو اللائق بالوضع الطبيعي السابق إلى الفهم، يجب أن يكون موضوعاً للمتغير ومحمولاً على الفلك، وذلك إنما يتم على هيئة الشكل الرابع دون الأول والثاني والثالث، وهذا الشكل - أعني الرابع - وإن أهمله المعلم الأول؛ لبعده عن الطباع، وجعل فائدة الترتيب الطبيعي فيه مهملة بالنظر إلى عسر إنتاجه، وتباعده عن الطبع دون الشكلين قبله فالموجب لذكره هاهنا - وإن كان محذور إهماله لبعده عن الطبع - يزيد على محذور تعلمه وتعلق غرض بعض المطالب به والتنبيه على ضعف المستلحق له بعد المعلم الأول عن الوقوف على غوره، ولا الكشف عنه، وعما يتعلق به من بيان الإنتاج، وشروطه، وأحكام الاختلاط، وغير ذلك مما أوضحناه.
Completion of the first art on defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. Our desired conclusion is 'some moving thing is sensitive' by means of 'animal'. What is appropriate for the natural order that comes first to understanding is that 'animal' should be the subject for 'moving' and 'sensitive'. This cannot be prepared except in the pattern of the third figure in the natural way, which is that we say: 'Every animal is moving' and 'Every animal is sensitive', so 'some moving thing is sensitive' as is known. And if you wanted to derive it from the first figure, it would not be possible unless we say 'some moving thing is animal' and 'every animal is sensitive', which is outside the natural order. As for what is not completed in the natural pattern except from the fourth figure, it is like if our desired conclusion is 'some changing thing is the celestial sphere' by means of 'moving'. 'Moving', according to what is appropriate for the natural order that comes first to understanding, must be a subject for 'changing' and a predicate for 'celestial sphere'. This is only completed in the form of the fourth figure, not the first, second, or third. This figure—I mean the fourth—although the first teacher neglected it due to its being far from natural dispositions, and he made the benefit of the natural arrangement in it negligible with regard to the difficulty of its production and its distance from nature without the two figures before it, what necessitates its mention here—even though the harm of neglecting it due to its distance from nature is increased over the harm of learning it—is the attachment of the purpose of some desired conclusions to it, and the alerting to the weakness of those who appended it after the first teacher regarding their inability to fathom its depth, nor to uncover it, nor to uncover what pertains to it of the explanation of production, its conditions, the rules of mixing, and other things that we have clarified.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage completes the first part on defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms by discussing the third and fourth figures. The author begins with an example in the third figure: the desired conclusion is 'some moving thing is sensitive' (بعض المتحرك حساس) with the middle term 'animal' (حيوان). The natural order for this middle term is that it be the subject for both 'moving' and 'sensitive', i.e., two universal affirmative premises: 'Every animal is moving' and 'Every animal is sensitive'. This yields the desired conclusion in the third figure. Attempting to derive it from the first figure would require converting one premise to 'some moving thing is animal', which is unnatural. Then the author turns to the fourth figure with an example: the desired conclusion is 'some changing thing is the celestial sphere' (بعض المتغير فلك) with the middle term 'moving' (متحرك). The natural order requires that 'moving' be the subject of 'changing' and the predicate of 'celestial sphere'. This arrangement fits the fourth figure alone. The author notes that Aristotle (the first teacher) neglected this figure because it is far from natural mental habits and difficult to produce. However, the author includes it for two reasons: some conclusions require it (like this example), and it exposes the weakness of later logicians who added it after Aristotle without fully understanding its depth, conditions, and rules of mixing (when premises from different figures are combined). The passage thus defends the inclusion of the fourth figure despite its artificiality.

Key Terms
الشكل الثالث
الوضع الطبيعي
الإنتاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the author's example of the third figure, what is the natural order of the middle term 'animal'?
A) It is the predicate of both premises.
B) It is the subject of both premises.
C) It is the subject of one premise and predicate of the other.
D) It does not appear as a middle term.

Key Takeaway
The third figure is the natural pattern when the middle term must be the subject in both premises, yielding a particular conclusion.

The Fourth Figure: Natural Order and Justification
Author explains the fourth figure with an example and justifies its inclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a fourth-figure categorical syllogism and evaluate the author's argument for including it despite Aristotle's neglect.

Core Idea
The fourth figure, though unnatural, is necessary for conclusions that require the middle term to be predicate of the major and subject of the minor.

The author presents an example that only the fourth figure can handle naturally: deriving 'some changing thing is the celestial sphere' using 'moving' as the middle term. He then defends the fourth figure against Aristotle's omission, arguing that some conclusions require it and that later logicians who added it lacked understanding. Let's unpack this.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired conclusion and middle term
The conclusion is 'some changing thing is the celestial sphere' (بعض المتغير فلك). The middle term that connects them is 'moving' (متحرك).
2
Determine the natural order requirement
The natural mental order demands that 'moving' be the subject for 'changing' and the predicate for 'celestial sphere'. This means the premises must be: every moving is changing, and every sphere is moving.
3
See why only the fourth figure satisfies this
In the fourth figure, the middle term is the predicate of the major premise and the subject of the minor. In our case, the major premise (containing 'sphere', the predicate of conclusion) is 'every sphere is moving' (middle as predicate); the minor premise is 'every moving is changing' (middle as subject). This fits only the fourth figure.
The first, second, and third figures cannot place the middle term as both subject and predicate in the required positions.
4
Understand the author's justification
Aristotle (the first teacher) neglected the fourth figure because it is far from natural mental habits and its conclusions are difficult to produce. However, the author includes it because some desired conclusions (like this one) require it, and to expose the weakness of later logicians who added it without understanding its depth.
5
Note the difficulty and conditions
The author mentions that the fourth figure involves difficulty in production (الإنتاج), specific conditions, and rules of mixing when premises from different figures are combined. These are explained elsewhere.

The Five Types of Conditional Conjunctions
Author enumerates five chapters covering conjunctions of conditional propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five types of conjunctions from simple conditionals and classify any given combination.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms combine muttasila and munfasila in five systematic ways.

This lesson introduces the Second Principle (al-qa'idah al-thaniyah) of the Daqa'iq al-Haqa'iq, which enumerates the five possible types of compound syllogisms that involve conditional propositions. Understanding this classification is essential for analyzing arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh that use if-then or either-or reasoning. We will walk through each chapter and learn how to identify which combination is being used.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two basic simple conditionals
Review the definitions: a muttasila (conditional) is of the form 'if A then B', and a munfasila (disjunctive) is of the form 'either A or B'. These are the building blocks.
2
Recognize that conjunctions can pair same types or mix with categoricals
The five chapters cover all possibilities: two muttasilas (Ch.1), two munfasilas (Ch.2), a categorical with muttasila (Ch.3), a categorical with munfasila (Ch.4), and a muttasila with a munfasila (Ch.5). Note that a categorical (hamliyya) is a simple subject-predicate proposition.
3
Memorize the five chapters in order
Use the mnemonic: 'M-M, D-D, C-M, C-D, M-D' (M=muttasila, D=munfasila, C=categorical). Practice recalling the list without looking.
4
Apply the classification
Given a description of a conjunction, decide which chapter it belongs to. For example, 'If A then B, and if B then C' is two muttasilas (Ch.1). 'Either A or B, and it is not A' is a categorical and a munfasila (Ch.4).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثانية
في الاقترانات من بسائط الشرطيات
وتشتمل على خمسة فصول:
الفصل الأول في الاقترانات الكائنة من متصلتين. الفصل الثاني: في الاقترانات الكائنة من منفصلتين. الفصل الثالث: في الاقترانات الكائنة من حملية ومتصلة. الفصل الرابع: في الاقترانات الكائنة من حملية ومنفصلة.
الفصل الخامس: في الاقترانات الكائنة من المتصلات والمنفصلات
The Second Principle: On Conjunctions from Simple Conditionals. It comprises five chapters: Chapter One: On conjunctions composed of two conditionals (muttasil). Chapter Two: On conjunctions composed of two disjunctives (munfasil). Chapter Three: On conjunctions composed of a categorical and a conditional (muttasil). Chapter Four: On conjunctions composed of a categorical and a disjunctive (munfasil). Chapter Five: On conjunctions composed of conditionals (muttasil) and disjunctives (munfasil).
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the types of compound syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani) that involve conditional propositions. The author introduces the Second Principle (al-qa'idah al-thaniyah) dealing with 'conjunctions' (iqtiranat) from simple conditionals (basait al-shartiyyat). Simple conditionals are propositions that are either conditional (muttasila, 'if-then') or disjunctive (munfasila, 'either-or'), without internal complexity. The author states that this principle contains five chapters (fusul). The first chapter covers conjunctions made up of two muttasil statements (muttasilatayn). The second chapter covers conjunctions of two munfasil statements (munfasilatayn). The third covers conjunctions of a categorical (hamliyya) and a muttasil statement. The fourth covers conjunctions of a categorical and a munfasil statement. The fifth covers conjunctions of muttasil and munfasil statements. This outline systematically covers all possible combinations where one or both premises are conditionals, moving from homogeneous pairs to mixed pairs. In the Islamic logical tradition, after establishing categorical syllogisms (al-qiyas al-hamli), the focus shifts to conditional syllogisms, which are crucial for arguments like: 'If the world is created, then it has a Creator; it is created; therefore it has a Creator.' Such arguments are common in kalam (dialectical theology) and usul al-fiqh (legal theory). The five chapters here set the stage for detailed analysis of each combination's validity and moods.

شرطيات
متصلتين
منفصلتين
حملية

Key Takeaway

Classification of Conditional Compound Syllogisms
Author outlines the five chapters on compound syllogisms from simple conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to name the five types of compound syllogisms from simple conditionals and identify which premises each combines.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms from conditionals are classified by their premise types: conjunctive, disjunctive, categorical, and mixed.

This passage outlines the five chapters covering compound syllogisms (iqtiranat) formed from simple conditional propositions (basa'it al-shartiyyat). The author systematically enumerates every possible combination of two premises: both conjunctive, both disjunctive, one categorical with one conditional, and mixed conjunctive-disjunctive. Mastering this classification will prepare you for the detailed rules in each chapter.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching category
The second rule (al-qa'ida al-thaniya) deals with compound syllogisms from simple conditionals. Note that 'simple conditionals' include both conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) propositions.
2
Memorize the five chapter topics
The text lists five chapters: (1) two muttasila, (2) two munfasila, (3) one hamliyya and one muttasila, (4) one hamliyya and one munfasila, (5) a mix of muttasila and munfasila. Associate each with a number.
Use the Arabic terms: متصلتين, منفصلتين, حملية ومتصلة, حملية ومنفصلة, المتصلات والمنفصلات.
3
Recognize the combinatorial logic
Observe that the classification covers all combinations of two premise slots. Each slot can be categorical (hamliyya), conjunctive conditional (muttasila), or disjunctive conditional (munfasila). The author ensures no gap: both conditionals, both disjunctives, each categorical paired with each conditional type, and a mixed conditional pair.
This exhaustive division is typical of classical logic works.
4
Connect to Islamic sciences
Think of real arguments in kalam or fiqh that use conditional syllogisms. For example, in kalam, 'If the world is created, then it needs a creator; the world is created; therefore it needs a creator' is a conditional syllogism with a categorical minor premise (chapter 3).
This helps ground the abstract classification in actual reasoning.

Classification of Compound Conditional Syllogisms
Author classifies compound syllogisms from conditional and categorical premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate and distinguish the five types of compound conditional syllogisms as classified by the author.

Core Idea
Compound conditional syllogisms have five types and four figures, mirroring categoricals.

Just as categorical syllogisms are classified by figure and mood, compound conditional syllogisms—those with conditional or mixed premises—also fall into distinct types. This lesson unpacks the author's taxonomy, showing how premises and conclusions divide, and how the types of compounds are enumerated. Understanding this classification is your first step toward mastering complex conditional reasoning in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the division of premises and conclusions
Note that premises are either categorical (hamliyya) or conditional (shartiyya). Conclusions are similarly divided. This symmetry is the foundation for the classification.
2
Identify the five compound types
The author lists five types: from two conditionals, from two disjunctives, from categorical and conditional, from categorical and disjunctive, and from conditional and disjunctive. Each will have its own chapter.
Type 4 and 5 are repeated in the list but distinct in order.
3
Apply the analogy with categorical figures
Just as categorical syllogisms have four figures based on the middle term's position, compound conditionals also divide into four figures, with exceptions to be noted later.
This analogy is crucial for understanding later derivations.
4
Map the exception clause
The author says 'except what we will exclude,' hinting that not all combinations yield valid figures. This prepares you for restrictions in later chapters.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة الثانية
الاقترانات من بسائط الشرطيات
نقول: إنه لما كانت المقدمات منقسمة إلى حَمْلية وشرطية، كانت المطلوبات أيضاً منقسمة إلى حَمْلية وشرطية، وكما أنه قد يؤلف من الحَمْليات اقترانات مفيدة للمطلوبات الشرطية، وهي إما أن يكون تأليفها من متصلتين، أو من منفصلتين، أو حَمْلية ومنفصلة، أو حَمْلية ومتصلة، أو متصلة ومنفصلة، وكما أن الاقترانات من الحَمْلية منقسمة إلى الأشكال الأربعة باعتبار اختلاف هيئة الحد الأوسط بين المقدمتين، فكذلك الاقتران الكائن من الشرطيات أو الشرطيات مع الحَمْليات، ينقسم إلى الأشكال الأربعة أيضاً، إلا في ما نستثنيه، ولنرسم في بيان كل صنف فصلاً:
أ في الاقترانات الكائنة من متصلتين.
ب في الاقترانات الكائنة من منفصلتين.
ج- في الاقترانات الكائنة من حَمْلية ومتصلة.
د- في الاقترانات الكائنة من حملية ومنفصلة.
هـ - في الاقترانات الكائنة من المتصلات والمنفصلات.
The second base: Compounds from simple conditionals. We say: Since premises are divided into categorical and conditional, the sought conclusions are also divided into categorical and conditional. And just as compounds may be composed from categoricals that yield conditional conclusions, and their composition is either from two conditionals (mutasila), or from two disjunctives, or from a categorical and a disjunctive, or a categorical and a conditional, or a conditional and a disjunctive; and just as the compounds from categoricals are divided into four figures based on the different form of the middle term between the two premises, so too the compound from conditionals or conditionals with categoricals is divided into four figures as well, except what we will exclude. And we will lay out a chapter for each type: A) On compounds consisting of two conditionals. B) On compounds consisting of two disjunctives. C) On compounds consisting of a categorical and a conditional. D) On compounds consisting of a categorical and a disjunctive. E) On compounds consisting of conditionals and disjunctives.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage introduces the second base (al-qa'ida al-thaniya) concerning compound syllogisms (iqtirayat) whose premises are conditional or mixed conditional and categorical. The author begins by noting that just as premises are divided into categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (shartiyya), the conclusions (matlubat) are similarly divided. Then he draws an analogy with categorical syllogisms: just as categorical syllogisms can be arranged in four figures depending on the position of the middle term, so too compound conditional syllogisms are divided into four figures, with some exceptions. He then enumerates five types of such compounds: from two conditionals (mutasilatayn), from two disjunctives (munfasilatayn), from a categorical and a conditional, from a categorical and a disjunctive, and from conditionals and disjunctives. Each type will be treated in its own chapter (fasl). This passage is essentially taxonomic, setting the framework for the detailed discussion that follows.

شرطية
حملية

Key Takeaway
Compound conditional syllogisms are classified into five types based on the combination of premises, and they analogously have four figures like categoricals, with exceptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Categorize the following compound syllogism according to the author's five types: 'If the water is pure, it is permissible for wudu. The water is not permissible for wudu. Therefore, the water is not pure.' (Hint: This uses a categorical premise in the second premise?) Actually, the second premise is categorical: 'The water is not permissible for wudu.' That makes it a compound of conditional and categorical, which is Type C or D. Identify which specific type.
?
Construct an example of a compound syllogism from two conditionals using a Fiqh ruling on zakat (e.g., If wealth reaches nisab, then zakat is due. If zakat is due, then it must be paid.) and identify the conclusion.

The Syllogism
intermediate
8 min
The Four Figures of Compound Conditional Syllogisms
Author explains the first type of compound conditional syllogism and derives its four figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which of the four figures a given compound conditional syllogism belongs to based on the position of the shared complete part.

Core Idea
Conditional syllogisms have four figures just like categoricals, determined by the shared part's position.

In the previous lessons, we learned about conditional propositions and simple conditional syllogisms (istithna'i). Now we move to compound syllogisms formed from two conditionals. The key concept is the 'shared part' that links the two premises. When that shared part is a complete part (the entire antecedent or consequent), its position creates four distinct figures, analogous to the four figures of categorical syllogisms. The author here defines the minor and major premises in terms of the conclusion: the minor contains the antecedent of the conclusion, the major contains its consequent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditional premises
The compound syllogism has two conditional premises. Label the minor (sughra) as the one containing the antecedent of the conclusion, and the major (kubra) as the one containing the consequent of the conclusion.
If unsure, first identify the conclusion: it is a conditional whose antecedent and consequent are partially given in each premise.
2
Find the shared complete part
The shared part (al-mushtarak) is a complete part (juz' tam) – either the entire antecedent (muqaddam) or entire consequent (tali) of one premise that also appears in the other. It must be identical in both premises.
If the shared part is incomplete (e.g., only a term within a clause), this lesson does not apply; that is a different type.
3
Determine the position in each premise
For each premise, note whether the shared part is the antecedent (muqaddam) or the consequent (tali). Record this for both the minor and major.
4
Classify into one of the four forms
Based on the positions, the form is: (1) consequent in minor and antecedent in major; (2) consequent in both; (3) antecedent in both; (4) antecedent in minor and consequent in major.
These correspond respectively to the first, second, third, and fourth figures of compound conditional syllogisms.
5
Name the figure
Associate the form with its figure number. For example, form 1 is the first figure, form 2 is the second, etc.
In practice, you can label them by the pattern: (tali in minor, muqaddam in major) = 1st; (tali in both) = 2nd; (muqaddam in both) = 3rd; (muqaddam in minor, tali in major) = 4th.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الأول
في الاقترانات الكائنة من المتصلات
والشركة بينها إما في جزء تام أو غير تام فإن كان الأول فهيئة ما به الاشتراك بين المقدمتين إما بكونه تالياً في الصغرى وهي ما مقدم المطلوب مصرح به فيها ومقدماً في الكبرى، وهي ما تالي المطلوب مصرح به فيها، وإما بكونه تاليهما، أو مقدمهما، أو مقدماً في الصغرى وتالياً في الكبرى، وباعتبار اختلاف هيئاته هذه انقسم إلى الأشكال الأربعة.
Chapter One: On compounds consisting of two conditionals. The common part between them is either a complete part or an incomplete part. If it is the first [i.e., complete], then the form of what is shared between the two premises is either: it is a consequent in the minor premise — which is that in which the antecedent of the sought conclusion is explicitly mentioned — and an antecedent in the major premise — which is that in which the consequent of the sought conclusion is explicitly mentioned — or it is a consequent in both, or an antecedent in both, or an antecedent in the minor and a consequent in the major. And according to the variation of these forms, it is divided into the four figures.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage constitutes the first chapter (al-fasl al-awwal), which examines compound syllogisms formed from two conditional premises (mutasilat). The author explains that the shared part between the two premises can be either a complete part (juz' tam) or an incomplete part (juz' ghayr tam). Here he only discusses the case of a complete part. When the shared part is complete, its position relative to the minor and major premises gives rise to four possible forms: (1) it is the consequent in the minor and antecedent in the major; (2) it is the consequent in both; (3) it is the antecedent in both; (4) it is the antecedent in the minor and consequent in the major. These four different positions correspond to the four figures of conditional compound syllogisms, analogous to the four figures in categorical syllogisms. The author's explanation references the 'sought conclusion' (matlub) in relation to the premises: the minor premise contains the antecedent of the conclusion, and the major contains the consequent. This is standard terminology in logic for compound syllogisms.

Key Terms
جُزْء تام (juz' tam): A complete part, i.e., the entire antecedent or consequent of a conditional.
جزء غير تام (juz' ghayr tam): An incomplete part, not the whole antecedent or consequent.
صغرى (sughra): The minor premise, containing the antecedent of the conclusion.
كبرى (kubra): The major premise, containing the consequent of the conclusion.
تال (tali): The consequent of a conditional.
مقدم (muqaddam): The antecedent of a conditional.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, how many forms (hiya) arise from the position of the complete shared part in a compound conditional syllogism from two conditionals?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Compound conditional syllogisms from two conditionals have four figures based on whether the complete shared part is the antecedent or consequent in each premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider these premises from Usul al-Fiqh: (1) If the contract is a sale, then it requires offer and acceptance. (2) If it requires offer and acceptance, then it is valid. Identify the shared part and determine which figure this compound conditional syllogism belongs to.
?
Construct your own compound conditional syllogism on a fiqh topic using the first figure (shared part is consequent in minor, antecedent in major). Label the parts.

Classification of Compound Conditional Syllogisms
Classifies compound conditional syllogisms into five types and outlines chapters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and identify the five types of compound syllogisms involving conditionals and explain their mapping to the four figures.

Core Idea
Compound conditional syllogisms mirror categorical figures through five premise combinations.

This passage begins the second main rule, introducing how compound syllogisms incorporate conditionals. Just as categorical syllogisms are classified by figure, mixtures of conditionals and categoricals also follow a structured typology. You will learn the five compositional types and their analogy to traditional figures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise and conclusion division
Note that both premises and conclusions are divided into categorical and conditional. This sets the framework for combining them.
2
List the five types of compound syllogisms
The author enumerates exactly five: from two conditionals, two disjunctives, categorical+disjunctive, categorical+conditional, and conditional+disjunctive.
Memorize this list as it organizes the rest of the chapters.
3
Understand the analogy with categorical figures
Just as categorical syllogisms have four figures based on middle term position, conditional syllogisms also have figures with exceptions noted later.
This shows the systematic nature of logic.
4
Recognize the chapter structure
Each type corresponds to a chapter (A to E). The first chapter covers two conditionals, which is the focus of the next lesson.

The second rule: Compound syllogisms from simple conditionals. We say: Since premises are divided into categorical and conditional, the sought conclusions are also divided into categorical and conditional. And just as compound syllogisms from categoricals may be composed that yield conditional sought conclusions, and their composition is either from two conditionals, or two disjunctives, or a categorical and a disjunctive, or a categorical and a conditional, or a conditional and a disjunctive; and just as compound syllogisms from categoricals are divided into the four figures by virtue of the difference in the configuration of the middle term between the two premises, likewise the compound syllogism composed of conditionals or conditionals with categoricals is divided into the four figures as well, except what we will except. Commentary
This passage opens the second rule of the book, dealing with compound syllogisms that involve conditional propositions. He then draws an analogy with categorical syllogisms, which are divided into four figures based on the position of the middle term. If it is a complete part, the configuration of that common part—whether it appears as the antecedent or consequent in the minor and major premises—determines the figure. The author describes four possible configurations: consequent in minor and antecedent in major, consequent in both, antecedent in both, and antecedent in minor and consequent in major. The Four Figures of Compound Conditional Syllogisms (Two Conditionals)
Explains the four figures for compound syllogisms from two conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the figure of a compound syllogism composed of two connected conditionals based on the position of the common part in the premises.

Core Idea
The figure of a conditional syllogism depends on whether the common part is antecedent or consequent in each premise.

This lesson dives into the first chapter, focusing on syllogisms made of two conditional premises. The author explains that the shared part can be either a complete term or an incomplete term. If complete, its position in the minor and major premises determines the figure. You will learn the four possible configurations that correspond directly to the four figures of categorical syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Define minor and major premises
The minor premise is where the antecedent of the conclusion appears; the major is where the consequent appears. This identification is key to analyzing the figure.
2
Locate the common part between the two premises
The common part is the shared term (either antecedent or consequent) that appears in both premises. It may be complete (the whole proposition) or incomplete (a part of the proposition).
Focus on the case of a complete common part as the author does.
3
Determine the position of the common part in each premise
Check whether the common part is the antecedent (muqaddam) or the consequent (tali) in the minor premise, and the same for the major premise.
There are four logical possibilities: consequent in minor & antecedent in major; consequent in both; antecedent in both; antecedent in minor & consequent in major.
4
Classify the figure
Each of the four positions corresponds to one of the four figures, analogous to categorical syllogisms. For example, common part as consequent in minor and antecedent in major corresponds to the first figure.
This analogy helps translate categorical figure knowledge to conditional arguments.
