The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
First Figure of Conditional Syllogisms: Structure and Four Moods
Defines the first figure of conditional syllogisms and lists its four valid moods with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first figure of a conditional syllogism and determine whether a given mood is valid based on its conditions.

Core Idea
The first figure of conditional syllogisms requires the shared term to be consequent in the minor and antecedent in the major.

In this lesson, we examine the first figure (al-shakl al-awwal) of conditional syllogisms. The author defines its distinctive structure: the common part (al-mushtarak) appears as the consequent (tālī) in the minor premise and as the antecedent (muqaddam) in the major premise. This is analogous to the categorical first figure but inverted. The conditions for validity are that the minor must be affirmative and the major universal. The figure yields four moods, each producing a different conclusion. Understanding this figure is essential for constructing sound theological and legal arguments that use conditional reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common part
In a conditional syllogism, the common part is the term that appears in both premises. In the first figure, this common part is the consequent (tālī) of the minor premise and the antecedent (muqaddam) of the major premise. Locate this term to confirm the figure.
2
Check the conditions
The author states two conditions: the minor premise must be affirmative (positive) and the major premise must be universal (either affirmative or negative). Verify these for the given syllogism.
If the minor is negative or the major is particular, the syllogism is not a valid first-figure mood.
3
Analyze the four moods
The passage enumerates four moods: (1) both universal affirmative, (2) both universal with major negative, (3) minor particular affirmative, major universal affirmative, (4) minor particular affirmative, major universal negative. For each, note the quality and quantity of premises and the conclusion.
The first mood is justified by the principle 'lāzim al-lāzim lāzim' (the necessary of the necessary is necessary).
4
Apply the principle of transitivity
The first mood works because if A implies B and B implies C, then A implies C. This transitivity is analogous to 'the predicate of the predicate is predicate of the subject' in categorical syllogisms.
This principle holds for conditional propositions with universal quantification.
5
Compare with categorical first figure
In categorical first figure, the middle term is subject in the major and predicate in the minor. In conditional first figure, the common term is consequent in the minor and antecedent in the major. The conditions (affirmative minor, universal major) are analogous.
Recognize the structural inversion due to the nature of conditionals.

As for the first figure—which is the one where the configuration of the common part is that it is the consequent in the minor and the antecedent in the major—it requires the affirmation of its minor, the universality of its major, and produces the four sought conclusions, and its production is self-evident and complete. As for the quantity of its moods, it follows what was previously established in categoricals, regardless of the quantity and quality of the parts of the conditionals. As for its moods: The first of them is from two universal affirmatives, and its conclusion is a universal affirmative conditional, as when we say: 'Whenever B is J, then H is R' and 'Whenever H is R, then D is A', therefore 'Whenever B is J, then D is A'. Because the necessary of the necessary is necessary, just as the predicate of the predicate is predicate of the subject. The second mood is from two universals with the major negative, as when we say: 'Whenever B is J, then H is R' and 'It is absolutely not the case that if H is R then D is A', therefore 'It is absolutely not the case that if B is J then D is A'. The third mood is from two affirmatives with the minor particular, and its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, as when we say: 'Sometimes when B is J, then H is R' and 'Whenever H is R, then D is A', therefore 'Sometimes when B is J, then D is A'. The fourth mood is from a particular affirmative minor and a universal negative major, and its conclusion is a particular negative, as when we say: 'Sometimes when B is J, then H is R' and 'It is absolutely not the case that if H is R then D is A', therefore 'It is absolutely not the case that whenever B is J, then D is A'.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage defines the first figure of conditional syllogisms and lists its four valid moods. The author states that in the first figure, the common part (the term shared between the two conditional premises) appears as the consequent (tālī) in the minor premise and as the antecedent (muqaddam) in the major premise. This corresponds to the categorical first figure where the middle term is subject in the major and predicate in the minor, but here inverted because conditionals are reversed in structure. The conditions for validity are: the minor must be affirmative, the major must be universal, and the figure produces all four types of conclusions (affirmative/negative, universal/particular). The mood count follows the categorical pattern. Four moods are then enumerated:

1. Both premises universal affirmative: e.g., if B then H, and if H then D, therefore if B then D. The justification: the necessary of the necessary is necessary, analogous to 'the predicate of the predicate is predicate of the subject' in categoricals.
2. Both universal, major negative: if B then H, and it is never that if H then D, therefore it is never that if B then D.
3. Minor particular affirmative, major universal affirmative: sometimes if B then H, and always if H then D, therefore sometimes if B then D.
4. Minor particular affirmative, major universal negative: sometimes if B then H, and never if H then D, therefore it is not always that if B then D.

Note the logical variables: ب, ج, هـ, ر, د, أ are placeholder terms. The OCR has some corruption in the second example. The underlying logical principle is that the first figure yields valid syllogisms analogously to categoricals.

Second Figure of Conditional Syllogism: Definition and Moods
Author defines second figure of conditional syllogism and lists its four moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second figure of conditional syllogism by its defining feature (shared consequent) and produce its four moods with correct conclusions.

Core Idea
The second figure shares a common consequent; it always yields a negative conclusion.

In this lesson, we examine the second figure of conditional syllogism, where the middle term is the consequent in both premises. The author first states its defining property, then lists four moods with symbolic letters. Understanding this figure is crucial for recognizing valid argument forms in Islamic theology and jurisprudence that use conditional statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature
The second figure is defined by having the same consequent (tali) in both the minor and major premises. Compare with the first figure where the consequent of minor is the antecedent of major.
2
Recall validity conditions from categoricals
The conditions for validity are identical to those of the categorical second figure: one premise must be negative and the major premise must be universal. This ensures a valid negative conclusion.
3
Analyse the first mood as an example
First mood: universal affirmative minor ('Every B is C implies H is R') and universal negative major ('Never if D is A then H is R') yield universal negative conclusion ('Never if B is C then D is A'). Verify the conditions: major is universal and negative, minor is affirmative and universal.
4
Examine the remaining moods
Second mood: universal negative minor + universal affirmative major → universal negative conclusion. Third mood: particular affirmative minor + universal negative major → particular negative conclusion. Fourth mood: particular negative minor + universal affirmative major → particular negative conclusion. Note all conclusions are negative.
OCR issues affect the fourth mood; it likely parallels the second mood with a particular minor.

Source Text

As for the second figure: it is that in which the consequent of the minor is the consequent of the major. The verification of its properties, conditions for production, being second, and the quantity of its moods, are as known in categoricals. Its first mood: 'Every B is C implies H is R' and 'It is never the case that if D is A then H is R' therefore 'It is never the case that if B is C then D is A'. Its second mood: 'It is never the case that if B is C then H is R' and 'Every D is A implies H is R' therefore 'It is never the case that if B is C then D is A'. Its third mood: 'Sometimes if B is C then H is R' and 'It is never the case that if D is A then H is R' and its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, namely 'Not every if B is C then D is A'. Its fourth mood: 'Not every if C is H is R' and 'Every D is A is R' therefore 'Not every if B is C then D is A'.

This passage defines the second figure of conditional syllogism and enumerates its four moods. The distinguishing feature is that the common term is the consequent in both premises. Then he lists four moods: (1) universal affirmative minor and universal negative major yields a universal negative conclusion; (2) universal negative minor and universal affirmative major yields a universal negative conclusion; (3) particular affirmative minor and universal negative major yields a particular negative conclusion; (4) universal negative minor? This figure is considered less direct than the first figure, often requiring a reduction or proof by contradiction.

Third Figure of Conditional Syllogism: Six Moods
Author defines third figure of conditional syllogism and lists its six valid moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining feature of the third figure of conditional syllogism and list its six valid moods with their conclusion types.

Core Idea
The third figure always yields a particular conclusion, never universal.

The third figure is defined by the common term being the antecedent in both premises. Just as in categorical logic, the third figure restricts the conclusion to particularity. The author presents six moods, each with a specific combination of premise quantities and qualities, and the corresponding conclusion. Understanding these moods is essential for evaluating conditional arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining feature of the third figure
The middle term (common term) is the antecedent in both premises. In the passage, the common term is represented by 'ب ج' appearing as antecedent in both the minor and major premises.
This contrasts with first figure where middle is antecedent in minor and consequent in major.
2
Recognize the analogy to categorical third figure
The author states that the conditions, properties, and number of moods are analogous to the categorical third figure. In categorical logic, the third figure always yields a particular conclusion; the same holds here.
3
List the six moods and their premises
For each mood, note the quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) of the two premises. The moods are: (1) two universal affirmatives, (2) particular affirmative + universal affirmative, (3) universal affirmative + particular affirmative, (4) universal affirmative + universal negative, (5) universal affirmative + particular negative, (6) particular affirmative + universal negative.
The order of premises matters: minor then major.
4
Determine the conclusion for each mood
Moods 1-3 give particular affirmative conclusions; moods 4-6 give particular negative conclusions. No mood yields a universal conclusion.
The first mood's conclusion in the passage uses 'هـ ر' as antecedent, likely an OCR error for 'ب ج'.
5
Understand why conclusions are particular
Because the middle term is subject in both premises, it is not distributed in either. A universal conclusion would require distribution of the middle term in at least one premise.
This is the same reason as in categorical third figure.

Commentary
This passage defines the third figure of conditional syllogism and enumerates its six moods. The defining feature is that the common term (the 'middle') is the antecedent in both premises. Fourth Figure of Conditional Syllogism: Definition and Structure
Author briefly defines the fourth figure of conditional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth figure of conditional syllogism by the position of the common term and describe its conditions as analogous to the categorical fourth figure.

Core Idea
The fourth figure reverses the common term's position from the first figure.

We have already studied the first figure of conditional syllogism, where the common term is consequent in the minor and antecedent in the major. The fourth figure is the opposite. In this lesson, we will define the fourth figure and note that its conditions, properties, and number of moods are the same as those of the categorical fourth figure. This illustrates a consistent pattern across categorical and conditional syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the common term in the definition
In the definition, the common term (ما به الاشتراك) is the term shared between the two premises. Locate it in the Arabic text: it is the term that appears in both premises.
2
Determine its position in the minor premise
The text states that the common term is the antecedent (مقدماً) in the minor premise. This means it is the 'if' part of the conditional in the first premise.
3
Determine its position in the major premise
The text states that the common term is the consequent (تالياً) in the major premise. This means it is the 'then' part of the conditional in the second premise.
4
Compare with the first figure
In the first figure, the common term is consequent in the minor and antecedent in the major. The fourth figure is the reverse, which makes it an indirect figure often reducible to the first.
This reversal is analogous to the categorical fourth figure.
5
Note the analogy to categoricals
The author says that the conditions (شروط), properties (خواص), and number of moods (كمية اقتراناته) are the same as in the categorical fourth figure. This means the valid moods of the categorical fourth figure (e.g., Baralipton, Celantes, Dabitis, Fapesmo, Frisesomorum) apply to conditional syllogisms as well.
Some logicians reject the fourth figure as independent, but it is taught for completeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الشكل الرابع وهو ما هيئة ما به الاشتراك بين المقدمتين بكونه مقدماً في الصغرى وتالياً في الكبرى، والحكم في شروطه، وخواصه، وكمية اقتراناته كما في الحمليات.
As for the fourth figure, it is the form in which the common term between the two premises is the antecedent in the minor and the consequent in the major. The judgment concerning its conditions, properties, and the quantity of its moods is as in the categoricals.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage provides a brief definition of the fourth figure of conditional syllogism. The author states that the common term appears as the antecedent in the minor premise and as the consequent in the major premise. This is the opposite of the first figure (where it was consequent in the minor and antecedent in the major). The author then says that the conditions, properties, and number of moods are the same as in the categorical fourth figure. This suggests that the fourth figure has five moods (a position adopted by some logicians) and is considered indirect, often reducible to the first figure. The passage is concise and serves as a summary, referring back to the detailed treatment of categoricals. This is typical of texts that assume prior knowledge of categorical logic.

The Third Figure of Conditional Syllogisms: Definition and Properties
Definition and general properties of the third figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining property of the third figure of conditional syllogisms and state its general productivity conditions.

Core Idea
In the third figure, the middle term is the antecedent in both premises, yielding only particular conclusions.

The third figure of conditional syllogisms is a powerful but limited form. Its defining characteristic is that the shared term (middle) appears as the antecedent in both the minor and major premises. This structure forces the conclusion to be particular, never universal. Understanding this figure helps you recognise a common pattern in theological and juristic arguments where general principles lead to specific applications.

Guided Path
1
Recognise the defining property
The author states: 'As for the third figure: it is the one whose form of the shared thing is by being antecedent in the minor and major premises.' This means the middle term (the term common to both premises) is always the 'if' part (antecedent) in both conditional premises.
Do not confuse with categorical third figure, where the middle term is the subject of the minor and predicate of the major.
2
Compare to categorical third figure
The author notes it follows the pattern of the third categorical figure in conditions of productivity and properties. In categorical third figure, the middle term is subject in both premises, analogous to antecedent in both here.
This analogy helps memory but be aware of differences in quantifiers.
3
Identify productivity conditions
The third figure never produces a universal conclusion. The author shows all six moods yield either a particular affirmative or particular negative conditional conclusion.
This is a key distinguishing feature from first and second figures.
4
Understand the purpose of the figure
The third figure is useful for deriving partial truths or counterexamples. For instance, in usul al-fiqh, it can show that a certain ruling applies only to some cases, not universally.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الشكل الثالث: وهو ما هيئة ما به الاشتراك بكونه مقدماً في الصغرى والكبرى، فعلى نسق الثالث من الحمليات في شرائط الإنتاج، وخواصه، وكمية قرائنه. الاقتران الأول منه قولنا: (كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "هـ" "د") و(كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "أ") ونتيجته جزئية موجبة متصلة وهي: قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "ر" فـ "د" "أ". الاقتران الثاني منه قولنا: (قد يكون إذا كان "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "أ") ونتيجته كالذي قبله. الاقتران الثالث منه قولنا: (كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "هـ" "د") و(قد يكون إذا كان "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "أ") ونتيجته كالذي قبله. الاقتران الرابع منه قولنا: (كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "هـ" "ر") و(ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "أ") ونتيجته جزئية سالبة متصلة وهي: ليس كل ما كان "هـ" "ر" فـ "د" "أ". الاقتران الخامس منه قولنا: (كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "هـ" "د") و(ليس كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "أ") ونتيجته كالاقتران الذي قبله. الاقتران السادس منه قولنا: (قد يكون إذا كان "ب" "ج" فـ "هـ" "د") و(ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "أ") ونتيجته كالذي قبله.
As for the third figure: it is the one whose form of the shared thing is by being antecedent in the minor and major premises. It follows the pattern of the third among categoricals in terms of conditions of productivity, its properties, and the quantity of its moods. The first mood of it is our statement: (Every thing that is B is C is H D) and (Every thing that is B is C is D A) and its conclusion is a particular affirmative conditional, namely: It may be that whenever it is H R then it is D A. The second mood of it is our statement: (It may be that whenever it is B C then it is D A) and its conclusion is like the previous. The third mood of it is our statement: (Every thing that is B C is H D) and (It may be that whenever it is B C then it is D A) and its conclusion is like the previous. The fourth mood of it is our statement: (Every thing that is B C is H R) and (It is not at all that whenever it is B C then it is D A) and its conclusion is a particular negative conditional, namely: Not every thing that is H R is D A. The fifth mood of it is our statement: (Every thing that is B C is H D) and (Not every thing that is B C is D A) and its conclusion is like the preceding mood. The sixth mood of it is our statement: (It may be that whenever it is B C then it is H D) and (It is not at all that whenever it is B C then it is D A) and its conclusion is like the previous.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage defines the third figure of conditional syllogisms and then enumerates its six productive moods. The author first describes the defining property of the third figure: the middle term (the term shared between both premises) is the antecedent in both the minor and major premises. This parallels the third figure of categorical syllogisms. The author then lists each mood. The first three moods yield the same conclusion: a particular affirmative conditional (قد يكون). Their premises vary: the first has two universal premises, the second has two particular premises, and the third has one universal and one particular. The fourth, fifth, and sixth moods yield a particular negative conditional (ليس كل). The first premise is universal affirmative or particular affirmative, and the second is universal negative or particular negative. The pattern shows that the third figure never produces a universal conclusion; it always yields a particular one. Students should memorize these moods as they are the building blocks for evaluating arguments in this figure.

The Six Productive Moods of the Third Figure
Enumerates the six moods with concrete examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct each of the six moods of the third figure of conditional syllogisms and determine their conclusion type.

Core Idea
The six moods of the third figure all yield particular conclusions: three affirmative, three negative.

Now that you understand the structure of the third figure, let's dive into its six productive moods. Each mood is a specific combination of premise quantifiers (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) and yields a particular conclusion. Mastering these moods is essential for quickly evaluating arguments that take this form in Islamic logical texts.

Guided Path
1
List the first three moods (affirmative conclusions)
Moods 1-3 all have a particular affirmative conditional conclusion (قد يكون). The premises vary: Mood 1 has two universal affirmatives; Mood 2 has two particular affirmatives; Mood 3 has one universal and one particular affirmative.
Memorize the pattern: universal-universal, particular-particular, universal-particular.
2
List the last three moods (negative conclusions)
Moods 4-6 all have a particular negative conditional conclusion (ليس كل). Mood 4 has a universal affirmative first premise and a universal negative second premise; Mood 5 has universal affirmative and particular negative; Mood 6 has particular affirmative and universal negative.
Note that the second premise is always negative in these moods.
3
Notice the role of quantity
The third figure never produces a universal conclusion. The author emphasises this by showing all conclusions are particular, whether affirmative or negative.
This is a strict rule; any mood claiming a universal conclusion in the third figure is invalid.
4
Practice constructing a mood
Take a pair of premises in the form: 'If B then H' and 'If B then D'. If both are universal, it is Mood 1 and yields 'Sometimes if H then D'. If one is particular, adjust accordingly.
Use the Arabic variables to keep track: 'كل ما كان ب ج فـ هـ د' etc.

The Fourth Figure of Conditional Syllogisms and Its Five Moods
Author describes the fourth figure of conditional syllogisms and enumerates its five moods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth figure of a conditional syllogism by its middle term position and enumerate its five valid moods.

Core Idea
The fourth figure mirrors the categorical fourth figure, with the middle term as antecedent in the minor and consequent in the major.

Conditional syllogisms, like categorical ones, have four figures. The fourth figure is defined by the position of the middle term: it appears as the antecedent in the minor premise and the consequent in the major premise. This figure has five productive moods, each with a specific combination of premise quantities and qualities. Understanding these moods helps in constructing valid conditional arguments, which are essential in theological and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Define the fourth figure
The fourth figure of a conditional syllogism is characterized by the middle term being the antecedent (muqaddam) in the minor premise and the consequent (tālin) in the major premise. This parallels the fourth figure in categoricals (subject in minor, predicate in major).
Middle term is 'what is shared' between premises.
2
Recognize the five moods
The author lists five moods (iqtiranat). Each mood is a specific combination of premise types (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) that yields a valid conclusion. Memorize the pattern: first mood uses two universal negatives, second uses universal affirmative and universal affirmative, etc.
The conclusions vary: first gives universal negative, second and third give particular affirmative, fourth and fifth give particular negative.
3
Verify the parallel with categoricals
The author states that the conditions, properties, and number of moods are 'as in the categoricals'. This means the logical structure is isomorphic to the categorical fourth figure. If you know the categorical fourth figure, you can transpose it to conditionals by swapping subject-predicate with antecedent-consequent.
This reinforcement aids memory.
4
Apply an Islamic example
Take the first mood: 'It is not at all that when B is C then D' and 'Every thing that is C A is B C' yields 'It is not at all that when H is R and D A'. Replace variables with Islamic content: e.g., if the middle term represents a condition for obligation, the conclusion may negate a different condition. Work through the substitutions carefully.
Due to OCR issues, the exact variables are uncertain, but the logical form remains clear.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الشكل الرابع وهو ما هيئة ما به الاشتراك بين المقدمتين بكونه مقدماً في الصغرى وتالياً في الكبرى، والحكم في شروطه، وخواصه، وكمية اقتراناته كما في الحمليات. الاقتران الأول منه قولنا: ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فـ "د") و(كل ما كان "ج" "أ" فـ "ب" "ج") فـ (ليس البتة إذا كان "هـ" "ر" و "د" "أ"). الاقتران الثاني منه قولنا: كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "هـ" "ر") و(كل ما كان "ج" "أ" فـ "ب" "ج") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "د" و "د" "أ"). الاقتران الثالث منه قولنا: كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "هـ" "د") و (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "أ" فـ "ب" "ج") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" "د" و "د" "أ"). الاقتران الرابع منه قولنا: كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "هـ" "ر") و(ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "أ" و "ب" "ج") فـ (ليس كل ما كان "هـ" "ر" فـ "د" "أ"). الاقتران الخامس منه قولنا: (قد يكون إذا كان "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "ر") و (ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "أ" فـ "ب" "ج") فـ (ليس كل ما كان "هـ" "ر" فـ "د" "أ"). وما به تتفق هذه الأشكال وتفترق فعلى ما سبق في الحمليات.
As for the fourth figure, it is the one whose form of the shared thing between the two premises is by being antecedent in the minor and consequent in the major. The judgment concerning its conditions, properties, and quantity of its moods is as in the categoricals. The first mood of it is our statement: (It is not at all that when B is C then D) and (Every thing that is C A is B C) therefore (It is not at all that when H is R and D A). The second mood of it is our statement: (Every thing that is B C is H R) and (Every thing that is C A is B C) therefore (It may be that when H is D and D A). The third mood of it is our statement: (Every thing that is B C is H D) and (It may be that when D A then B C) therefore (It may be that when H is D and D A). The fourth mood of it is our statement: (Every thing that is B C is H R) and (It is not at all that when D A and B C) therefore (Not every thing that is H R is D A). The fifth mood of it is our statement: (It may be that when B C then D R) and (It is not at all that when D A then B C) therefore (Not every thing that is H R is D A). And what unites and distinguishes these figures is as stated previously in the categoricals.

The Five Mixed Conjunctions in Conditional-Categorical Syllogisms
Author lists five forms of mixed conditional-categorical syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantifier combinations of the five mixed conjunctions and determine their valid moods.

Core Idea
Five mixed conjunctions: each defined by the quantifiers of its conditional and categorical premises.

In this passage, the author enumerates five specific forms of mixed syllogisms, where one premise is conditional and the other is categorical. Just as categorical syllogisms have figures and moods, mixed syllogisms have their own valid conjunctions. Understanding these five conjunctions will allow you to quickly identify valid mixed syllogisms in Islamic logical texts, especially in kalam arguments that combine conditional and categorical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the variables
Recognize that the Arabic letters (ب, ج, د, هـ, ر, أ, و) are placeholders for terms. Their positions vary across conjunctions, so do not assume they refer to the same terms.
The same letter in different conjunctions may represent different terms.
2
Identify the major and minor premises
In each conjunction, the major premise is the conditional (shartiyya) and the minor is the categorical (hamliyya). Note the quantifier of each: universal affirmative (كل ما), universal negative (ليس البتة), particular affirmative (قد يكون), or particular negative (ليس كل).
The quantifier determines the strength of the premise.
3
Extract the conclusion quantifier
The conclusion is also conditional. Observe how its quantifier relates to the premises. For example, in the first conjunction, two universal negatives yield a universal negative conclusion.
The conclusion's quantifier follows rules analogous to categoricals.
4
Compare with categorical rules
The author notes that agreements and differences follow the same principles as in categoricals. Recall that in categoricals, universal premises yield universal conclusions when certain conditions are met. Apply similar reasoning to these mixed forms.
This analogy helps validate the conjunctions.
5
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the first conjunction: Major: 'Absolutely not if the world is eternal then it is not contingent' (universal negative conditional). Minor: 'Every created thing is contingent' (universal affirmative categorical). Conclusion: 'Absolutely not if the world is created then it is not contingent.' This matches the form and is valid.
The example uses kalam terms to show real application.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاقتران الأول منه قولنا: ليس البتة إذا كان "ب" "ج" فـ "د") و(كل ما كان "ج" "أ" فـ "ب" "ج") فـ (ليس البتة إذا كان "هـ" "ر" و "د" "أ"). الاقتران الثاني منه قولنا: كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "هـ" "ر") و(كل ما كان "ج" "" فـ "ب" "ج") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" د و). الاقتران الثالث منه قولنا: كل ما كان "ب" "ج" هـ" "د") و (قد يكون إذا كان "د" "أ" فـ "ب" "ج") فـ (قد يكون إذا كان "هـ" د و). الاقتران الرابع منه قولنا: كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "هـ" "ر") و(ليس البتة إذا كان "د" "أ" و "ب" "ج") فـ (ليس كل ما كان "هـ" "ر" ف "د" "أ"). الاقتران الخامس منه قولنا: (قد يكون إذا كان "ب" " ج البتة إذا كان "د" "أ" فـ "ب" "ج") فـ ليس كل ما كان "هـ" "" فـ "د" "أ") وما به تتفق هذه الأشكال وتفترق فعلى ما سبق في الحَمْليات.
The first conjunction of it is our statement: 'Absolutely not if B is C then D' and 'Everything that is C is A is B is C' then 'Absolutely not if H is R and D is A'. The second conjunction of it is our statement: 'Everything that is B is C is H is R' and 'Everything that is C is [missing] is B is C' then 'It may be that if H is D and W'. The third conjunction of it is our statement: 'Everything that is B is C is H is D' and 'It may be that if D is A then B is C' then 'It may be that if H is D and W'. The fourth conjunction of it is our statement: 'Everything that is B is C is H is R' and 'Absolutely not if D is A and B is C' then 'Not everything that is H is R is D is A'. The fifth conjunction of it is our statement: 'It may be that if B is J, absolutely not if D is A then B is C' then 'Not everything that is H is [missing] is D is A'. And what these forms agree on and differ in is as previously in categoricals.

Commentary
This passage enumerates five forms (conjunctions) of mixed syllogisms where premises include both conditional and categorical propositions. Each conjunction is defined by the quantifiers of its major and minor premises. For example, the first conjunction has a universal negative conditional as its major premise and a universal affirmative categorical as its minor, yielding a universal negative conditional conclusion. The second has a universal affirmative conditional major and a universal affirmative categorical minor, yielding a particular affirmative conditional conclusion. The third has a universal affirmative conditional major and a particular affirmative categorical minor, yielding a particular affirmative conditional conclusion. The fourth has a universal affirmative conditional major and a universal negative categorical minor, yielding a universal negative conditional conclusion. The fifth has a particular affirmative conditional major and a universal negative categorical minor, yielding a universal negative conditional conclusion. The author notes that the agreements and differences among these forms follow the same principles as in categorical syllogisms, meaning the rules of figure and mood apply analogously.

What Makes a Syllogism Productive: Form Under Assumption
Author discusses necessity of conclusion under assumption and case of incomplete middle term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between formal necessity under assumption (taslim) and real implication, and identify when a syllogism fails due to incomplete sharing of the middle term.

Core Idea
A syllogism's validity depends on form under assumption, not on real truth of premises.

In classical mantiq, a syllogism's productivity is often misunderstood. Many think that premises must be true for a conclusion to follow. The author of this passage clarifies that even if premises are false or have false parts, the conclusion is still necessary under the assumption (taslim) of the implication. However, if the middle term is not fully shared between premises, the syllogism fails regardless of form. This lesson unpacks these two critical points.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish taslim from real implication
The author says the conclusion is 'لازمة من تسليم اللزوم للمقدمات' (necessary from acknowledging the implication of the premises). This means the necessity is conditional on granting the implication, not on the factual truth of the premises. Identify this key distinction in the Arabic.
Taslim is a technical term: the act of assuming premises for the sake of argument.
2
Examine the first-figure example
The author gives: 'كل ما كان الفرد منقسماً بمتساويين فهو زوج' and 'كل ما كان زوجاً فهو عدد' leading to 'كل ما كان الفرد منقسماً بمتساويين فهو عدد'. Recognise this as a valid Barbara syllogism regardless of whether the premises are actually true. The conclusion follows under assumption.
Notice that the middle term 'زوج' (even) appears identically in both premises.
3
Analyse the case of incomplete sharing
The author writes 'كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "ر"' and 'كل ما كان "د" "أ" في "د" "ل"' as an example where the shared part is not complete. Identify that the middle term in the major premise is not the same as in the minor. This prevents the conclusion from following. No production, even if the form resembles the first figure.
The notation uses variables; here the missing identity is the core issue.
4
Apply the condition to an Islamic syllogism
Take the kalam proof: 'The world is temporally originated (A); everything temporally originated has a cause (B); therefore the world has a cause (C).' Check whether the middle term 'temporally originated' is fully shared. If an opponent reinterprets it differently in each premise, the argument fails. This condition prevents such fallacies.

واعلم أن النتيجة في جميع هذه الاقترانات من الأشكال الأربعة لازمة من تسليم اللزوم للمقدمات وسلبه، وسواء كانت الصادقة منها كاذبة الأجزاء أو صادقة وسواء كانت الصادقة منها حقيقية أو غير حقيقية، لكن ما تألف من الصادقة الكاذبة الأجزاء، أو المقدمات الصادقة غير الحقيقية، فالنتيجة فيها وإن كانت لازمة فليس إلا بحسب التسليم، لا بحسب اللزوم في نفس الأمر، ولعدم صدق أجزاء النتيجة في نفس الأمر عن الاقتران الكائن من المقدمات الصادقة الكاذبة الأجزاء، فقد يظن أنه غير منتج، وليس كون الاقتران من أي شكل قدر منتجاً، لكون اللازم عنه صادق الأجزاء في نفسه، بل لكونه إذا سلم لزم من تسليمه تسليم قول آخر له - ونظير ذلك من الشكل الأول من الاقتران الأول منه - (كل ما كان الفرد منقسماً بمتساويين فهو زوج) و(كل ما كان زوجاً فهو عدد) فـ (كل ما كان الفرد منقسماً بمتساويين فهو عدد) وعليك باعتباره في ما بقي من الاقترانات. وأما إن كان الاشتراك في جزء غير تام فإن كانت هيئة المتوسط على هيئة الشكل الأول كقولنا كل ما كان "ب" "ج" فـ "د" "ر") و(كل ما كان "د" "أ" في "د" "ل") فلا إنتاج؛ لجواز أن يكون المقدم من الكبرى غير لازم للتالي من الصغرى، وإنما ينتج أن لو كان لازماً، إما ملازمة المساوي أو الأعم، ومع ذلك فالإنتاج لا يكون لازماً لعموم الصورة، بل لخصوص المادة، ولا اعتبار به، ولهذا المعنى يمتنع الإنتاج، وإن كان على نسق باقي الأشكال واعتبر كل ما يفرض من اقتراناتها بنفسك تجده على ما حققناه
And know that the conclusion in all these conjunctions of the four figures is implied from the acknowledgment of the implication for the premises and its negation, regardless of whether the premises are true with false parts or true, and regardless of whether the premises are genuine or non-genuine. However, what is composed of true premises with false parts, or true non-genuine premises, the conclusion, although implied, is only according to acknowledgment, not according to implication in reality. And due to the lack of truth of the parts of the conclusion in reality from the conjunction composed of true premises with false parts, it may be thought that it is non-productive. And the productivity of a conjunction from any figure is not because the implied conclusion is true in itself, but because if it is acknowledged, the acknowledgment of another statement is implied from its acknowledgment. An analogue of this from the first figure of its first conjunction is: 'Everything that is divisible into two equal parts is even' and 'Everything that is even is a number' so 'Everything that is divisible into two equal parts is a number'. And you should consider it for the remaining conjunctions. As for if the sharing is in a part that is not complete, and the shape of the middle is like the shape of the first figure, such as our statement: 'Everything that is B is C is D R' and 'Everything that is D A is in D L' then there is no production; because it is possible that the premise from the major is not implied by the consequent of the minor. It would only produce if it were implied, either with the implication of the equal or the more general. And even then, the production is not necessary for the generality of the form, but for the particular matter, and no consideration is given to it. For this reason, production is impossible, even if it is in the pattern of the remaining figures. And consider every assumed conjunction yourself, you will find it as we have verified.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage explains the nature of validity in mixed syllogisms. The author distinguishes between logical necessity under assumption (taslim) and real implication. Even if premises are factually false or have false parts, the syllogistic form still yields a conclusion as long as the implication relation is granted. He gives the example: 'Everything divisible into two equals is even; every even is a number; therefore everything divisible into two equals is a number' — this syllogism is formally valid regardless of the truth of its premises. He then addresses a case where the middle term is not fully shared (incomplete sharing). If the middle term in one premise is not the same as in the other, the syllogism fails to produce. For instance, if the major premise has the form 'Everything that is B is C is D R' and the minor 'Everything that is D A is in D L', then because the middle term is not identical, no conclusion follows. The author warns that even if the shape resembles the first figure, productivity depends on material implication, not just form. This lesson guards against assuming that any syllogism with a similar pattern is automatically valid.

نتيجة
تسليم
إنتاج
اشتراك

Key Takeaway
Syllogistic necessity is conditional upon assuming the premises' implication, not their factual truth; and a complete sharing of the middle term is essential for production.

Condition for Production in Conjunctive Syllogisms
Author explains the necessary condition for conclusion in conjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the necessary condition for a conjunctive syllogism to produce a conclusion and distinguish between formal validity and material necessity.

Core Idea
Conclusion requires a necessary relation, not just valid form.

You already know that a conjunctive syllogism links two premises. But not every conjunction yields a valid conclusion. The author of this passage reveals a crucial condition: the antecedent of the major premise must be necessary for the consequent of the minor. Even if the form looks correct, without this necessary link, the syllogism is sterile. Let's unpack this condition and see why production depends on matter, not just shape.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
A conjunctive syllogism has a major premise (a conditional) and a minor premise (another conditional). Locate the antecedent (muqaddam) in the major and the consequent (tali) in the minor.
These are not the same as categorical syllogism terms.
2
Check the necessary relation
Ask: Is the antecedent from the major necessarily true for the consequent from the minor? The author says production only occurs if this relation holds, either as equality (musawi) or more general (a'am).
Equality means they are coextensive; more general means the antecedent is a superset.
3
Distinguish form from matter
Even if the necessary relation exists, the conclusion is not guaranteed by the syllogistic form (surah) alone. Production arises from the specific content (maddah) of the premises.
This means you cannot rely solely on the shape of the argument; you must examine the actual propositions.
4
Test an example
Take a valid conjunctive syllogism: 'Every action is subject to divine command. Every prayer is an action. Therefore prayer is subject to divine command.' Here, 'action' (antecedent from major) is more general than 'prayer' (consequent from minor), so the necessary relation holds. Verify that this matches the author's condition.
The example is from Islamic fiqh (actions and prayer).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمة الفن الأول: في تعريف صور القياسات الاقترانية. فلا إنتاج؛ لجواز أن يكون المقدم من الكبرى غير لازم للتالي من الصغرى، وإنما ينتج أن لو كان لازماً، إما ملازمة المساوي أو الأعم، ومع ذلك فالإنتاج لا يكون لازماً لعموم الصورة، بل لخصوص المادة، ولا اعتبار به، ولهذا المعنى يمتنع الإنتاج، وإن كان على نسق باقي الأشكال واعتبر كل ما يفرض من اقتراناتها بنفسك تجده على ما حققناه.
Continuation of the first art: On defining forms of conjunctive syllogisms. So there is no production because it is possible that the antecedent from the major is not necessary for the consequent from the minor. It only produces if it is necessary, either with the necessity of equality or of the more general. Nevertheless, production is not necessary due to the generality of the form, but due to the particular matter, and it is not considered. Hence production is impossible, even if it is in the pattern of the remaining figures, and consider whatever assumptions of their conjunctions yourself, you will find it as we have verified.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition under which a conjunctive syllogism yields a conclusion. The author states that there is no production (intaj) if the antecedent in the major premise is not necessary (lazim) for the consequent in the minor premise. Production only occurs if there is a necessary relation, either equal (musawi) or more general (a'am). However, even then the production is not due to the form (surah) but due to the matter (maddah), so it is not considered a purely formal conclusion. The passage emphasizes that form alone does not guarantee production; the logical relation between propositions matters. The author invites the reader to test any assumed conjunction and see that it holds true as verified. For example, in a valid conjunctive syllogism like 'Every action (fi'l) is subject to divine command. Every prayer is an action. Therefore, prayer is subject to divine command,' the antecedent (action) in the major is necessary for the consequent (prayer) in the minor.

التالي من الصغرى

Key Takeaway
A conjunctive syllogism yields a conclusion only when the antecedent of the major premise is necessarily related to the consequent of the minor premise; this necessity is material, not formal.

Exception to Two Affirmative Real Disjunctives
Author explains exception to rule that two affirmative real disjunctives yield no conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when two affirmative real disjunctive premises yield a valid conclusion and when they do not.

Core Idea
Real disjunctives generally block conclusions from two affirmatives, but necessary concomitants break the rule.

This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq delves into a subtle exception to a well-known rule. While scholars agree that two affirmative real disjunctives produce no conclusion, the author reveals a condition where they do—when the opposite of one part and the repeated part are necessary concomitants of the other. Understanding this exception sharpens your skill in evaluating disjunctive syllogisms in Islamic logical texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premises
Locate the two real disjunctive premises in the passage. They are affirmative (no negation). In the example: 'Either the sun is rising or the night exists' and 'Either the night exists or the day exists'.
Real disjunctives are exclusive (cannot be both true and cannot be both false).
2
Check the common term
Identify the term that appears in both premises. Here, 'night exists' is the common term (had mushtarak). It is complete because it appears fully in both.
A complete common term occurs when the same expression is repeated exactly.
3
Apply the general rule
Recall that scholars agree: two affirmative real disjunctives with a complete common term yield no conclusion. Why? Because a real disjunctive implies no third alternative; combining two such disjunctives would create a contradiction unless a condition is met.
The general rule prevents production unless an exception applies.
4
Examine the condition for the exception
The author states that real opposition (al-'inad al-haqiqi) prevents a third alternative only if the opposite of one part and the repeated part are not necessary concomitants (lawazim) of the other part. If they are necessary, the rule does not apply. In the example, 'night' is a necessary concomitant of 'day not existing'.
Necessary concomitance means the terms are so related that one necessarily implies the other.
5
Derive the conclusion
Using the necessary relation: if the sun is rising, then the night does not exist. From the second disjunctive, if the night does not exist, then the day exists. Therefore, if the sun is rising, the day exists. This conclusion is valid because the condition of real opposition is satisfied (the opposite and repeated are necessary concomitants).
The conclusion is a conditional statement, not a disjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في الاقترانات الكائنة من المنفصلات. نقول: قد عرف أنه ليس من الاقترانات قياس إلا وله حد مشترك جزأي المطلوب، فالاقتران الكائن من المنفصلتين لا بد له من حد مشترك، وهو إما أن يكون تاماً أو ناقصاً، فإن كان تاماً فإما أن تكون المنفصلات حقيقية أو غير حقيقية، فإن كانت حقيقية فقد اتفقوا على أنه لا إنتاج فيها من موجبتين، قال أفضل المتأخرين: لأنك إذا قلت لا يخلو إما أن يكون "أ" "ب" وإما أن يكون "ج" "د" فهذا القول إنما يكون صدقاً إذا لم يكن قسم ثالث؛ لأن معنى قولنا: (لا يخلو إما أن يكون "أ" "ب" وإما أن يكون "ج" "د") هو أنه إما أن يكون "أ" "ب" وأما إن لم يكن كان لا محالة "ج" "د") فـ (إن كان قد لا يكون "ج" "د") فالقضية كاذبة. اللهم إلا أن تُجعل جزئية، فإذا كان قولنا: (إما أن يكون "أ" "ب" وإما أن يكون "ج" "د") إنما صدقا إذا لم يكن قسم ثالث، فإن كرر الجزء الأوسط فقيل (إما أن يكون "ج" "د" وإما أن يكون "ج" "د" وإما أن يكون "هـ" "ر" ) فإن كان ("هـ" "ر") هو "أ" "ب" القضيتان واحدة، والنتيجة باطلة؛ لأنه ينتج إما أن يكون "أ" "ب" وإما أن يكون "أ" "ب") وإن كان قولنا : ( "هـ" "ر") غير قولنا: "أ" "ب") فهاهنا قسم ثالث، والقضيتان المنفصلتان كاذبتان وهو بعيد عن التحقيق فإن العناد الحقيقي وإن كان مانعاً من قسم ثالث فليس ذلك مطلقاً، بل بشرط أن لا يكون المعاند للجزء والمكرر من لوازم الجزء الآخر ، وأما إن كان ملازماً له فلا يمنع من العناد التام، ولا يوجب الكذب في القضيتين ولا الاتحاد بينهما، وذلك كما في قولنا: إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون الليل موجوداً) أو (دائماً إما أن يكون الليل موجوداً وإما أن يكون النهار موجوداً) فالنتيجة كل ما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار (موجود لأنه مهما سلم المنع من الجمع بين طلوع الشمس ووجود النهار والمنع من الخلو منهما، وسلم المنع من الجمع بين وجود الليل ووجود النهار والمنع من الخلو
Second chapter on conjunctions composed of disjunctives. We say: It is known that there is no conjunction that is a syllogism except that it has a common term between the two parts of the desired [conclusion]. So a conjunction composed of two disjunctives must have a common term, which is either complete or incomplete. If it is complete, then the disjunctives are either real or non-real. If they are real, they have agreed that there is no production from two affirmative [disjunctives]. The best of the later [scholars] said: Because when you say 'It is not free that either it is A B or it is C D', this statement is only true if there is no third division; because the meaning of our statement 'It is not free that either it is A B or it is C D' is that it is either A B, and if it is not, then necessarily it is C D. So if it might not be C D, then the proposition is false. Unless it is made particular. So if our statement 'Either it is A B or it is C D' is only true if there is no third division, then if the middle term is repeated and it is said 'Either it is C D or it is C D or it is H R', then if H R is A B, the two propositions are one, and the conclusion is invalid because it yields 'Either it is A B or it is A B'. And if H R is not our statement A B, then there is a third division, and the two disjunctive propositions are false. And that is far from verification, because real opposition, although it prevents a third division, is not absolute, but only on condition that the opposite of the part and the repeated item are not from the necessary concomitants of the other part. But if it is necessary, then it does not prevent complete opposition, nor does it cause falsehood in the two propositions nor identity between them. As in our statement: 'Either the sun is rising or the night exists', or 'Always either the night exists or the day exists'. So the conclusion: whenever the sun is rising, the day exists because whenever the prevention of combination between sunrise and the existence of day and the prevention of void from both are conceded, and the prevention of combination between the existence of night and the existence of day and the prevention of void are conceded...
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage begins a new chapter on syllogisms composed of disjunctive propositions. The author first reiterates that every syllogism must have a common term (had mushtarak) between the two parts of the desired conclusion. For two disjunctives, this common term may be complete (tamm) or incomplete. If it is complete, then the disjunctives are either real (haqiqiyya) or non-real. The focus is on real disjunctives (exclusive 'or'), and the author states that scholars agree that no conclusion follows from two affirmative real disjunctives. The best of the later scholars explains: when we say 'Either it is A B or it is C D' (meaning a disjunctive proposition), this is only true if there is no third alternative. The meaning implies that if it is not A B, then necessarily it is C D. So if it is possible that it is not C D, the disjunctive is false. However, this holds only if the disjunction is universal; if made particular, it might be different. The author then considers the case where the middle term is repeated: if we say 'Either C D or C D or H R', then if H R is identical to A B, the two premises become one and the conclusion is trivial (either A B or A B). If H R is different from A B, then there is a third alternative, making both disjunctive premises false. The author then critiques this view, stating that real opposition (al-'inad al-haqiqi) indeed prevents a third alternative, but not absolutely. It only prevents a third alternative if the opposite of the part and the repeated item are not necessary concomitants (lawazim) of the other part. If they are necessary, then there is no falsehood or identity. An example: 'Either the sun is rising or the night exists' and 'Either the night exists or the day exists' – here, the conclusion is that whenever the sun is rising, the day exists, because the prevention of combination and void are conceded. This demonstrates that when the terms are necessarily related, two affirmative real disjunctives can produce a conclusion.

Key Terms
حد مشترك
منفصلات حقيقية
العناد الحقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) When both premises are particular
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Two affirmative real disjunctives can yield a conclusion when the opposite of one part and the repeated part are necessary concomitants of the other part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

1. Construct a real disjunctive syllogism with two affirmative premises that would be invalid under the general rule, and explain why. Use an Islamic example (e.g., 'Either this act is wajib or it is haram; either it is haram or it is mubah'). Then modify it by making the terms necessarily related (e.g., 'Either it is fajr or it is sunrise; either it is sunrise or it is daytime') and show that a conclusion follows.

Why Two Affirmative Real Disjunctives Yield No Conclusion
Author states rule and explains why two affirmative real disjunctives yield no conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why two affirmative real disjunctives fail to produce a valid conclusion and identify the conditions under which such syllogisms are invalid.

Core Idea
Two positive real disjunctives have no common middle term that yields a valid conclusion.

This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq explains a key rule in disjunctive syllogisms: two real disjunctives (منفصلتان حقيقيتان) with both parts affirmative produce no conclusion. The author provides the reasoning and a subtle example about the sun and night to show why such syllogisms are invalid. This lesson will guide you through the logical structure and the pitfalls.

Guided Path
1
Understand the requirement of a common term
Every syllogism requires a common term (حد مشترك) connecting the two premises. In disjunctive syllogisms, this common term may be complete or incomplete.
The common term is the part that appears in both disjunctives.
2
Recognize the rule for real disjunctives
For real disjunctives (mutually exclusive and exhaustive), logicians agree that two affirmatives produce no conclusion. Because a real disjunctive like 'Either A is B or C is D' is only true if there is no third alternative.
If the middle term is repeated, you get a tautology; if a new term appears, one premise becomes false.
3
Analyze the example of sun and night
The author gives: 'Either the sun is rising or night exists' and 'Either night exists or day exists'. The apparent conclusion 'if sun rising then day exists' leads to a contradiction (both night and day absent at once), violating the disjunctive condition.
This shows why the syllogism fails despite seeming plausible.
4
Identify the subtle condition about concomitants
Real incompatibility does not absolutely prevent a third if the incompatible part is a concomitant of the other. In the example, night is concomitant to both sunrise and day, leading to the absurd result that when one is absent, both are absent.
This nuance explains why the conclusion is not valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقول: قد عرف أنه ليس من الاقترانات قياس إلا وله حد مشترك جزأي المطلوب، فالاقتران الكائن من المنفصلتين لا بد له من حد مشترك، وهو إما أن يكون تاماً أو ناقصاً، فإن كان تاماً فإما أن تكون المنفصلات حقيقية أو غير حقيقية، فإن كانت حقيقية فقد اتفقوا على أنه لا إنتاج فيها من موجبتين، قال أفضل المتأخرين: لأنك إذا قلت لا يخلو إما أن يكون "أ" "ب" وإما أن يكون "ج" "د" فهذا القول إنما يكون صدقاً إذا لم يكن قسم ثالث؛ لأن معنى قولنا: (لا يخلو إما أن يكون "أ" "ب" وإما أن يكون "ج" "د") هو أنه إما أن يكون "أ" "ب" وأما إن لم يكن كان لا محالة "ج" "د") فـ (إن كان قد لا يكون "ج" "د") فالقضية كاذبة. اللهم إلا أن تُجعل جزئية، فإذا كان قولنا: (إما أن يكون "أ" "ب" وإما أن يكون "ج" "د") إنما صدقا إذا لم يكن قسم ثالث، فإن كرر الجزء الأوسط فقيل (إما أن يكون "ج" "د" وإما أن يكون "ج" "د" وإما أن يكون "هـ" "ر" ) فإن كان ("هـ" "ر") هو "أ" "ب" القضيتان واحدة، والنتيجة باطلة؛ لأنه ينتج إما أن يكون "أ" "ب" وإما أن يكون "أ" "ب") وإن كان قولنا : ( "هـ" "ر") غير قولنا: "أ" "ب") فهاهنا قسم ثالث، والقضيتان المنفصلتان كاذبتان وهو بعيد عن التحقيق فإن العناد الحقيقي وإن كان مانعاً من قسم ثالث فليس ذلك مطلقاً، بل بشرط أن لا يكون المعاند للجزء والمكرر من لوازم الجزء الآخر ، وأما إن كان ملازماً له فلا يمنع من العناد التام، ولا يوجب الكذب في القضيتين ولا الاتحاد بينهما، وذلك كما في قولنا: إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون الليل موجوداً) أو (دائماً إما أن يكون الليل موجوداً وإما أن يكون النهار موجوداً) فالنتيجة كل ما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود لأنه مهما سلم المنع من الجمع بين طلوع الشمس ووجود النهار والمنع من الخلو منهما، وسلم المنع من الجمع بين وجود الليل ووجود النهار والمنع من الخلو منهما، لزم أن يكون وجود النهار ملازماً لطلوع الشمس، وإلا فعلى تقدير وجود الليل أحدهما فلا يلزم وجود الآخر، وأيهما وجد فإنه يلزم منه امتناع وجود الليل إذ قد سلم المنع من الجمع بينهما، وذلك يفضي عند طلوع الشمس إلى اجتماع وجود الليل ووجود النهار في العدم، وقد سلم المنع من الخلو منهما، وكذا يفضي عند وجود النهار إلى اجتماع طلوع الشمس ووجود الليل في العدم، وقد سلم المنع من الخلو منهما وهو خلف.
We say: It has been known that of compounds there is no syllogism except that it has a common term of the two parts of the sought [conclusion]. So the compound formed from two disjunctives must have a common term, which is either complete or incomplete. If it is complete, then the disjunctives are either real or non-real. If they are real, they have agreed that there is no production from two affirmatives. The best of the later [scholars] said: Because when you say 'necessarily either A is B or C is D', this statement is only true if there is no third division; because the meaning of our saying 'necessarily either A is B or C is D' is that either it is A is B, and if it is not, then inevitably it is C is D. So if it is possible that it is not C is D, then the proposition is false. Unless it is made particular. So when our saying 'either A is B or C is D' is only true if there is no third division, then if the middle part is repeated, and it is said 'either C is D or C is D or H is R', then if H is R is A is B, the two propositions are one, and the conclusion is false because it yields 'either A is B or A is B'. And if our saying H is R is not our saying A is B, then here there is a third division, and the two disjunctive propositions are false. And this is far from verification, for real incompatibility, even if it prevents a third division, is not absolute, but with the condition that the incompatible of the part and the repeated [part] be not among the concomitants of the other part. But if it is concomitant to it, then it does not prevent complete incompatibility, nor does it necessitate falsehood in the two propositions nor union between them. That is like our saying: 'Either the sun is rising or the night exists' or 'Always either the night exists or the day exists'. So the conclusion is that whenever the sun is rising, the day exists, because whenever prevention of conjunction between sunrise and existence of day and prevention of absence of both are granted, and prevention of conjunction between existence of night and existence of day and prevention of absence of both are granted, then it necessarily follows that the existence of day is concomitant with sunrise; otherwise, on the assumption of the existence of night, one of them would not necessarily exist, and whichever exists, it necessitates prevention of existence of night because the prevention of conjunction between them is granted. And that leads at sunrise to the coming together of existence of night and existence of day in non-existence, and prevention of absence of both is granted. Similarly, it leads at the existence of day to the coming together of sunrise and existence of night in non-existence, and prevention of absence of both is granted, and this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of why two affirmative real disjunctives do not form a productive syllogism. The author begins with a general principle: every syllogism requires a common term (حد مشترك) between the two premises. For a conjunction (اقتران) formed from two disjunctives, this common term can be complete or incomplete. If complete, and the disjunctives are real (حقيقية) — meaning they truly exclude a third alternative — the logicians agree that no conclusion can be drawn from two affirmatives. The reason is that a real disjunctive like 'Either A is B or C is D' is only true if there is no third possibility. The author quotes 'the best of the later scholars' to elaborate: if you have two such disjunctives, the middle term might be repeated, leading to either a trivial tautology or a third category that makes one of the disjunctives false. He then gives a subtle condition: real incompatibility (عناد حقيقي) does not absolutely exclude a third unless the incompatible part is not a concomitant of the other. Using the example of the sun and night, he shows that if the premises are 'Either the sun is rising or night exists' and 'Either night exists or day exists', the conclusion that 'if the sun is rising, then day exists' appears valid but is actually a contradiction when fully analyzed, because it leads to mutual nullification. Thus, the passage concludes that such syllogisms are invalid.

Key Terms
حد مشترك
منفصلة حقيقية
إنتاج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if the middle part is repeated when combining two real disjunctives?
A) A valid conclusion is produced
B) The conclusion becomes a tautology (e.g., 'Either A or A')
C) One premise becomes false due to a third division
D) Both B and C are possible outcomes

Key Takeaway
Two affirmative real disjunctives never produce a valid conclusion because they either yield a tautology, introduce a false premise, or lead to a contradiction under analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

1. Consider these two real disjunctive premises: 'Either the action is obligatory (wajib) or it is recommended (mandub)' and 'Either the action is recommended (mandub) or it is permissible (mubah)'. Using the rule from the passage, explain why no valid conclusion can be drawn from these two affirmatives. (Hint: Think about the common term and the possibility of a third division.)
?

Real Disjunctives with Negative or Mixed Components
Author discusses real disjunctives when one or both are negative, ruling no production.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a real disjunctive syllogism yields no conclusion due to negative or mixed components.

Core Idea
Negation in real disjunctives destroys the incompatibility needed for a valid conclusion.

In previous lessons, we saw that a real disjunctive requires complete incompatibility between its two parts for a conclusion to follow. But what happens when one or both parts are negative? In this passage, the author examines this scenario and rules that no conclusion can be drawn. Understanding why is crucial for avoiding invalid arguments in Islamic theological and jurisprudential reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage considers two cases: both disjuncts negative, and one affirmative with one negative. Recognize that each case disallows a conclusion.
The negation can enter upon one part, both, or neither – still no conclusion.
2
Analyze the 'both negative' case
If both disjuncts are negative, the negation of complete incompatibility does not rule out a third alternative. For example, 'not A and not B' can both be false if A and B are not exclusive.
The author says 'لا إنتاج' (no production) because the disjunction is no longer exhaustive.
3
Analyze the mixed (affirmative/negative) case
With one affirmative and one negative, the terms may have a generality relationship. The example shows that 'D is A' could be general to both 'B is C' and 'H is R', destroying incompatibility.
This is illustrated with variable letters: possibility of generality or specificity prevents a necessary conclusion.
4
Apply the rule to Islamic examples
Test real disjunctives from fiqh or kalam: if any premise is negative, the syllogism fails. Practice distinguishing valid (both affirmative, complete incompatibility) from invalid cases.
Example: 'Either prayer is invalid without niyyah or it is valid without niyyah' is real and valid; but 'Either prayer is invalid without niyyah or it is not valid with it' is mixed and invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العناد بل إن قيل بعد الانسياق، فليس إلا لعدم اطراده، وعلى هذا يكون الحكم، وسواء كان حرف السلب غير داخل ولا على أحد الجزأين، أو هو داخل عليهما، أو على أحدهما دون الآخر. وأما إن كانتا سالبتين فلا إنتاج؛ لأن سلب العناد التام فيهما لا يمنع من غير الحقيقي، ولا إنتاج عنه كما يأتي. وأما إن كانت إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة كقولنا: (دائماً إما كل "ب" "ج" وإما "هـ" "ر") و(ليس البتة إما أن يكون "هـ" "ر" وإما "د" "أ") فلا إنتاج؛ لجواز أن يكون ("د" "أ") عاماً لـ ("ب" "ج") و("هـ" "ر") فلا يكون معانداً لهما؛ ولجواز أن يكون خاصاً بـ "هـ" "ر" فلا يكون معانداً له، ويكون معانداً لـ "ب" "ج").
Incompatibility. But if it is said after the sequence, it is only because of its non-consistency, and the ruling is upon this. It is the same whether the letter of negation is not entering upon either of the two parts, or it enters upon both, or upon one without the other. And if they are both negative, then there is no production, because the negation of complete incompatibility in them does not prevent from something other than the real, and there is no production from it as will come. And if one is affirmative and the other negative, as in our saying: 'Always either every B is C or H is R' and 'Not at all either that H is R or D is A', then there is no production, because it is possible that D is A is general to B is C and H is R, so it is not incompatible with them; and because it is possible that it is specific to H is R, so it is not incompatible with it, and it is incompatible with B is C.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage deals with real disjunctives when one or both are negative. The author first discusses the placement of the negation particle (حرف السلب) and states that the ruling applies regardless. If both disjunctives are negative, no conclusion follows because the negation of complete incompatibility does not prevent a third alternative. If one is affirmative and one negative, as in the example with variables, again no conclusion is possible because the terms may have a generality relationship that does not entail incompatibility.

Non-Real Disjunctives: مانعة الجمع دون الخلو
Explaining non-real disjunctives that prevent conjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a non-real disjunctive (مانعة الجمع دون الخلو) and explain why it fails to produce a valid syllogistic conclusion.

Core Idea
Non-real disjunctives lack the necessary exclusivity for valid syllogistic inference.

In this passage, the author examines a type of non-real disjunctive that prevents conjunction but not absence. He demonstrates through an example involving 'human', 'horse', and 'rational' that such disjunctives cannot yield a necessary conclusion, regardless of the quantity and quality of the premises. Understanding this helps us distinguish valid from invalid disjunctive arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunctive
Recognize that مانعة الجمع دون الخلو allows both parts to be false but not both true. This is a non-real disjunctive.
2
Examine the given example
The author uses the pair: 'Always either this is a human or a horse' and 'Always either this is a horse or rational'. Note that the middle term 'horse' is shared.
3
Determine why no conclusion follows
The author argues that the middle term may be incompatible with one disjunct and compatible with another, so no necessary relation holds between the remaining terms.
4
Test with a substitution
Replace 'rational' with 'donkey' to see that the conclusion is not consistent; it may be neither incompatible nor necessary.
5
Generalize the rule
For affirmative non-real disjunctives of this type, no syllogistic production is possible, and the same holds for other combinations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت غير حقيقية فإما مانعة الجمع دون الخلو، أو الخلو دون الجمع، أو مختلطة، فإن كانت مانعة الجمع دون الخلو فإن كانت موجبة فلا إنتاج منها؛ لأنه من الجائز أن يكون الجزء المشترك معانداً للجزأين الآخرين وأحدهما لازم للأخر غير معاند له فلا تكون النتيجة بالعناد بينهما، وذلك كما لو قلنا (دائماً إما أن يكون هذا إنساناً وإما أن يكون فرساً) و(ودائماً إما أن يكون فرساً وإما ناطقاً) ومن الجائز أن يكونا متعاندين، فلا تكون النتيجة بينهما باللزوم، كما لو بدلنا الناطق بالحمار، فالنتيجة غير مطردة لا بعناد ولا رفع العناد، وكذلك الحكم إن كانت إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة، أو هما سالبتان. وأما إن كانت مانعة الخلو دون الجمع فإن كانتا موجبتين فلا إنتاج لا بالخلو
And if they are non-real, then they are either preventive of conjunction without absence, or preventive of absence without conjunction, or mixed. If they are preventive of conjunction without absence, then if they are affirmative, there is no production from them; because it is possible that the common part is incompatible with the other two parts, and one of them is necessary for the other without being incompatible with it, so the conclusion is not by incompatibility between them. That is as if we said: 'Always either this is a human or this is a horse' and 'Always either this is a horse or a rational [animal]', and it is possible that they are incompatible, so the conclusion between them is not by necessity, as if we replaced 'rational' with 'donkey', the conclusion is not consistent, either by incompatibility or by removal of incompatibility. And the same ruling if one is affirmative and the other negative, or both are negative. And if they are preventive of absence without conjunction, then if they are both affirmative, there is no production, not by absence
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage explains the cases for non-real disjunctives, starting with those that prevent conjunction but not absence (مانعة الجمع دون الخلو). These are disjunctives where both parts can be false simultaneously but cannot both be true. The author states that even when both premises are affirmative, no conclusion follows. He gives an example with 'human', 'horse', and 'rational' to show that the middle term may be incompatible with one but not the other, and no necessary relation results. The same applies if one or both are negative. Then he begins to discuss the second type (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع) but the text cuts off. The key point is that non-real disjunctives do not guarantee a valid syllogism.

Key Terms
غير حقيقية
مانعة الجمع دون الخلو
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Non-real disjunctives that prevent conjunction without absence do not yield a valid syllogistic conclusion because the middle term may be incompatible with the other terms in an inconsistent way.

Rules of Disjunctive Syllogisms with a Shared Part (العناد)
Author details conditions for valid inference in compound disjunctive syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a conclusion follows from two disjunctive premises sharing a common part, based on the type of disjunctive and the quality of the premises.

Core Idea
Not every pair of disjunctive premises yields a conclusion; the type and quality matter.

In this lesson, we delve into the intricate rules governing compound disjunctive syllogisms where two disjunctive premises share a common part (al-juzʾ al-mushtarak). The author of Daqāʾiq al-Ḥaqāʾiq systematically classifies disjunctives into real (ʿinād) and non-real (māniʿat al-jamʿ, māniʿat al-khuluw) and then tests each combination of affirmative and negative premises for productive inference. Understanding these rules is crucial for constructing valid arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared part
Locate the term or proposition that appears in both disjunctive premises. This linking element is the key to potential inference.
In the text, shared parts are often labeled with variables like ب, ج, etc.
2
Classify the disjunctives
Determine whether each disjunctive is real (ḥaqīqiyya, i.e., preventing both conjunction and absence) or non-real (ghayr ḥaqīqiyya). Non-real disjunctives come in two main types: māniʿat al-jamʿ dūn al-khuluw and māniʿat al-khuluw dūn al-jamʿ.
Real disjunctives are also called ʿinād.
3
Check the quality of premises
Note whether each premise is affirmative (positive) or negative. For example, 'always either A or B' is affirmative; 'never either A or B' is negative.
The text examines all combinations: both affirmative, one affirmative one negative, both negative.
4
Apply the rule for real disjunctives
If both are real disjunctives and both are affirmative, the conclusion is valid (the shared part cancels the opposite). If one is negative, no conclusion follows because the shared part may not be truly opposite. If both negative, no conclusion.
The author uses examples with day/night to illustrate real disjunctives.
5
Apply the rule for non-real disjunctives
For māniʿat al-jamʿ dūn al-khuluw: no conclusion in any combination (both affirmative, one affirmative one negative, both negative) because the shared part may be opposite to only one part. For māniʿat al-khuluw dūn al-jamʿ: similarly no conclusion; even if both affirmative, absence of both parts may still be possible.
Examples: human/horse/rational/donkey for māniʿat al-jamʿ; colors/vision for māniʿat al-khuluw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
