لازم للأخر غير معاند له فلا تكون النتيجة بالعناد بينهما، وذلك كما لو قلنا (دائماً إما أن يكون هذا إنساناً وإما أن يكون فرساً) و(دائماً إما أن يكون فرساً وإما ناطقاً) ومن الجائز أن يكونا متعاندين، فلا تكون النتيجة بينهما باللزوم، كما لو بدلنا الناطق بالحمار، فالنتيجة غير مطردة لا بعناد ولا رفع العناد، وكذلك الحكم إن كانت إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة، أو هما سالبتان. وأما إن كانت مانعة الخلو دون الجمع فإن كانتا موجبتين فلا إنتاج لا بالخلو من الجزأين المختلفين ولا بمنع الخلو، أما الأول فلجواز أن لا يخلو الحال من أحدهما كما لو قلنا (دائماً إما أن يكون الجسم لا أبيض وإما لا أسود) و(دائماً إما أن يكون الجسم لا أسود وإما لا أصفر) فإنه لا يخلو الحال من لا أبيض ولا أصفر، كما لا يخلو من لا أبيض ولا أسود، ولا أسود ولا أصفر. وأما الثاني، فلجواز أن يخلو الحال منهما، بأن يكون أحد الطرفين المختلفين ملازماً للآخر، كما في قولنا: (الجسم إما لا أبيض وإما لا أسود) و(إما لا أسود وإما لا لونا مفرقاً للبصر) فإنه وإن امتنع الخلو من لا أبيض ولا أسود، وامتنع الخلو من لا أسود ولا مفرق للبصر، فقد يخلو من الأبيض والمفرق للبصر. وأما إن كانت إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة فلا إنتاج أيضاً لا بالمنع من الخلو ولا بسلبه، أما الأول فكما إذا قلنا (الجسم دائماً إما لا أسود وإما لا أبيض) و(دائماً ليس إما لا أسود وإما أصفر) فإنه كما لا يمتنع الخلو من لا أسود ومن الأصفر، فلا يمتنع الخلو من لا أبيض ومن لا أصفر. وأما الثاني فكما لو بدلنا الأصفر بلا جامع للبصر، فإنه يمتنع الخلو من لا أبيض ولا جامع البصر؛ إذ هو ملازم لسلب السواد. وأما إن كانتا سالبتين فلا إنتاج أيضاً، لا بالمنع من الخلو ولا بسلبه، أما الأول، فلجواز أن يكون الطرفان المختلفان مما يجوز الخلو منهما كما في قولنا: (دائماً ليس إما أن يكون الجسم أبيض أو أسود)، و(دائماً ليس إما أن يكون أسود وإما أصفر)، فإنه لا يمتنع الخلو من الأبيض ولا أصفر. وأما الثاني فكما في قولنا: (دائماً إما ليس إما أن يكون الجسم لا أسود وإما أصفر) و (دائماً ليس إما أن يكون أصفر وإما لا أبيض) فإنه وإن لم يمتنع الخلو من لا أسود وأصفر، ومن الأصفر ولا أبيض، فيمتنع الخلو من الطرفين المختلفين وهما لا أسود ولا أبيض كما سبق، وعلى هذا فلا تخفى أحكام المختلطات أيضاً. وأما إن كان الجزء المشترك غير تام فإما أن يكون محمولاً في أجزاء الانفصال في أحدهما وموضوعاً لأجزاء الانفصال في الأخرى، أو أنه محمول في أجزاء
Continuation of the first art: on defining the forms of conjunctive syllogisms. From them, it follows that the existence of daytime is concomitant with sunrise, otherwise given the existence of night, one of them does not necessitate the existence of the other; whichever exists necessitates the impossibility of night since the prevention of their conjunction has been conceded. That leads at sunrise to the conjunction of the existence of night and the existence of daytime in non-existence, and the prevention of their both being absent has been conceded. Similarly it leads at the existence of daytime to the conjunction of sunrise and the existence of night in non-existence, and the prevention of their both being absent has been conceded, which is a contradiction. Al-ʿInād: But if it is said after conformity, it is only due to its lack of regularity, and upon this the judgment rests, whether the particle of negation is not entering at all, nor upon one of the two parts, or it enters upon both, or upon one without the other. But if both are negative, there is no production; because the negation of complete ʿinād in them does not prevent from the non-real, and there is no production from it as will come. But if one is affirmative and the other negative, as our saying: (always either every B is J or H) and (not at all either that H is R or D A) then there is no production; because it is possible that D A is universal to B J and H R, so it would not be opposite (muʿānid) to them; and because it is possible that it is particular to H, so it would not be opposite to it, and would be opposite to B J. But if they are non-real, then either they are preventive of conjunction without prevention of absence, or preventive of absence without prevention of conjunction, or mixed. If they are preventive of conjunction without prevention of absence, then if they are affirmative, there is no production from them; because it is possible that the shared part is opposite to the other two parts, and one of them is necessary for the other not opposite to it, so the conclusion is not due to opposition between them. That is as if we said (always either this is a human or a horse) and (always either it is a horse or rational), and it is possible that they are opposite, so the conclusion does not follow by necessity, as if we replaced rational with donkey, the conclusion is not regular either by opposition or by removal of opposition. The same rule applies if one is affirmative and the other negative, or both are negative. But if they are preventive of absence without prevention of conjunction, then if both are affirmative, there is no production either by allowing absence from the two different parts or by preventing absence. As for the first, because it is possible that the state is not devoid of one of them, as if we said (always either the body is not white or not black) and (always either the body is not black or not yellow), then the state is not devoid of not-white and not-yellow, just as it is not devoid of not-white and not-black, nor of not-black and not-yellow. As for the second, because it is possible that the state is devoid of both, by one of the two different parts being necessary for the other, as in our saying: (the body is either not white or not black) and (either not black or not a color that scatters vision) — although prevention of absence from not-white and not-black, and prevention of absence from not-black and not-a-scatterer-of-vision, hold, it may be devoid of white and the scatterer of vision. But if one is affirmative and the other negative, there is no production either by preventing absence or by negating it. As for the first, as when we say (the body is always either not black or not white) and (always it is not the case that either not black or yellow), then just as absence from not-black and yellow is not impossible, so absence from not-white and not-yellow is not impossible. As for the second, as if we replace yellow with not-gathering-of-vision, then absence from not-white and not-gatherer-of-vision is impossible because it entails negation of blackness. But if both are negative, there is no production either by preventing absence or by negating it. As for the first, because it is possible that the two different parts are such that absence from them is possible, as in our saying: (always it is not the case that either the body is white or black) and (always it is not the case that either it is black or yellow), then absence from white and yellow is not impossible. As for the second, as in our saying: (always it is not the case that either the body is not-black or yellow) and (always it is not the case that either it is yellow or not-white) — even though absence from not-black and yellow, and from yellow and not-white, is not impossible, absence from the two different parts, namely not-black and not-white, is impossible as before. Upon this, the rules of mixed ones are not hidden. But if the shared part is not complete, then either it is a predicate in the parts of disjunction in one and a subject for the parts of disjunction in the other, or it is a predicate in the parts...
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a detailed expansion on the rules of disjunctive syllogisms that involve a shared part (العناد). The author begins by stating that the discussion is a continuation of the definitions of conjunctive syllogisms. He then presents a scenario involving day and night to illustrate logical necessity. The core concept is 'al-ʿinād' — a relationship where two propositions are such that they cannot both be true (manʿ al-jamʿ) and cannot both be false (manʿ al-khuluw). The passage examines various cases based on the quality (affirmative/negative) of the premises and the types of disjunctive (ḥaqīqiyya vs. ghayr ḥaqīqiyya, which includes māniʿat al-jamʿ, māniʿat al-khuluw, and mixed). For each case, the author explains whether a conclusion (intāj) is produced and why. For example, when both premises are negative, no conclusion follows. When one is affirmative and the other negative, still no conclusion due to possible lack of 'opposition' (ʿinād). The author then delves into non-real disjunctives: for māniʿat al-jamʿ, even if both affirmative, no conclusion is produced because the shared part might be opposite to the other two parts and one of them might be necessary for the other without opposition. He gives examples involving human, horse, rational, donkey. For māniʿat al-khuluw, he shows that even if both are affirmative, no conclusion is possible either way. He provides examples with colors and vision. The passage systematically covers all combinations: both affirmative, one affirmative one negative, both negative, for both types of non-real disjunctives. Finally, he mentions the case where the shared part is not complete (al-juzʾ al-mushtarak ghayr tāmm), i.e., not a full term, and explains that it can be a predicate in one premise's disjuncts and a subject in the other's, or predicate in both, etc. The discussion is cut off at this point. The author's method is to list possibilities and demonstrate with counterexamples why a conclusion fails. This is a sophisticated treatment of compound disjunctive syllogisms typical in later Islamic logic.

مانعة الجمع دون الخلو
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع
الجزء المشترك

Checkpoint
In the discussion of māniʿat al-jamʿ dūn al-khuluw, if both premises are affirmative, what is the reason that no conclusion follows?
A) Because the shared part might be opposite to the other two parts and one of them might be necessary for the other without opposition.
B) Because the premises are not properly formed.
C) Because the disjunctives are not real (ḥaqīqiyya).
D) Because one premise is negative.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a compound disjunctive syllogism depends on the type of disjunctive, the quality of premises, and the completeness of the shared part; not every pair yields a conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a pair of disjunctive premises using an Islamic legal example (e.g., regarding ritual purity) that should yield a valid conclusion according to the real disjunctive rule, and explain why the conclusion follows.
?
Given the pair 'Always either prayer is valid with ablution or it is invalid' and 'Always either prayer is invalid or it is rejected', apply the rules for māniʿat al-jamʿ to determine whether a conclusion that prayer is rejected follows, and explain your reasoning.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Mixed and Negative Premises in Compound Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author explains failure of conclusion in compound disjunctive syllogisms with mixed or negative premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a compound disjunctive syllogism with mixed or negative premises yields a necessary conclusion, by applying the test of prevention of emptiness.

Core Idea
Mixed or both-negative premises in compound disjunctive syllogisms never produce a conclusion.

In this passage, the author examines special cases of compound disjunctive syllogisms where the premises are not both affirmative. He proves that when one premise is affirmative and the other negative, or both are negative, no conclusion can be drawn, regardless of the condition of prevention of emptiness (man' al-khulu). This rule is essential for evaluating complex arguments in Islamic logic, particularly in juristic reasoning where multiple disjunctive propositions may appear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases presented
The author first discusses mixed premises (one affirmative, one negative), then both negative. Notice he splits each into two subcases: evaluation by prevention of emptiness and by its negation.
2
Analyze the mixed premises case
Take the example: 'The ruling is always either not-haram or not-wajib' (affirmative disjunction) and 'It is always not that it is either not-haram or mubah' (negative of a disjunction). Check if emptiness of the disjuncts (e.g., not-haram and mubah) is prevented. The author shows it is not, no conclusion follows. Then replace 'mubah' with 'not-necessary' to see emptiness prevented, but still no conclusion because the terms do not produce a necessary relation.
The key insight: mixed quality (affirmative/negative) disrupts the linkage needed for a conclusion.
3
Analyze the both-negative premises case
Example: 'It is always not that the act is either halal or haram' and 'It is always not that it is either haram or makruh'. Emptiness from halal and not-makruh is not prevented. In a variant, emptiness from the two different terms (not-haram and not-wajib) may be prevented, yet still no conclusion because the premises themselves are negative and do not establish a necessary link.
4
Generalize the rule
For any compound disjunctive syllogism, if the premises are not both affirmative, no conclusion is produced. This holds whether we consider prevention of emptiness or its negation. Apply this to check validity of syllogisms in fiqh and kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة فلا إنتاج أيضاً لا بالمنع من الخلو ولا بسلبه، أما الأول فكما إذا قلنا الجسم) دائماً إما لا أسود وإما لا أبيض) و (دائماً ليس إما لا أسود وإما أصفر فإنه كما لا يمتنع الخلو من لا أسود ومن الأصفر، فلا يمتنع الخلو من لا أبيض ومن لا أصفر. وأما الثاني فكما لو بدلنا الأصفر بلا جامع للبصر ، فإنه يمتنع الخلو من لا أبيض ولا جامع البصر؛ إذ هو ملازم لسلب السواد.
وأما إن كانتا سالبتين فلا إنتاج أيضاً، لا بالمنع من الخلو ولا بسلبه، أما الأول، فلجواز أن يكون الطرفان المختلفان مما يجوز الخلو منهما كما في قولنا: دائماً ليس إما أن يكون الجسم أبيض أو أسود)، و(دائماً ليس إما أن يكون أسود وإما أصفر)، فإنه لا يمتنع الخلو من الأبيض ولا أصفر. وأما الثاني فكما في قولنا: دائماً إما ليس إما أن يكون الجسم لا أسود وإما أصفر) و (دائماً ليس إما أن يكون أصفر وإما لا (أبيض فإنه وإن لم يمتنع الخلو من لا أسود وأصفر، ومن الأصفر ولا أبيض، فيمتنع الخلو من الطرفين المختلفين وهما لا أسود ولا أبيض كما سبق، وعلى هذا فلا تخفى أحكام المختلطات أيضاً.
And if one of the two [premises] is affirmative and the other negative, then there is also no production, neither by prevention of emptiness nor by its negation. As for the first, it is like when we say: 'The body is always either not-black or not-white' and 'It is always not that it is either not-black or yellow'; for just as emptiness from not-black and yellow is not prevented, so emptiness from not-white and not-yellow is not prevented. As for the second, it is like when we replace yellow with 'not-collecting-the-sight', for then emptiness from not-white and not-collecting-the-sight is prevented, because it is necessary for the negation of blackness. And if both are negative, then also no production, neither by prevention of emptiness nor by its negation. As for the first, it is because it is possible that the two different terms might be such that emptiness from them is possible, as in our saying: 'It is always not that the body is either white or black' and 'It is always not that it is either black or yellow'; for then emptiness from white and not-yellow is not prevented. As for the second, it is like in our saying: 'It is always that it is not that the body is either not-black or yellow' and 'It is always not that it is either yellow or not-white'; for although emptiness from not-black and yellow, and from yellow and not-white, is not prevented, emptiness from the two different terms, namely not-black and not-white, is prevented as before. And on this basis, the rules of the mixed [syllogisms] are not hidden.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a rule-based exposition on compound disjunctive syllogisms, specifically addressing the cases where the two premises do not share the same quality (affirmative or negative). The author first considers the scenario where one premise is affirmative and the other is negative. In such a situation, he states that no necessary conclusion (production) results, whether we consider the condition of preventing emptiness (man' al-khulu) or its negation. He provides two sub-examples: one illustrating the failure of prevention of emptiness (the body is either not-black or not-white, and it is never not-black or yellow), and another illustrating the prevention of emptiness when a term is replaced (with 'not-collecting-the-sight'), showing that even if emptiness is prevented, the syllogism remains invalid. Next, the author addresses the case where both premises are negative. Again, no production occurs, with two sub-cases: one where emptiness from the different terms is possible (e.g., 'the body is never white or black' and 'it is never black or yellow'), and another where emptiness from the two different terms (not-black and not-white) is prevented despite the absence of production in the original terms. The author concludes that the rules for mixed syllogisms (al-mukhtalitat) become clear from these illustrations. Throughout, the author uses examples from physical properties (color and sight) to demonstrate the logical principles.

Key Terms
المنع من الخلو
الإنتاج
المختلطات

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens in a compound disjunctive syllogism when both premises are negative?
A) A conclusion is always produced.
B) No conclusion is produced because emptiness cannot be prevented.
C) No conclusion is produced, regardless of prevention of emptiness.
D) A conclusion is produced if one premise is universal.

Key Takeaway
In compound disjunctive syllogisms, mixed or both-negative premises never yield a necessary conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an Islamic example of a compound disjunctive syllogism with one affirmative and one negative premise (using terms like halal, haram, wajib, etc.) and show why no conclusion follows.
?
Given two negative premises: 'It is always not that the prayer is either nullified by coughing or by laughing' and 'It is always not that it is either nullified by laughing or by talking.' Explain why no ruling about nullification can be derived.
